THE
PARLIAMENTARY DEBATES
(OFFICIAL REPORT)
IN THE
FOURTH SESSION OF THE FIFTH PARLIAMENT OF NORTHERN IRELAND
APPOINTED TO MEET AT STORMONT, ON THE TWENTY-FIFTH DAY OF FEBRUARY, IN THE
FIFTH YEAR OF THE REIGN OF
HIS MAJESTY KING
KING GEORGE VI.
VOLUME XXIV.
THE SENATE.

---

Thursday, 16th January, 1941.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Agriculture:
The Pig Industry Council (Term and
Conditions of Office of Members)
(Amendment) Rules (Northern Ireland),
1940.
The Pig Industry Council (Casual
Vacancies) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1940.
The Marketing of Pigs (Form of
Curers' Returns) Regulations (Northern
Ireland), 1936 (Revocation) Order, 1941.
The Bacon Industry (Pig Nutrition
Research Grant) Order (Northern Ireland), 1941.
The Swine Fever (Emergency Sale)
Order, 1941.
Ministry of Commerce:
Report by the Ministry of Commerce
of proceedings under the General Pier
and Harbour Act, 1861, and the General
Pier and Harbour Act, 1861, Amendment Act, in respect of the year ended
31st December, 1940. (By Act.)
The Belfast Harbour Commissioners
(Postponement of Elections) Order (Northern Ireland), 1940.
The Londonderry Port and Harbour
Commissioners (Postponement of Eleetions) Order (Northern Ireland), 1940.
Ministry of Finance :
Statement of Guarantees which the
Ministry of Finance have given up to
31st December, 1940.
Accounts of Capital Receipts and Payments and of earnings on Capital
Advances or Investments, together with
various subsidiary Accounts, for the
year ended 31st March, 1940.
Account of Receipts and Payments of
the Teachers' Superannuation Fund for
the year ended 31st March, 1940, together with the Report of the Comptroller and Auditor-General thereon.
Ministry of Home Affairs:
Report by the Ministry of Home
Affairs of applications and proceedings
under the Railway Companies' Powers
Act, 1864, for the year ended 31st
December, 1940.
Report by the Ministry of Home
Affairs of applications and proceedings
under the Railways Construction Facilities Act, 1864, for the year ended 31st
December, 1940.
Report by the Ministry of Home
Affairs of proceedings under the Railway Regulation Act, 1893, for the year
ended 31st December, 1940.
Annual Report and Statement of
Accounts and Statistics of the Northern
Ireland Road Transport Board for the
year ended 30th September, 1940.
Ministry of Labour:
Factories (Weekly hours of Young
Persons under sixteen in the Printing,
Bookbinding and Allied Industries)
Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1940.
Unemployment Insurance (Emergency
Powers) (Amendment) Regulations (No.
6) (Northern Ireland), 1940.
Unemployment Insurance (Approval
of Arrangements) Regulations (Northern
Ireland), 1940.
National Health Insurance (Navy,
Army and Air Force Auxiliary Services)
Amendment Regulations, 1940.
Ministry of Public Security:
Air Raid Precautions (Schemes) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1940.
Air Raid Precautions (Storage and
Loan of Equipment) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1940.
Civil Defence (Compulsory Area)
(No. 2) Order (Northern Ireland), 1940.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Member Sworn.
George Whaley, Esq. Sworn.
Business of the House.
Moved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's
sitting for the purpose of taking all
the stages of the Salaries of Ministerial
Offices Bill, when it is brought from the
House of Commons."-(Mr. Long).
Ministerial Offices Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
Mr. LESLIE: I presume that perhaps
on the Second Reading we shall be given
some indication of the necessity for
urgency in this matter?
Mr. LONG: Yes, the Minister of
Labour will give that information.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Salaries
of Ministerial Offices Bill to which they
desire the concurrence of this House.
Salaries of Ministerial Offices Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long).
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That
this Bill be now read a Second time."
I do not intend to go into the details
of the Measure because we have the
privilege of having a Cabinet Minister
with us. We are exceedingly pleased to
welcome the Minister of Labour, the
Right Hon. John F. Gordon, who will
explain the object of this Bill and reply
to any points raised during the debate.
The MINISTER OF LABOUR (Mr.
Gordon): The duty of bringing this Bill
before the Senate would, in the ordinary
course, have fallen to my old and valued
Friend, the. Minister of Education. But
he, unfortunately, has been laid aside by
illness, and I feel that I must ask for
the indulgence of yourself and this
honourable House in attempting to take
his place, as best I can, at very short
notice and on an unfamiliar subject.
The object of the Bill is, as hon.
Senators will observe, to ask Parliament
to make financial provision for the salary
at the rate of £1,000 a year each of the
Parliamentary Secretary of the Ministry
of Agriculture and the Parliamentary
Secretary of the Ministry of Commerce.
The amount of the salary is, of course,
what is appropriate to the office and
enjoyed by the existing Parliamentary
Secretaries. The offices themselves,
however, are. new, but I am sure the
necessity for them will be appreciated by
this House.
We all know the widespread activities
of the Ministry of Agriculture which so
vitally affect the lives of everyone in our
community, and how the stern necessity
of war has not only multiplied those
activities, but made them of even greater
importance to us all than they were. in
times of peace.
The Ministry of Commerce has also
been compelled to expand itself in many
directions with great advantage to our
industries. It has been instrumental in
securing important orders and Government contracts for war work; it
represents the Ministry of Supply in connection with certain matters affecting our
security, to which it would not be proper
for me to refer more particularly; and
it has had to undertake new and exacting
functions for the Ministry of Food.
The Governmemnt, consider, therefore,
that they should be strengthened by the
addition of a Parliamentary Secretary
in each of these Ministries, and they are
satisfied that it is in the public interest
that these appointments should be made.
very
It is necessary and proper that Parliamentary approval should be obtained.
for the creation of these new Secretaryships; but there may be some
natural anxiety about the consequent
apparent increase. in the financial burden
borne by our people. I should like to
allay that anxiety by adding that the
necessity for economy has not been overlooked. The Prime. Minister has
informed me that he hopes to make some
adjustments and arrangements in other
directions which will obviate any addition
to the total cost of Ministerial offices as
a result of this Measure.
We
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: The right
hon. Gentleman has not given us any
reasons for urgency in this matter.
have had in this House during the
past few months a number of Bills in
respect of which Standing Order No.
XXV has been suspended. There was one
Bill in particular I remember, of 115
clauses, which came from the Ministry of
Labour and we had only a couple of days
to consider that whole Bill and pass it
through this House.
I certainly do most strongly object to
suspending the Standing Order to rush
matters in this House when, in this case,
the Bill has had the attention of another
House for a much longer period.
Although the Bill is a short one, yet there
Ministerial Offices Bill-
Second Reading
16 JANUARY 1941
may be controversy in this House as to
the necessity for these two new appointments. What I protest against is the
suspension of the Standing Order to take
the Bill through all its stages at one
sitting, and I hope we shall not have a
recurrence of the conditions that have
prevailed in this House for the past
few months regarding the rushing of
legislation.
Mr. LESLIE: First of all, I should
like to congratulate our old friend, the
Minister of Labour, on making his first
appearance in this House.
If I may say
so, I have rarely been so fortunate as to
hear better any Ministerial utterance.
Not only were the observations which the
right hon. Gentleman saw fit to address
to us well founded in themselves, but they
were distinctly audible, and for that those
of us who sit on this side of the House are
very grateful. We rather hope we may
have the opportunity of hearing him
again, whether on matters peculiarly concerned with the Department which he
administers or general matters, we do not
care. But we do want to hear him again,
and we have had that advantage this
afternoon.
The right hon. Gentleman opposite (Sir
Joseph Davison) has complained, and I
think justly complained, that we had no
indication as to the urgency of the matters
before us. I did give a hint on the
Motion for the suspension of the Standing
Order that we expected to receive some
intimation as to why the proposal was
urgent, and I rather gathered from the
Deputy Leader of the House that we were
going to get that intimation. We have
not had it, and we have not been given
any indication as to why this matter is
urgent. Perhaps the Minister of Labour
or
the Parliamentary Secretary will
amplify that matter and go more into
detail as to the reasons which have moved
the Government to ask this House to deal
with the whole thing in one day.
The Bill itself is a very short one, but
it seems to me to be one which would
repay examination, and not only a Bill
which would repay examination, but a
Bill which those we represent outside
would expect us to examine. When I
say examine I do not suggest examination
in the spirit of controversy or even,
perhaps, of criticism, but the Bill ought
to be examined, and we ought to see what
it purports and what it really means, and
what its implications are.
First of all, if we pass this Bill to-day
we shall be consenting to the creation of
two new Government offices. The effect of
the creation of those two offices will be
that the number of Government representatives in another place will rise to 16.
At the moment there is one Member of
the Cabinet whose position one may
assume is temporary-the Minister of
Public Security. It was admittedly a
war-time appointment, and when it was
mentioned in this House I suggested that
it might have been advisable to give him
a Parliamentary Secretary also. But my
idea was that, perhaps, he should have a
Parliamentary Secretary sitting in this
House in order that the new Ministry
might during the war be represented in
this Chamber. We must disregard the
Minister of Public Security in considering
the matter before us, but as the Minister
of Public Security is there the total of
Government representatives in the House
of Commons will amount to 16. That is,
I must say, a very high proportion in a
House of 52 Members.
Let us for a moment examine the constituent parts of that House of 52.
There are two Members, of course, the
Speaker and the Chairman of Ways and
Means who for debating purposes, and
for debating purposes only, do not
count. I think I am right in saying
that seven Members who have a right to
sit in another place are at present
honourably engaged on military service.
That reduces the number rather more.
There are five or six Members who do not
attend the House. That, of course, is
their business. When added together
the debating strength outside the Government is left very small. It is a matter
which I cannot but believe that constituents outside will take note of-that
the Government representatives in a
House of 52 are now to be raised to
sixteen.
There is no suggestion that these
additional Parliamentary Secretaries are
purely for the duration of the war. That
is a matter, perhaps, for consideration
in another place, and not particularly
But there is another matter,
and that is one essentially for this House.
If the number of Government representatives in the other place is such a
for us.
Ministerial Offices Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
of
an
for
high proportion of the number
Members it throws on this House
addition to our responsibilities
examining Measures submitted to us.
It is hard enough as it is to get Measures
sufficiently examined and discussed in
detail, but the burden will certainly be
increased when the number of effective
debaters in another place is reduced.
That is a matter I commend to my fellow
Members.
As regards the question of the cost of
this increase, the right hon. Gentleman
told us that it is the proposal of the
Prime Minister to make some adjustments. The right hon. Gentleman cannot very well do that without the consent
of Parliament, and I should rather like
to take this opportunity of, to use a
common expression, rubbing that in. It
is all very well for the Prime Minister
to say he proposes to do this and that,
but he has no power for himself; he
must carry Parliament with him.
The salaries attached to Ministerial
offices and I do not suggest for a
moment that they are too high-depend
on the will of Parliament, not of the
Prime Minister, and I do not myself
believe that it was intended to suggest
anything to the contrary. May I remind
the House that those emoluments
attached to Ministerial offices were not
originally fixed at the instance of the
Ministry itself. They were fixed by Parliament on the recommendation of a
Committee appointed by Parliament.
That is a matter that I think ought not
ever to be forgotten and which it is perhaps not immaterial to call attention to
to-day.
They were affected recently by
a Measure which passed through
Parliament appointing the Minister
of Public Security. That Measure
provided for a reduction in the
emoluments of the other Cabinet Ministers in order that provision might be
made for the new Cabinet Minister without casting any further expense on the
public. It was my privilege to suggest,
when that Measure was before this
House, that thanks were due to the
holders of Cabinet office for their selfsacrifice in that respect. So much for
the cost.
As regards the emoluments proposed
for these two new Parliamentary Secretaries, they are fixed at £1,000 a year
each. That means two things. First of
all, the provision for Parliamentary Secretaries is to be stereotyped at £1,000 a
year.
From that it would seem to follow
that the Parliamentary Secretaries do not
propose to follow the example of Cabinet
Ministers in consenting to a reduction in
their emoluments in order to provide for
the two new entrants into their class.
The Prime Minister, if he is going to
suggest any general readjustment will, I
am afraid, find himself rather tied by
this Bill in that particular matter. Parliamentary Secretaries in another place
have to get their £1,000 a year.
Some
The other point I want to draw
attention to is one I mentioned some
years ago. That is that it is proposed
to stereotype only the emoluments of
Parliamentary Secretaries who sit in
another place at £1,000 a year, and it
is proposed to maintain the disparity
between them and the one Parliamentary
Secretary allotted to this House.
years ago I pointed out that although
we have only one Parliamentary Secretary in this House, and he is a gentleman who has no Departmental
responsibility, he has to act as Deputy
Leader of this House, and as
Deputy Leader he has presumably
to acquaint himself with every detail
of every Bill presented to this
House, from whatever Department it
may emanate. This is a distinction it
has always seemed to me a little hard to
justify, and it is apparently proposed to
continue it. That is a matter entirely
without regard to the personalities either
of the gentlemen who act as Parliamentary Secretaries elsewhere or the two or
three, Parliamentary Secretaries we have
had in this House.
I do not myself propose to oppose this
Bill for a moment, but I have thought it
right to draw the attention of the House
to the various implications which I have
mentioned, and I hope we shall receive
further information on that subject
before we finally part with the Bill.
we
Mr. MCALLISTER: I would like to
join other hon. Members in welcoming
the Minister of Labour to this House..
As Members of the Opposition
naturally have a certain suspicion of
every move of the Government of Northern Ireland, and I am inclined to
465 Ministerial Offices Bill-

16 JANUARY 1941
Second Reading
suspect that in order to get this rather
delicate-I will not say tricky-
Measure through they are playing on our
feelings through the medium of the.
never-failing courtesy of the Minister of
Labour. Having said that in all
sincerity, I wish again to protest, as I
have always protested, against the
practice of taking all stages of a Bill at
one day's sitting of this House.
a
I have said before, and I say again,
that we are failing to uphold the dignity
of this House if we consent to this procedure being continued. As the hon. and
learned Member (Mr. Leslie) has so well
pointed out, it is more than ever necessary that this House should take
critical view of legislation brought before
it. I am prepared to oppose to-day, as
I always will, taking more than the first
and second reading of a Bill at one
sitting. I do not want to let myself go
on this question, but I do say the Government are not treating this House with
the courtesy we are entitled to.
I want to support the arguments put
forward by Senator Leslie. I would like
to know from the Minister of Labour if
he can mention any Parliament in the
world which has thirty per cent. of the
Members of the Government party
directly associated with the administration of the Government. Surely it is a
very dangerous precedent to set up-that
thirty per cent. of the Members of the
House of Commons should be directly
associated with the administration and
tied to the Government. From that point
of view it would require very great
justification to add to the number of
Members directly associated with the
administration.
On the question of these new appointments I rather differ from Senator Sir
Joseph Davison. I do not think there is
any urgency in the matter of these
appointments, but what I do suggest is
that on account of the colossal unemployment in Northern Ireland at the present
time, and the fact that no practical steps
have been taken to relieve that unemployment-
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I did not
say anything about the urgency of the
appointments.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I recognise that.
I am now dealing with another point. I
think every Member of this House and
every responsible person in the Province.
must view with alarm the future of the
commerical and industrial position of
Northern Ireland. If I thought for
one moment that these two additional
appointments at salaries of £1,000
a year could justify themselves there
is no
one in this House who would
support them more than I would. But
there have been no facts put before us
to show that these. appointments are
going to improve the industrial and commercial position of this country.
I was rather surprised by the speech of
the Minister of Labour. He referred
to the great work already done by the
Ministry of Commerce in getting contracts
for Northern Ireland. I think that was
a very unhappy allusion in view of the
present state of affairs in Northern
Ireland. Is it not one of the grievances
in all parts of the country that contracts
have not been secured, and that the linen
industry has been suppressed and
destroyed, and is it not one of the
reasons why the whole country is alarmed
at the present moment?
I am prepared to move the rejection of
this Bill. It is a finance Bill and we
cannot amend it, but as a protest against
the action of the Government in deliberately pushing this Bill down our throats
at one sitting I move that the word
"now" be deleted from the Motion, and
that at the end there be added the words
"this day six months.'
Mr. GORDON: As I listened to my old
friend, Senator McAllister, who was
formerly a Member of the other House, I
felt that I was coming into mortal combat
with an old opponent. I think I can explain the reason why this Bill is necessary.
The objection offered by Senator Sir
Joseph Davison was that we were unnecessarily rushing the Bill through the
Senate, and that his experience was that
other Bills which had been sent forward
from the House of Commons were unnecessarily rushed through the Senate.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: That is my
contention.
Mr. GORDON: I want to explain to
the right hon. Gentleman and other
Members the reason for presenting this
Bill and requesting the Senate to pass it
through all its stages as rapidly as pos-
Ministerial Offices Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Gordon]
sible. I also want to deal with the points
put forward by Senator McAllister about
colossal unemployment and my unhappy
allusion to contracts being secured by the
Ministry of Commerce. The Prime
Minister is a new Prime Minister, and
hon. Members will know that during the
past few months there has been considerable argument in another place that
certain things were not being done that
should be done; that we were not getting
sufficient orders; and that we ought to set
about doing the work that lay close to our
hand and do it with greater zeal than
we have hitherto done.
Our new Prime Minister felt that the
criticism that was levelled against the
Ministry of Commerce and the Ministry
of Agriculture was ill-founded, and for
this reason.
Each of the Ministers of
these two Departments was doing more
than one man's work. During the past
six months I have had some little experience of the work being done by the
Ministry of Commerce and the Ministry
of Agriculture. The Minister of Agriculture has gone from town to town
endeavouring to focus attention upon the
necessity for increasing the food supplies.
of our country.
Now I come to the remark of Senator
McAllister when he said it was an
unhappy allusion I made to contracts. He
said the linen industry
industry had been
suppressed and destroyed. The linen
industry has not been suppressed
and destroyed by any action of the
Ministry of Commerce. I am speaking to some in this House who have experience of the linen industry and who
know what it is to endeavour to maintain
output when it is impossible to get
material to carry out their orders.
Mr. MCALLISTER: What about
home-grown flax?
Mr. GORDON: I am coming to that.
If the linen industry has been hurt it is
due to the closing of the ports of Europe.
If we want cotton or other raw materials
we can get them from America and
Africa, but for flax to carry on our linen
industry we must go to the ports of
Europe or get it here, and the ports of
Europe are closed to us. Therefore, I
think, the hon. Member is labouring
under a grave misapprehension if he
thinks that anything has been done by
the Ministry of Commerce injurious in
any way to the linen trade. Because of
the shortage of flax there would have
been greater unemployment had it not
been that the Ministry of Commerce were
able to get immense orders for cotton
goods, with the consequence that numbers
of our weaving factories were kept going
when they would otherwise have had to
close down.
Mr. LESLIE: On cotton ?
I
Mr. GORDON: Yes, on cotton.
could tell of large orders being given to
other firms in the stitching and other
trades. Probably four-fifths of the industries of the country are working today as the result of Government orders.
It is not within my province, and it
would not be right for me to state the
nature of the work that is being carried
on, but I can say that if it were not for
the work that has been given to us by
various Departments of the Imperial
Government there would have been a
great deal more unemployment than
there is at the present time.
Mr. MCALLISTER: How many mills
are closed?
Mr. GORDON: I have already explained that that is because we have not
got the raw material owing to the ports
being closed against us. But the Minister
of Agriculture is making a supreme
effort to increase the cultivation of flax
so that our linen workers may be kept
in employment. Senator Leslie asked
why the passage of this Measure was so
urgent. It is not possible for the Prime
Minister to make this new arrangement
until this Bill has been placed on the
Statute Book. Objection has been made
to the fact that the Bill was sent here
to be passed through all stages in one
day. The Bill was before the House of
Commons only yesterday, when it was
passed through all stages, and it is before.
the Senate to-day.
Mr. LESLIE: But why such hurry?
Mr. GORDON: The House of Commons has only been called together for a
period of three days, and will adjourn
to-day until 28th January.
Mr. LESLIE: Why?

16 JANUARY 1941
The only real objection put forward is
that the Measure has been unnecessarily
rushed through the other House and this
House. I want to say on behalf
of the Prime Minister that he did
not mean to rush the Bill through the
House. He was anxious that you should
have an opportunity of discussing it in
all its stages to-day. It is a small Bill,
and the only point at issue is these new
appointments of Parliamentary Secretaries the Government have decided on.
Mr. LESLIE: For Departmental or
Parliamentary work?
Mr. GORDON: Parliamentary work.
Mr. LESLIE: But not for Departmental work?
Mr. GORDON: I do not understand
the hon. and learned Member.
Mr. LESLIE: Are these two new
Parliamentary Secretaries required to do
Departmental work or only Parliamentary work? We all know that sometimes a Parliamentary Secretary does
help in the Department. At other times
he is rather the fifth wheel of the coach.
The point made about urgency is that the
House of Commons are only sitting one
more day. If that is so the question of
Parliamentary work would not seem
very vital. But if these gentlemen are
to assist in the work of their Departments outside Parliament it might be
that the position would be different.
That is what we have not been told.
Mr. GORDON: That is like a point
that would be raised in another place to
endeavour to place me in a difficult
position. There is a great difference
between a departmental official and a
political Parliamentary Secretary. The
departmental official carries out the
duties connected with his Department,
but the politician who is appointed a
Parliamentary Secretary generally acts
in an administrative capacity. Not only
does he do that, but he attends at the
House of Commons and in the. Senate for
the purpose of explaining the nature of
Bills emanating from his particular
Department. Consequently these are
two new Parliamentary Secretaries who
can be held up to opprobrious criticism
or flattery, just as the House feels
inclined. A Parliamentary Secretary can
be congratulated or he, can be condemned.
Major DOBBS: Can he be condemned
or flattered in the Senate as well as in
the House of Commons?
Mr. GORDON: No. I understand
that only a Cabinet Minister may appear
in this House.

Question to Minister.
WORKERS SENT TO GREAT
BRITAIN.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state the total number
of persons who, since June last, have
been sent to work in England and
Scotland by the Ministry of Labour;
how many of these were from Belfast
and the country respectively, and
whether he can state the religious
denomination of the men sent.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Before asking the
question on the Paper I should like to say
that we are all very pleased to see the
Leader of the House back again, and we
hope he has fully recovered from his
recent illness. I have put down this
question from two points of view.
certainly agree with a recent statement
I
Prorogation

Question to Minister
18 FEBRUARY 1941
[Mr. McLaughlin]
made by the Minister of Finance that he
would prefer that work came here rather
than that we should send our men away
to work. I would point out that a good
many of the men who have been sent
across the water to work are workers on
the land in Northern Ireland, and I fear
that if this policy is continued there will
be a scarcity of farm workers in this
country. I trust these land workers will
be kept in Northern Ireland to help in the
food production campaign.
I am sorry to say that when going about
the country I have observed that many
linen mills have been dismantled and the
machinery taken out of them for the purpose of billeting troops in those mills. I
fear that this will have a serious effect on
employment in the linen trade, and will
lead to a crisis in that industry.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
regret that it would be contrary to public
policy to state the total number of persons
who have been sent to work in Great
Britain since June last. I may, however, inform the hon. Senator that 48 per
cent. of the total came from the Belfast
area and the remaining 52 per cent. from
the rest of the country. No note is
taken, and therefore no information is
available of the religious denominations
of the men sent.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I cannot understand how it is that the Leader of the
House states
Mr. SPEAKER: Does the hon.
Member wish to ask a supplementary
question?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Yes. Why is it
that in October last in another place the
Minister of Labour was able to give the
percentage. I ask for, and the Leader of
this House informs me that it would be
contrary to public policy to do so.
Mr. ROBB: I do not know personally
about this matter, but my information is
that we are able to give you the percentage as between Belfast and the
country.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am talking
about the religious denomination of the
men sent away.
Mr. ROBB: No note is taken about
the religious denominations of workers
sent across the water, and therefore there
is no information available on that point.
Mr. BARCLAY: Is it not a fact that
these men are sent across the water by
the employment exchange? If that is so,
from my experience of the employment
exchanges they do not make inquiry as
to whether you are Orange or Green or
black or white. You are a workman in a
certain trade, and they send you across
the water to that particular trade.
PROROGATION OF PARLIAMENT.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command
from his Grace the Governor to read
forthwith in this Chamber to the Senators
and Commons of Northern Ireland
therein assembled his Grace's Proclamation proroguing this present Session
of Parliament.
Resolved: "That the acting Gentleman
Usher of the Black Rod be commanded to inform the Commons that the
Proclamation proroguing the present
Session of Parliament is to be read
forthwith, and inviting the Commons to
attend in order to hear the same."-(Mr.
Robb.)
The Commons, being at the Bar, with
their Speaker the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
his Grace's command), as followeth :-
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR OF
NORTHERN IRELAND
A PROCLAMATION.
For Proroguing the present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
certain enactments amending the said
Act, that the Governor of Northern Ireland shall, in His Majesty's name,
summon, prorogue and dissolve the
Parliament of Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the
twenty-fifth day of February,
thousand nine hundred and forty-one:
one
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
DUKE OF ABERCORN, KNIGHT OF
THE MOST NOBLE ORDER OF THE
GARTER, KNIGHT OF THE MOST
ILLUSTRIOUS ORDER OF SAINT
Prorogation
Prorogation
THE SENATE
PATRICK, Governor of Northern Ireland, by this Proclamation do publish
and declare His Majesty's pleasure that
the said Parliament be, as from the
reading of this Proclamation in the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday, the twenty-fifth
day of February, one thousand nine
hundred and forty-one.
AND I do hereby further declare His
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to be
that the said. Parliament shall on the said
twenty-fifth day of February,
thousand nine hundred and forty-one,
assemble and be holden at Stormont for
the despatch of divers urgent and imone
portant affairs, and the Senators and
Members of the House of Commons of
Northern Ireland are hereby required to
give their attendance accordingly.
Given this eighteenth day of February,
one thousand nine hundred and fortyone.
(Signed by his own hand),
ABERCORN.

KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor, being seated
on the Throne, commanded the acting
Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod
(Brigadier-General H. G. Young, C.I.E.,
D.S.O.) to let the Commons know "It
is his Grace's pleasure that they attend
him immediately in this House.'
""
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, his Grace said :-
-
Since I last attended in this Chamber
we have all sustained a very grievous loss
in the death of Lord Craigavon, the first
Prime Minister of Northern Ireland. As
a tribute to the memory of a great
Statesman who has passed away I will
now ask you to stand for a brief space.
The Company then stood.
His Grace then delivered the following
speech from the Throne:
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
The
The struggle in which the Empire is
engaged for liberty and justice has
entered upon its second year.
people of Northern Ireland have responded willingly to all the calls which
have been made upon them. Large
numbers of men and women have voluntered for His Majesty's Forces; the
civil defence services have been well
maintained, and the energy and initiative of employers, reinforced by the
adaptability of our workers, point to the
value of the contribution which is being
made on the Home Front.
The most friendly relationship exists
between the civil population and the
members of His Majesty's Services,
whose presence in Northern Ireland all
our people view with much pleasure,
and whose comfort and happiness while
stationed here are
our desire. My
Ministers have been active to secure the
fullest co-operation with the Forces of
the Crown in connection with the varied
administrative problems which arise.
The Local Defence Volunteers have
been pursuing their training under
military direction and guidance and
have reached a stage of efficiency which
reflects credit on all ranks of the Force,
and is viewed with satisfaction by the
General Officer commanding the troops
in Northern Ireland.
A
King's Speech
King's Speech
THE SENATE
Considerable progress has been made
with civil defence measures. It is
evident that the community as a whole
is becoming increasingly alive to the
vital importance of this subject.
Hitherto the necessity to call the civil
defence services into action has fortunately not arisen, but I have no doubt
that, if it should, all sections of the
people will acquit themselves with the
same courage and resource as have been
displayed in Great Britain, and which
have evoked the admiration of the
world.
I have been impressed by the splendid
response which has already been made
by our farmers to the appeal by my
Government for an increase in the area
under the plough so as to provide more
foodstuffs from our own soil. The
coming year will make even greater calls
upon our resources, but I am confident
that our farmers will redouble their
efforts to grow more food and also to
increase the acreage under flax in order
to provide to the greatest extent possible
the raw material upon which our linen
industry depends.
I am glad to learn that our textile
trades are being utilised to a greater
degree in supplying the needs of the
Forces of the Crown, and that they
have thus been able, within the past
few months, to provide additional employment for our workers. Notwithstanding the cessation of flax supplies
from the Continent, it has been found
possible to arrange an allocation of
flax yarns for the manufacture of linen
for the export trade, the maintenance of
which is of such vital importance to the
national effort. It is gratifying to
know that the total number of unemployed has fallen, during the past year,
by fifteen thousand.
My Ministers have been able to conclude a satisfactory arrangement with
departments of the Imperial Government
under which flax processing
factories will be erected in areas where
the need is greatest, and I hope that
the adoption of these modern methods
will have a beneficial effect on our linen
industry and make a substantial contribution to relieving unemployment.
A measure will be laid before you to
make the household means test for
Unemployment Assistance and Supplementary Pensions more favourable to the
claimants and also to disregard certain
War Savings in computing the resources
of such claimants for these purposes.
You will be invited to consider proposals
for the preservation of certain superannuation rights in the case of persons
transferring to war employment, and
for the amendment of the Education
(Emergency Provisions) Act, passed
last Session, so as to authorise the payment of a grant, in appropriate cases,
of the entire cost of the protection of
schools and training colleges against
damage from air attack. You will also
be asked to consider an amendment of
the Fisheries Acts for the purpose of
modifying the law relating to the discharge of flax water into rivers and
lakes.
Members of the House of Commons.
The Estimates for the Public Services
will be laid before you in due course.
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
The reduction to 60 of the age at
which women may become entitled to
contributory old age pensions resulted
in the grant of pensions to a large
number of women who were either
themselves insured or who were the
wives of men who had reached the age
of 65 and had qualified for contributory
pensions. The introduction of the
Supplementary Pensions Scheme last
August did much to ameliorate cases of
hardship amongst old age pensioners.
It is of the utmost importance at this
time that there should be the minimum
of interruption in the country's
machinery of production, and it is,
therefore, satisfactory to record that
very few stoppages have taken place.
The settlement of disputes has been
greatly facilitated by the establishment,
under the Emergency Powers (Defence)
National Arbitration
Acts, of the
Tribunal (Northern Ireland).
I am pleased to learn that no difficulty
has been experienced in making satisfactory provision for the continuance of
the education of those children who were
evacuated last summer from Belfast to
country districts.
It is a matter of satisfaction that the
internal peace of Northern Ireland has
been well maintained and that the
King's Speech-
Address in Reply to
25 FEBRUARY 1941
Royal Ulster Constabulary and the
"B" Class Special Constabulary have
been vigilant and successful in the
maintenance of law and order.
I pray that the blessing of Almighty
God may rest upon your deliberations.
Then his Grace was pleased to retire
and the Commons withdrew.

following words:-
"Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's dutiful and loyal
subjects, the Senate of the Parliament
of Northern Ireland, beg leave to offer
our humble thanks to your Grace for
the Speech which your Grace has
addressed to both Houses of Parliament."
In moving the Address to His Grace
the Governor thanking him for the Speech
of His Most Gracious Majesty to his Parliament in Northern Ireland, I deem it
a great personal honour not only to myself
but to the City of Londonderry, which I
represent as its Mayor to-day. His
Majesty and the Royal Family are no
strangers, I am glad to say, to our ancient
city, and His Grace the Governor won his
first political spurs and honoured our city
as its representative for many years in the
Imperial Parliament.
Before entering into any details, may I
be allowed to offer my humble congratulations to His Grace the Governor, who has
accepted the appointment for a third term
as His Majesty's deputy in Northern
Ireland, which still remains, and is determined to remain, an integral and loyal
part of the British Empire. The share
that we are now taking in the present
titanic struggle will ensure and secure to
posterity our right to remain in and to
continue that glorious heritage.
In passing, might I extend to our new
Prime Minister and the other Members
of his Cabinet our thankfulness and appreciation that he and they have been chosen
to take up the reins of office at this, a most
trying time in our Empire's history, when
their services to the State are so necessary, exacting, and momentous.
We pray
and beseech the Almighty that He will
endow them with strength, wisdom, and
vision for their great task.
In the Gracious Speech due stress has
been laid upon the services rendered to
our Province by the Civil Defence Forces,
including all departments of the A.R.P.,
under our new Minister of Public
Security, Major MacDermott, and his
staff, and all the local voluntary helpers in
this new war-time and highly essential
service. To be forewarned is to be forearmed, and to be armed and ready for the
fray is half the battle. Our police and
"B" Specials deserve all the praise we can
give them for the maintenance of peace
and order that we may pursue our normal
everyday duties and so preserve the sanity
of our Province.
As a working woman wrote Mr.
Churchill the other day: "What gentlemen they are.” This might be said of
all His Majesty's Forces at present in
Northern Ireland, who are delighted with
the hospitality and friendship accorded
them by the civilian population in their
King's Speech--
Address in Reply to
THE SENATE
[Mayor of Derry]
homes. Londonderry has received them,
and treated them as our defenders and
deliverers deserve to be treated. I have
personally met many of the men and
chiefs of the Royal Navy, the Army, and
the R.A.F., and a finer type of gentlemanly manhood it would be hard to find.
We are proud and thankful to have
such a numerous and exemplary body of
men to stand between us and an
arrogant and unrelenting tyrant, and
to have as Chief of the Imperial
General Staff a Lurgan man in the
person of Sir John Dill. General
Wavell, the hero of Libya-who has given.
such well-merited praise to General
O'Connor, General Creigh, General
Wilson, and Air Chief Marshal Longmore-and Admiral Cunningham and
numerous other leaders will lead us on to
victory, or, as Mr. Churchill said in his
recent broadcast: "Sustained by the
courage of our merchant seamen and of
the dockers and workers at all our ports
we shall outwit, outmanoeuvre, outfight,
and outlast the worst that the enemy's
malice and ingenuity can contrive"; and
might I be allowed to repeat his words
of praise to our indomitable airmen :
"Never in the history of nations did so
many owe so much to so few."
Might I say a word as to our contemplated domestic legislation. The Bill for
the abolition of the means test in the
estimating of means for unemployment
assistance and increase of old-age pensions will be heartily welcomed by all
who desire to see improved social and
cultural conditions in our community,
but non-contributory State help should
not be encouraged to the detriment of
wage-earners who may withhold the love
and duty they owe as members of the
family to the aged and dependent
parents. The old and infirm have surely
a claim upon their children who are substantial wage-earners as well as on the
State. Need'ess to say, the State is ourselves, and has its limitations. No one
desires to see poverty or distress in our
midst, and it is therefore for
amelioration or elimination of this condition that we recognise the brotherhood
of man. The Bill to preserve pension
rights to those joining the colours is but
just and equitable, and cannot possibly
be opposed on principle. No person
the
should be allowed to suffer financially in
this way. All honour to those who,
in
a secured position, answer the
country's call against the tyrant.
The Bill granting schools and local
councils 100 per cent. on an approved
expenditure for air-raid shelters will
transfer the financial burden from the
local to the National Budget, and as a
war-time measure must meet with our
unqualified approval.
As to the Bill dealing with the discharge of flax water from dams, this will
be dealt with by our agriculturists and
piscatorial friends. The feature or phase
of the matter I am afraid only appeals
to
my olfactory nerves, which I
fear is a somewhat selfish and shortsighted attitude on this very serious
question. It must not, however, be
imagined for a moment that I am indifferent to legislation affecting the
farming fraternity. Such a frame of
mind would be simply outrageous. We
in the cities and towns are
ever and
always indebted to our farmers and farm
workers, and everything possible should
be done to encourage them in their great,
constant and anxious toil to grow more
food and fodder for man and beast.
this connection I should like to say that
agricultural labourers should not
not be
induced to desert their employment on
the land for war work. The land must
be cultivated.
In
It must be a great satisfaction to the
Government to know how successful have
been their arrangements by the Ministry
of Education for the. continuance of the
education of evacuated children. All
honour to teachers and foster parents.
In conclusion, may I be allowed to
again express the loyal devotion of the
citizens of Londonderry to the Throne
and person of His Most Gracious Majesty
King George VI, and his beloved Queen
Elizabeth, and the charming young
Princesses, and that they, with His Grace
and our own Duchess of Abercorn, and all
those leaders whose skill, foresight,
devotion and fortitude we all reverence
and admire, may be spared in health and
vigour to rejoice with the people of this
Empire in a glorious victory as the result
of our united and unanimous efforts in
God's good time by the defeat of a
ruthless, rash, and cruel foe. I have
much pleasure in moving this dutiful
Address to His Grace the Governor.
King's Speech-
Address in Reply to
25 FEBRUARY 1941
Mr. KINGAN (in morning dress): In
rising to second this humble Address of
thanks for the Gracious Speech from the
Throne, may I be permitted to say how
greatly I as one of the more recently
elected members of this Chamber appreciate this privilege, which I regard not
only as a compliment to me, but also
through me to the people of County Down.
In these grave times I feel sure that it
is a matter of great satisfaction to hon.
Members to know that the response to the
call for Local Defence Volunteers has been
so satisfactory, and that their training has
reached such a high level of efficiency, and
is regarded with satisfaction by the
General Officer Commanding the Troops
in Northern Ireland. The subject of Civil
Defence has been much in the minds of all
thoughtful people, especially during the
last few months, and I know that we all
realise that there is urgent necessity for
the civil population to make every effort
to organise and train themselves so as to
be prepared to meet the dangers which
threaten our land. The measures being
taken by our Government to secure this
end meet, I am sure, with our unanimous
approval and support. We are fighting
against a ruthless enemy and we must be
prepared to meet attack in every way.
One of the most important and difficult
questions which the war has brought to
the forefront is the supply of food for our
population, and in this connection a heavy
burden and responsibility are placed upon
the farmers of our Province. It is
gratifying to know that the call which was
made by the Government for the ploughing up of grass land and for additional
tillage last year met with such a splendid
response, and I feel confident that the
additional effort which this year calls for
will be made in the same spirit. I have
no doubt that we all realise that the
necessity for additional tilage raises
problems for those engaged in agriculture,
and that we appreciate the loyal and
patriotic way in which the farmers have
met the situation and are playing their
part in the great war effort.
Hon. Members will, no doubt, notice
with satisfaction the reference in the
Gracious Speech from the Throne to our
very important textile industry, and will
be pleased to know that its resources are
being used more and more to supply the
necessary materials required by the armed
forces of the Crown. The arrangements
which our Government have been able to
make with the Imperial Government and
which will result in the erection of flax
processing factories, are an important
step towards assisting the linen industry,
and I am confident that it will be an
encouragement to the producers of flax to
increase their output and will provide a
further outlet for employment.
Hon. Members will, doubtless, have
heard with pleasure that the numbers of
unemployed persons have fallen substantially during the past year, and I trust
that there may
be a still greater
diminution in the coming year. This war,
which is naturally uppermost in all our
minds, has presented difficult problems
to every member of the community, but I
feel that with courage and determination
we can overcome all difficulties, and that
we are justified in looking forward with
sober confidence to victory and to the
ultimate triumph of our cause. I second
the Motion.
Question proposed.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): It is
with great pleasure that I express the
congratulations of the House to the mover
and seconder of this Address. It is quite
true that the Speech from the Throne did
not offer such a wide field of comment as
is customary in such speeches, but the
mover and seconder have both of them
discharged the duty that fell upon them
with grace, humour and brevity, which is,
of course, commendable not only on this,
but on most other occasions. They are
new Members, and we were delighted to
hear one of them for the first time, and
we hope this will not be the only occasion
on which we shall have the advantage of
their contributions towards our discussions.
As I said the Speech did not offer such
a wide field for comment as is usual, but
there are one or two matters upon which
I might perhaps make one or two observations. It begins with a reference to the
contribution made by our people in this
Province towards the general war effort.
As has already been said there has been
an excellent response in this district to the
call for Local Defence Volunteers, and I
think it is being seen more and more
every day that the Local Defence
Volunteer might be called upon to discharge duties not only exacting, but vital
in the defence of the country. And might
ii

King's Speech--
Address in Reply to
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
I incidentally, although it is not referred
to in the Speech, call the attention of the
community in general to the effort which
has recently been started to form in our
midst an air training corps for the preliminary training of boys between 16 and
18, to enable them, when they come to be
18 and are eligible for enlistment in the
Air Force or any of the other Forces, to
qualify for the duties of that force more
quickly.
I am a little bit afraid to say how long
it takes ordinarily to train a man for the
duties of the Air Force, but this scheme
is designed to enable boys between the
ages of 16 and 18 to go through, before
they reach the age of 18, a considerable
portion of the training which they would
have to undergo after 18 were nothing
done for them before. The object is to
speed up very much the output of trained
men-pilots, air crew of various sorts,
ground staff, too, so that the danger,
which is a serious possibility, that the time
may come when we would have more
aeroplanes available than we can man,
may be averted.
Air Commodore Chamier, who was here
recently, told us that definitely there was
that possibility, unless something was done.
to speed up the training of these airmen.
I have no doubt whatever that in our area
we should have an ample supply of young
men, boys between 16 and 18, but I would
like to appeal again, as I appealed before
at a meeting held in the Wellington Hall,
for volunteers for officers for this corps.
They must be over 32 years of age to start
with at least that is the age limit set
down. It is not necessary that they
should be ex-servicemen, although exservicemen would, of course, start with
considerable advantage. The course for
these boys will consist of a certain amount
of instruction in mathematics and in
English, a certain amount of drill, a certain amount of physical training, and a
considerable amount of direct instruction
in the construction and manipulation of
aircraft.
Now, there must be in our Province
many men who have had experience in
leading boys in various organisations, and
who know they have a certain facility, a
certain power of adaptability, and a
certain capability to help boys at that age,
and here is a service to which they can
turn their hands. It is a voluntary
service. They will be given uniform, but
they will not receive any pay.
It is a
spare-time service. These boys, many of
whom will be working, will only have the
evening, Saturday, and week-ends, which
they may devote to this training. I again
appeal, as far as I can appeal, to any persons in this Province who have capabilities
which may be utilised in officering this
corps. I am quite certain the boys will
be forthcoming if we have the people to
train them.
One other point in regard to the Civil
Defence Service I would like to mention
is this. As time has gone on the tactics
in air attack have changed, and more
and more the attack has changed over
from high explosive to incendiary bombs.
I would like on behalf of the Government
to appeal for the utmost loyalty on the
part of all our people in responding to
the requirements and the advice given
by the Ministry of Public Security with
regard to fire watching and the practice.
of fire quenching. The great fire started
in the City of London might have been
very much ameliorated if we had known
then what we know now, and had we
realised the necessity for constant watching, constant training, and constant
effort to defeat the incendiary bomb.
The enemy was very adroit. The attack
was made on a night when as a matter
of ordinary business routine warehouses,
offices, and shops were empty. There
was nobody there to stop the spread of
the fire at the start, and that is what we
want more than anything else. That is
a matter in which everybody can help.
Everybody can be trained to extinguish.
an incendiary bomb. It is a duty that
devolves upon all of us, and it cannot be
too frequently or too emphatically commented upon.
I need not say anything about the
efforts our farmers have made and are
making in the development of our food
supply and the raw materials for our
industries. The necessity for efforts of
this kind is, I am sure, present in the
mind of every farmer and farm labourer
in the North of Ireland, and I know
they are prepared to do their best. Last
year many held back from sowing flax
through doubt as to whether they would
be able to get their flax scutched or find
a market for it green. The Government
has now undertaken that, if a farmer
cannot scutch his flax, they will
King's Speech--
Address in Reply to
25 FEBRUARY 1911
buy it green, and the Government
is also endeavouring to set up processing
factories wherein all flax that will be
forthcoming can be prepared for the
linen trade. With that assurance
hope the acreage under flax, upon which
our Province is so dependent now that
foreign supplies are cut off, will be
greatly increased.
We
Turning now to the problem of unemployment, may I give one figure to show
how the position is gradually improving.
The Gracious Speech records that during
last year the numbers of unemployed
registered fell by 15,000. That is a calculation from 1st January, 1940, to 1st
January, 1941, and it is with great
pleasure that I can report that during
the month of January, 1941, there was
a further fall of 7,000 in the number
registered, thus increasing the figure of
15,000 to 22,000. That is a gratifying
fact. Of course unemployment continues
and must continue with us a problem of
the very greatest difficulty. In the city
of Belfast and in our towns so many of
our population are dependent upon the
prosperity of the linen trade that it is
inevitable that the cutting off of the
supply of raw material from abroad
must produce a considerable amount of
unemployment. We hope it will be reduced in the future, but the linen trade
is a difficult one under present circumstances, and it has been increasingly
difficult for some years past. We can
only hope that the efforts of the Government will help to further reduce the
number of people unemployed.
Coming to the social services, I can
perhaps give some figures that may be
of interest to the House. The Supplemental Pensions Act came into force last
August. At the present time 35,000
supplemental pensions are being paid at
a cost of £675,000 a year. In addition,
6,200 additional women have obtained
contributory old-age pensions through
the reduction of the qualifying age to
sixty. Those pensions cost £161,200 a
year.
The legislation that is in contemplation
this Session is naturally limited. In
time of war such as this one cannot enter
upon highly controversial legislation, nor
can the Government embark upon experiments or developments in the ordinary
social services which might cost money
and which could very well be postponed.
measures
There are, however, four
referred to in the Speech about which I
might say a word. First of all, there is
a Bill to modify the means test. The
final form of that Measure is not yet
certain because we here will ask Parlia
ment to pass the Bill in the same form as
the Bill now before the Imperial Parliament will ultimately assume. That
form is not yet quite settled, but in the
main what it is proposed to do is to
change the present means test into a
personal means test. Under that test only
the applicant's own resources and those
of the applicant's wife or husband and
dependents will in general be taken into
account, and they will not be taken into
account up to a limit of £375 of new
money invested in war savings after, I
think, 20th August of last year.
The second Bill deals with the preservation of pension rights. That does
not affect the Civil Service, but it is a
Bill to provide that, where persons who
were in employment were contributors to
some pension fund, and have left that
employment and gone into one of the
services of the Crown, their pension rights
shall be preserved. No one can object to
that, and I am sure the Bill will find
ready support in this House.
The third Bill deals with air-raid protection in schools and colleges. The
House will remember that the Education
(Emergency Provisions) Bill provided
only for a percentage contribution from
Government funds to the cost of the provision of air-raid shelters in schools. In
England they have altered that, and they
have provided that a 100 per cent. grant
will be paid by the Government in cases
where the shelters can be made available,
not in school hours, to the general public.
We propose to follow that example here.
The last Bill is one dealing with the
vexed problem of flax water discharged
from the land into rivers. I can say quite
frankly that the final form these proposals will take has not yet been decided,
but it is a matter that is engaging
the anxious attention of the Government
and the Bill will be brought before
Parliament very soon. Its object is to
provide that farmers may not be deterred
from growing flax by the absolute prohibition that no. flax water may be
allowed to get into the rivers.
Reference is made. in the Gracious
Speech-and it has been commented upon
King's Speech-
Address in Reply to
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
by previous speakers-to the ease. with
which children evacuated from Belfast
have been absorbed into the educational
system in the country. That cuts two
ways. It has been so easy only because
there were so few children evacuated. It
is a matter for congratulation that the
education system in the country has not
been disturbed, but whether it will be a
matter for ultimate congratulation or not
one does not know. However, there it is.
Those children who have been evacuated
have been easily and without friction,
and by the goodwill of the teachers and
managers of country schools, absorbed
into the existing schools. It is a matter
that has given very anxious thought to
all those concerned with education. In
the meantime we are. gratified that we
have not met with all the difficulties we
expected to meet with, but whether that
is only because the evacuation has been
on a small scale remains to be seen.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I most heartily
associate myself with the Leader of the
House in his congratulations to the Mayor
of Derry and Senator Kingan for the very
able way in which they discharged a most
difficult task. In fact, I might say they
turned this skeleton Speech into a healthy
body. The Leader of the House, with all
his ingenuity and all his undoubted ability
and resource in debate, found the empty
Speech so embarrassing that he launched
out and made a very good speech of his
own.
The first thing I intend to deal with is
the reference to the position of the
farmers of Northern Ireland. You may
say it is a waste of time to turn back our
memories over the past 20 years, but my
suggestion is if we learn for the future
by the failures of the past it is not a waste
of time. It has occurred to me what tremendous and colossal sums of money were
paid out, and necessarily paid out, in unemployment benefit without any revenue
therefrom. What a tremendous mistake
has been made, which we are paying for
to-day, that a considerable amount of
that money was not expended on draining
and reclaiming thousands of acres of land
in Northern Ireland which would have
been available to-day to augment the food
supplies which are necessary to this
community.
Think of all the thousands of acres that
could have been made more productive,
more fruitful and more profitable. Now,
the argument against these unemployment
schemes and these improvement schemes,
as I understood it, put up by the Government has always been that the cost of
material to carry out these improvement
and unemployment schemes was so great
that it would not be justifiable expenditure. But I suggest to the House that
anyone with an elementary idea of the
question of drainage of land knows that
the cost of material required, apart from
wages for this purpose, was practically
infinitesimal, and therefore I say that the
Government bear upon their shoulders a
tremendous responsibility for any shortage
or difficulty there is in the production of
food for the necessities of Northern
Ireland to-day.
There is a reference in the Speech in
which it is stated that it is gratifying to
know that there is a reduction of 15,000
in the number of unemployed in Northern
Ireland. The Leader of the House
was naturally embarrassed to see that
statement and to deal with it. Who
was
the genius with the
the inspiration
to put that inane statement into the
King's Speech, for of course it is not the
speech of the King? We have 70,000
unemployed, and it is a matter of gratification that 15,000 of these have been
absorbed in this war. Now I know that
there is a Bill to be introduced to ease the
household means test, but of course that
will be a question for discussion when the
Bill is introduced. I hope, however, that
the humiliating conditions necessary at
present to obtain that relief will be done
away with.
There is one question which I would
like again to draw the attention of the
House to. A Bill is to be introduced
making the test for supplementary
pensions more favourable to the claimants
than at the present time. It may seem a
strange thing to the House to hear or read
about, that the one class of pensioners
excluded from this supplementary scheme.
is the most deserving and the most helpless of all, the blind persons. I have
appealed to the Government many times
to ease the provisions by which the people
are entitled to blind pensions. Surely if
there is one class in the country entitled
to consideration in this scheme of supplementary pensions to be introduced, it is
those at present entitled to blind pensions.
At the end of the Speech there is a
reference to the internal peace in this area
King's Speech-
Address in Reply to
25 FEBRUARY 1941
and a reference to the Royal Ulster and
Special Constabulary. I say without
hesitation that the Royal Ulster Constabulary, taken as a whole, discharge
their duties fairly, satisfactorily and
efficiently (hon. Members: Hear, hear)-
and if they are the victims of a vicious
system of administration it is not their
fault. It has always been a grave
humiliation to me as an Irishman and
Ulsterman to have to raise the question
of sectarian injustice in this House or in
any part of Northern Ireland. As I
understand it the duty of the Prime
Minister and the Government is that of
trustees and members of the Government
when they accept office are the trustees
and custodians of the liberties and
legitimate rights of the people-not only
of their own followers, but of every sect
and creed and every person under their
jurisdiction. I have often wondered if
the Government of Northern Ireland
realise that this is the position or if they
deliberately ignore it.
But what do we find? We find constitutional law wiped to one side in
Northern Ireland. In these days of great
democracies, when Europe is fighting for
democracy as the ideal system of
government, we find that the most reactionary government in Europe,
although it has only parochial duties to
perform, is situated in Northern Ireland.
I have been a constitutional Nationalist
all my life, but who can justify the
hundreds of men to-day taken from their
homes, the bread-winners and support of
families, and held in jail without the
right of trial or the right to appear
before a court, at the dictates of some
official probably working on some
anonymous letter or some report, Is
that the democracy for which you are
fighting to-day? Is that the democracy
which the great democratic people of
America are supporting? What is the
grave position in this supposedly
Christian country? We are not living
in Russia. What is the position?
Raids on raids in Catholic districts of
Northern Ireland are taking place in the
hours of the night or early morning
darkness. Catholic homes are being
desecrated. The privacy and sanctity of
Catholic homes have been torn down.
Is that democracy? Is that consistent
with the views of great democratic
countries like America? Is that the ideal
which men are going out to fight and lose
their lives for? Is it not tyranny of the
worst possible type? I have said the
Government with any sense of duty are
the trustees and custodians of all the
people, and have in trust the rights and
privileges of all the people. We are told
and we are led to believe that a man's
home is his castle, but in certain parts
of Belfast, with one hundred raids a
night in a limited area, a man's home
is made by a vicious Government composed of those of a different religion, not
his castle but his prison cell. I say it is
humiliation to me as an Irishman and
Ulsterman and proud of the fact to be
compelled to expose such a dastardly
state of affairs as a public representative.
I trust the lessons of this war will come
to the Government of Northern Ireland,
and that they will realise that we refuse
to live in a titulary Christian country,
but intend as long as we are public representatives of the Nationalist people to
expose by every means this type of
coercion.
Just one more word before I sit down.
I mean no personal reflection on the
Mayor of Derry. I know him. But he
has made a statement here to-day that
he comes here as the representative of
the people of Derry. With all due
respect to the Mayor of Derry, as a
public representative I am bound to say
he is not entitled to make that claim.
He is the Mayor of Derry under a hateful and indefensible system of gerrymandering the political representation of
that city-an indefensible system by
which the majority are denied their
rights as citizens. The Mayor of Derry
-I mean no disrespect to him personally
---cannot come here and say he represents
Derry. He represents a minority of the
people of Derry.

I am glad to see in the Speech from the
Throne that a measure of credit-I will
not say altogether of praise-has been
bestowed upon this Province with regard
to the number of men and women who
have joined His Majesty's Forces. Some
people seem to think that Northern
Ireland is not contributing to
Majesty's Forces as it should. But, personally, I know great numbers have
joined His Majesty's Forces and are
serving. They may not be found in the

because they have been taken for training across the water, while English and
Scotch contingents are brought over

been so maligned by the last speaker-

Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: Not the

The last paragraph of the Speech, to
which Senator McAllister has taken exception, states:
It is a matter of satisfaction that the
internal peace of Northern Ireland has
been well maintained and that the
Royal Ulster Constabulary and the
"B" Class Special Constabulary have
been vigilant and successful in the
maintenance of law and order.
I think any man who has the interests
of law and order in Northern Ireland at
heart will heartily endorse that statement.
When we consider the black-out and one
thing and another arising out of the war,
the men of the Royal Ulster Constabulary
and the "B" Special Constabulary have
carried out their duties in a manner that
deserves our highest praise. I thoroughly
agree with the statement made by Senator
McAllister that the Royal Ulster Constabulary has carried out its duties fairly,
satisfactorily and efficiently, and I wish
he could have extended that to the
auxiliary force, the "B" Special Constabulary. I know they have been doing
duty both day and night, and they run
considerable risks in maintaining peace
not only in Belfast but throughout the
country.
There is one aspect of this matter that
has caused a good deal of trouble and
anxiety, and that is juvenile crime. In
opening the Commission on this day week,
the Lord Chief Justice referred to the
growth of juvenile crime. It is not confined to Northern Ireland, Great Britain,
or Eire, but it seems to be rife all over
the world. It is regrettable that some
parents will not rise to their responsibilities and see that their children are looked
after better than they are. They are
allowed to drift with no parental control,
and they do things in the streets for which
they sometimes come before Courts of
Justice.
With regard to the reduction in the unemployment figure of some 15,000, that
is a matter for congratulation. We know
that a good deal of the present unemployment has been created by conditions which
prevail in the flax-spinning industry.
Flax was held back under orders, and some
of our linen mills had to close down. It
appears there is going to be more material
released in order to allow our mills and
factories to operate to a larger extent than
they have been doing.
It is quite possible that young people
who get into mischief are unemployed.
We all know there are many young people
who have never been employed until they
were 18 or 19 years of age.
boys who have been running about from
the time they left school until they were
18, and it is remarkable that the mischief

KING'S SPEECH.
Debate on the Address.
Order read for resuming debate on
Question (25th February), "That an
humble Address be presented to His
Grace in the following words:
"Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's most dutiful and
loyal subjects, the Senate of the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, beg
leave to offer our humble thanks to
your Grace for the Speech which your
Grace has addressed to both Houses of
Parliament."(The Mayor of Derry.)
Question again proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: Like other Members I
listened with interest and pleasure to the
observations made by the hon. Senators
who moved and seconded the Address.
Those hon. Senators have one point in
common, and they have another point of
difference, and yet even that difference
is not very far apart from a union. The
first point of union, of course, is the
fact that both hon. Members come to us
with a fairly recent mandate. The
second point, the point of difference
perhaps, is that one of them, the Mayor
of Derry, sits here ex-officio and by
Statute as the representative, constitutionally elected, of the Maiden City of
Londonderry. The other hon. Member,
also constitutionally elected, comes to us
in the more ordinary manner by the
voice of those who sit in another place.
Both hon. Members have discharged their
duty to our great pleasure and satisfaction, and I join in the hope expressed
by the Leader of the House that we may
in the future very often hear their voice
in our councils.
Passing to the Speech itself, it is a
Speech not of any great length, but it
does contain certain detail which demands more than passing comment. It
does give occasion for thought in the
various directions to which it points our
thoughts. The Speech opens with a recognition of the response which is being
made by the people of Northern Ireland
to the calls which come to them arising
from the present national emergency. I
only mention it in order to emphasise
once more that Northern Ireland is not
responsible for the fact that conscription,
as it applies in Great Britain, does not
apply in Northern Ireland. Any services.
that are given here in the Forces of the
Crown or the Civil Defence Forces or
ancillary Services are given completely
voluntarily, and I do not know that they
lose anything from that attribute. But
the fact does remain, and it cannot be
too often insisted upon, that conscription
as they know it across the water does not
exist here, and it is not the fault of this
Parliament or this people that that conscription does not exist.
The next point that may properly be
dealt with is the recognition of the fact
that a certain number of His Majesty's
armed Forces are at present resident
amongst us in Northern Ireland, and the
Gracious Speech points to the fact that
they have been universally received
with satisfaction, and that everybody is anxious to do all they
can to promote their comfort and
their efficiency. I should like to think
that that observation in the Gracious
Speech will meet with enthusiastic acceptance throughout our Province, but I
should also like to think that it may lead
to some slight widening of those views on
Sunday observance which prevail in some
parts of the Province. I do hope it will
be more and more recognised that,
although it is the right of every individual
to spend the first day of the week as he
chooses, it is not for him to impose any
obstacle in the paths of those who wish
to spend the day somewhat differently.
No individual has the right to deny
amusement and reasonable recreation to
people who may prefer to spend part of
that day in that manner. (Hon. Members:
Hear, hear.)
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
27 FEBRUARY 1941
The next important point in the
Gracious Speech deals with what one may
describe as the tillage campaign. It
speaks of the necessity of the farmer
giving his mind and his hands to the
plough. That is all very well. The
demands made upon the farmer in the
way of ploughing this year are an increase
on the demands made last year,
and although every effort is, so far as I
can observe myself and from what I have
heard from other people, being made to
carry out the programme at the moment
of speaking, it is somewhat doubtful
whether the full realisation will be
secured. As we know, the winter has
been severe, and the approach of spring
is not altogether as pleasant as we could
wish. But even supposing the actual
extra ploughing is done, it must be
remembered what remains behind.
While
unless you
cannot reap
you
plough, ploughing does not necessarily
mean that you will be able to reap.
harvest must depend to a great extent on
the weather, and when you have ploughed
your land you have not done with it.
Subsequently comes the harrowing and so
on, and that fact that a largely increased
area of land was put under plough last
year means that a largely
a largely increased
amount of harrowing has to be done this
year. The future as regards that is at
the moment somewhat doubtful, but if
the new programme does not attain complete realisation it will not be for want of
any goodwill, but from the necessities
imposed by a greater power than we can
contradict.
The
It is possible, I understand, to plough
by moonlight, but moonlight, although it
will enable you to plough, will not dry
the land, and much of our Ulster land
is unfortunately unduly wet. Whether
that wetness is due to lack of drainage,
and whether that lack of drainage is due
to a lack of foresight on the part of our
Government in imposing restrictions on
grants in the few years preceding the war
is a question on which I will not enter
at the moment. His succession to the
office of Prime Minister has afforded our
present Prime Minister the opportunity
of displaying originality in that matter
and perhaps of doing something more
for drainage, even if that which is done
is not strictly in accord with what has
been done across the water.
In the Gracious Speech we are also
referred to the desirability of growing
more flax in order to help the linen industry, directly and indirectly, and to
support the exchange rate between ourselves and countries abroad. Far be it
for me to decry the growing of flax in
any way whatever, but I must point out
that if a field is devoted to flax it cannot in the same year be devoted to the
growing of food. If you are going to
have flax for the mill you are not going
to have food for the appetite. Flax is
now put in the forefront, but for many
years past Northern Ireland flax has
been steadily decried. We have been
told again and again that flax grown in
Northern Ireland cannot compete with
flax grown in Belgium and other parts.
an
Flax figures twice in the Gracious
Speech. It is not only mentioned as a
desirable crop to be produced, but we
are also warned that there will be a
Measure submitted dealing with
amendment of the law regarding the disposal of flax water. I should be the
last to suggest that any difficulty should
be put in the way of a man who wishes
to put his flax through the old damming
process, but I must point out that the
release of flax water into the rivers has
a very serious effect on both trout and
salmon fishing. Both these branches of
piscatorial art are of very great importance to the country. They represent
the expenditure of large sums of private
money. Private interests are involved,
and the public interest is involved as
regards the protection of salmon as an
article of food.
I must point out that there are
methods of getting rid of flax water without doing serious harm to the rivers.
There is the catch dam and the possibility of pumping the water on to the
land and so manuring it, and making
some use of irrigation. The evil effect of
polluting a stream is not limited to the
destruction of fish. There is the trouble
of interference with water used by cattle
in the fields, and the further trouble,
which is perhaps borne in upon us at present rather more than it has been in past
years the problem that arises when the
water supply intended for adaptation for
human consumption is contaminated.
Those matters have to be carefully considered, and it will be for both Houses
to weigh very carefully those proposals.
We do not yet know what these proposals
are, but they will require very careful
consideration.
