Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
Allusion is made to the gratifying fall
in the unemployment figures. Those
figures, it is said, represent a reduction
of 15,000, but they still leave us with
some 60,000 unemployed. We can only
hope, that that may mend one way or
another. To some extent amelioration has
been produced by finding people work in
England or Scotland. That is all to the
good. Many of those people will enjoy
their new work and their new surroundings, and will perhaps see a little more
of the world than they would had they
remained in Northern Ireland, and they
will gather more advantage in the way
of money by working over there.
The universal cry in Northern Ireland
is to establish new industries. It is a
beautiful ideal, but we cannot get away
from the fact that Northern Ireland cannot support an indefinite number of new
industries. The industries concerned with
the war are particularly industries also
concerned with metal which requires coal
for its treatment. In Northern Ireland we
have neither iron nor coal, and if you
are to turn Northern Ireland to any great
extent into a munition factory you have
to face, the fact that all the coal you
employ will have to be imported. You
have to import the materials first, make
up your finished product, and then ship
it back again.
I am afraid I think-without prejudice.
to supporting any reasonable proposal for
establishing new industries-that we
should do better to concentrate on the
industries we have, in the first place agriculture, and in the second place linen and
shipbuilding, for which we have some
facilities apart from the lack of coal and
iron. That is a matter which can be dealt
with only tentatively. You can only
try your new industries and if it succeeds
so much the better. If it does not you
had better cut your losses.
I
Next we come to the reference in the
Speech to the household means test.
imagine we shall adopt the same. legisla

that the main point is that in dealing
with a man's dependants you will only
deal with his wife and children and not
go further afield, or further round the
house. I say further afield because that
was the term originally used. At present
there are all sorts of people regarded as
dependants, and there is a very general
feeling that that is not quite fair. The
rates to be paid will no doubt require
consideration. As things are, with the
increasing stringency as regards money,
it is not desirable that those rates should
be put up too high. We ought not to
depart further than we have already
departed from the old fashioned recommendation that thrift has its advantages,
and that these various forms of subvention in the way of unemployment
assistance are not intended to take the
place of thrift. People are expected to
save out of their incomes if they can to
provide for that rainy day which at
present is very much upon us. Those are
matters not particularly perhaps for this
House.
Reference is made to the right hon.
Gentleman's own Department. We are
to have an Education Bill which will enable the State to meet the cost of certain
structural alterations required in the
matter of air raid precautions. The idea
is that the State will bear that cost and
the local authorities will not be required
to contribute. If that is so, I hope the
local authorities and other authorities
concerned with schools, which is not always
the same thing, will receive that form of
assistance with some feelings of gratitude,
partly to the Government for introducing
the Bill, but still more to the taxpayer,
who will have to bear the burden.
There remains only the concluding remark regarding the condition of the
Province and our comparative freedom
from organised crime. But there is
nothing said about the increase in juvenile
delinquency. That is not a matter on
which we can congratulate ourselves, and
I had rather hoped that we would have
had some indication in the Speech that it
was proposed to deal with that valuable
Report submitted some two years ago. I
do not say that I shall bind myself to agree
with every item in the Report, but I had
hoped we should have heard there was
some intention to bring proposals before
Parliament even during the war. Speaking of even during the war, I am reminded
of another matter, and that is the desire
widely expressed throughout the Province.
of an extension of County Court jurisdiction. Questions regarding that have
been asked in this House and in another
place, and in each case the reply was that
the Government were considering the
matter. The proposal was only put forward as a war measure, and one would

There are many things on which we can

Mr. TAYLOR: I should like to join
with the previous speakers in congratulating the mover and seconder of the
Address. Their speeches were in good
taste and excellent in every respect. The
reference in the Gracious Speech to the
friendly relationship existing between
the civil population and the members of
His Majesty's Forces is most gratifying.
We in Northern Ireland are loyal
subjects of His Majesty, and we welcome
all those who are wearing the King's
uniform. We cannot do sufficient for
our fighting forces. They are protecting
and safeguarding us from cruel tyrants,
and we are confident that they will crush
and defeat Hitler and Mussolini, and
their brutal, unscrupulous and bloodthirsty gangs.
It is the duty of every citizen to help.
in defence measures, and there is a
vacancy for every man and woman in the
Home Defence. We cannot neglect the
home front. The danger is imminent,
and we must be prepared for any
eventuality. Ulster is playing a noble
part in this grim struggle. The "Spitfire"
fund, inaugurated by
by the
"Belfast
Telegraph," has raised over £86,000;
War Weapons Week raised about seven
millions, and Ulster Saving Certificates
about six millions, but we are not only
giving of our wealth. Though conscription
does not apply to Ulster our young men
in their thousands have joined the Army,
the Navy, and the Air Force. Whereever the fighting is hottest, whether it be
on land, sea, or in the air-wherever
sacrifices have to be made or deeds of
valour displayed Ulstermen are in the
van, as the awards and decorations for
gallantry clearly indicate.
Little legislation is foreshadowed in
the Gracious Speech, and I think under
present circumstances that is only right.
I am glad, however, to notice that a
Bill is to be introduced making the household means test for unemployment
assistance and supplementary pensions
more favourable to the claimants. This
will remove the worst features of the
household means test. In its application
to supplementary pensions and unemployment assistance the Bill does not
abolish the means test, but only certain
aspects of it.
At present the incomes of all people
in the household are taken into account
in assessing allowances. Under the Bill
if an unemployed man or an old-age
pensioner has a wage-earning wife, son, or
daughter living with him, their earnings
will be taken into consideration as a
means test, but the incomes of son-inlaw, daughter-in-law, or father-in-law,
living in the house, wil! now be
disregarded. Whilst the Bill is a little
more favourable to claimants, I think it
would have been better to abolish the
means test altogether.
I am sure we are all delighted that
our Ulster farmers are putting forth
such magnificent efforts to provide us
with food supplies, and thus to ensure
shipping space for munitions and the
transport of troops, I have no doubt those
efforts will be continued with vigour and
success. Here I should like to congratulate Lord Glentoran on his appointment as Minister of Agriculture, an
appointment which is heartily approved
by the whole farming community. As a
practical farmer his lordship is the right
man in the right place. He is most
popular among the farmers and already
has made his influence felt. We wish
him all success in the difficult tasks
which lie ahead.
I would be wanting in my duty if I did
not also warmly congratulate our new
Prime Minister. In following Lord
Craigavon, whose lamented death we all
deplore, Mr. Andrews's office will be no
easy one; but we are certain that with
his foresight, wisdom and sagacity he will
steer the ship of Ulster through all
hazardous waters, and prove a worthy
successor to the great statesman who
guided us so safely and triumphantly in
the past.
I am sorry
that Senator McAllister
should have introduced a controversial
subject. At this time when we are
engaged in a life and death struggle we

Mr. McALLISTER: On a point of
order, is it in order for an hon. Senator

Mr. TAYLOR: The citizens of Derry,
like the people of other parts of Northern
Ireland, are not behind in their war
efforts, and many of them, like their
forefathers, have joined up to repel the
invader. Yet Senator McAllister seizes
upon this occasion to refer to gerrymandering. His allegations are without
the slightest foundation,, and to prove
the truth of my statement I shall read an
extract from a speech by the late Lord
Craigavon. Speaking in the House of
Commons on 24th April, 1934, his Lordship said:
When this Ulster Government took
office we sent down the Deputy Recorder
of Belfast, a learned King's counsel,
and a man whom everybody respected
in his profession. He was quite free
of the Government or of any taint of
the Government either before the Act
of 1920 or after it. We sent him
throughout the Province in order that
he should map out what should be the
proper local government areas. We left
the matter entirely to him.
He issued an advertisement for
every county and every local authority.
into whose area he went, stating that
on certain days he would sit and hear
the evidence, not of Orangemen, not of
Loyalists, but of everyone inside that
area, the Nationalists as well as the
others. And what happened? What
help did the Nationalists give him?
They boycotted his court. They refused
to allow anybody to go near him to
give evidence. They left the whole
business entirely in his hands, and he
did the best he could in fairness to the
people in making out a proper schedule
for local government in Northern
Ireland.
Surely that is a sufficient reply to
Senator McAllister and those who make
allegations about gerrymandering. Then
in the House of Commons, on 21st
November, 1934, when further allegations
of "gerrymandering" were made by the
Nationalist members, Lord Craigavon, in
reply, said:
They (the Nationalists) boycotted the
Deputy Recorder sent down to readjust
the electoral divisions. Now I hold
very strongly when an eminent lawyer
is appointed to investigate and fix the
local government areas and he invites
evidence from all those interested and
they refuse to give him any assistance,
they have no cause to complain if the
areas are not in accord with their
wishes. But it was hoped by this boycotting action to pour scorn on this
Parliament and to prevent it from
functioning.
Does Senator McAllister suggest that
in fixing the areas the Deputy Recorder
did not carry out his duties with strict
impartiality, free from Government interference and in accordance with population
and rateable valuation? I really think in
view of the Government's repeated denials
that there was any "gerrymandering,"
action should be taken against those who
in order to create disaffection and trouble
persist in making allegations for which
there is not the slightest foundation.

Now I come to a matter affecting the
Press. Even the Press cannot be left
alone.. Even the Roman Catholic Press
cannot get fair play. I have a case under
my notice at the present time of the
"Ulster Herald," a County Fermanagh
Roman Catholic paper, which I am given
to understand has the largest circulation of
any weekly paper in County Fermanagh.
That paper is deprived of the insertion of
the notices and regulations issued by the
verious Government Departments. I hold
that that is unfair to the Roman Catholic
minority, and I go further and say that if
such a state of affairs is going to continue,
and if you cease to insert these important
notices in our Roman Catholic papers, you
deprive our Roman Catholic people of that
information. It is a well known custom
in this country that you read your
political organs and I read mine.
Mr. ROBB: Some of us read both.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Very few people
do that. Beyond their own they read no
other paper, and if this state of affairs
continues and any prosecution is brought
against these people, you cannot hold
them responsible for any offence against
regulations that are not published in every
paper in the country, irrespective of
politics, religion or creed.
I am sorry to have to refer to officials,
because since I came to this House I have
never anywhere received nicer or more
courteous treatment than I have from the
officials at Stormont. I have no hesitation
in saying that. I always appreciated
their courtesy, and I hope I showed
my appreciation by my thanks.
But I had occasion to write on the 4th
of this month to three or four Departments on the question of the "Ulster
Herald." There is one Department from
which I got neither an acknowledgment
nor a reply to my letter. I am a public
representative. I am not acting in this
matter on my own behalf; I am acting
on behalf of the public, and as their
representative I will bring under the
notice of any Department under this
Government any matter that I consider
it necessary to bring before them, and
any grievance I consider should be
attended to. In doing so I expect it will
at least receive attention, and if any
official of this Government thinks he is
going to treat me discourteously he is in
the
wrong shop. I am doing my best
on behalf of the people who sent me
here, and I expect to receive at least
common courtesy and attention from the
officials, who are well paid for dealing
with matters affecting the public interest,
not my own private interest.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
There is one thing at least in the
Gracious Speech which
is very encouraging. I am very pleased to see that
the unemployment figures have fallen,
because they have been very high for
such a small State. I am sure it was a
source of thankfulness to his Grace the
Governor that he was able to announce
such a substantial reduction in the number of unemployed. I only hope in the
course of a short time the figures will
be further reduced.
I am grateful to learn that our Government intends to follow the lead of the
Imperial Government as regards the
means test for unemployment assistance
and supplementary pensions. I do not
agree with my colleague, Senator Taylor,
when he advocates that the means test
should be abolished altogether, because
there are people in good circumstances
who are entitled to unemployment benefit
and assistance, as well as others who are
entitled to a pension, and such people have
no real need for supplementary pension.
Therefore, I think a means test is
necessary. At the same time, there are
some cruel regulations under the existing
means test. When a poor old man is
compelled to live with his son
daughter-in-law, it is hardly fair to expect
the son to contribute to the maintenance
of his father in addition to keeping his
own family.
and

created by Senator McLaughlin that
principally men of his religion are selected
by the labour exchanges to be sent to
work across the water. The labour exchanges know no politics or religion, or
they are not supposed to, and the officials

do not inquire about a man's political or

They are living in a country where they

so far, fortunately, we in this part of

from the war as have our fellow citizens
across the water. Therefore, our Roman

them.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Our complaint is

and the fact of

Mr. MCALLISTER: They have not

Mr. Speaker's Indisposition.

Question to Minister.
EXTENSION OF SUMMER TIME.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie: :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the proposed further extension
of summer time is expected to apply to
Northern Ireland, and, if so, whether
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
25 MARCH 1941
this House will have an opportunity of
discussing the proposal before it
becomes effective.
Mr. LESLIE: It was really rather
difficult for me to frame the question
which appears in my name on the Order
Paper. The difficulty arose, particularly,
I think, from one's complete state of
ignorance as to how far the proposal had
actually got, and whether the proposal,
if it was to become effective, was going
to be made under the
the Emergency
Provisions Act of the Parliament of the.
United Kingdom or not, and also there
was a doubt as to whether it was intended
that it should or should not apply to
Northern Ireland. The question, as the
House will recognise, is one of very considerable importance to Northern Ireland.
I must not allow myself to be drawn into
any attempt to debate it to-day, but it
must be recognised that there are various
arguments, the old argument of the
children going to sleep, and the more.
modern argument of dig for victory, with
special reference to allotments.
But those points, and various others at
issue, are all qualified and overriden by
the supreme consideration that this
country is very far west of Greenwich.
That is a fact we cannot get away from,
and although we understand that Joshua
got on very well without Defence
Regulations, we have not yet heard that
the Defence of the Realm Regulations
ever succeeded in conscripting Joshua.
All these things point in one direction,
and that is that before this proposal,
whatever it may be-and we have no
definite information yet as to what it is—
does become. effective it seems to me that
this House ought to have an opportunity
of considering it and debating it, as it is
a question specially affecting Northern
Ireland because it is so far west of
Greenwich.
an
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The Summer Time Act of 1922 was
Imperial Act applying to Northern Ireland, and the recent amendment of it by
Defence Regulations has also been
applied to Northern Ireland.
If any
further alterations are made in Great
Britain I presume they will be applied
here. at the same time, but it would also
be possible for representations to be made
to the Imperial authorities on the subject,
if Parliament so wishes.
Mr. STEVENSON: I think this question should be looked at entirely from a
national point of view. I am sure none
of us like the idea of getting up an hour
earlier in the morning, but if it helps
the Empire in the prosecution of this
awful war we should do everything to pull
our weight. If the Imperial Government.
wish us to accept this extension of summer
time we should all gladly do so.
Mr. MCALLISTER: As I understand
the reply of the Leader of the House, he
gave us an indication that if the Government of Northern Ireland have no power
to deal with this question, they have the
power to make representations to the
Imperial Parliament on the matter. I
think this Parliament should make such
representations, and the Government
should give this House an undertaking
that before they make representations to
the Imperial authorities they will obtain.
the views of this Parliament so that they
will be in a position to submit their representations as the views of the people of
this country. The Government cannot
claim to have a monopoly of the views of
the people of Northern Ireland or a monopoly of the opinions of the farming community, and therefore I suggest that it
is very necessary that the Government
should be informed of the feeling of the
farming community.
After all, farming is the basic industry
of Northern Ireland, and while I entirely
accept the view expressed by Senator
Stevenson, I do not think that question
arises. The question simply is, how can
food production in Northern Ireland be
best developed without in any way interfering with the war effort generally. We
all must recognise that what is suitable
for an industrial town in England respecting summer time is not suitable for an
agricultural community, and I suggest
that the Leader of the House should give
an undertaking that whatever representations are made to the Imperial Government, they should embody the views of
the people this Government represents,
and that those views will be ascertained
through this Parliament, so that the
collective opinion of all the Members can
be submitted in support of our representations.
MAJOR DOBBS: I would like to support what Senator McAllister has just
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
said, that representations on this subject.
should be made to the competent authorities, especially from the farming point of
view. There is one question we cannot
get over. You can make as many laws
as you like, but you cannot alter the
power of the sun.
In the ordinary way,
from the farming point of view, in the
most important months of July and
August, the dew will be off the ground
about 8-30 sun time. In England, with
summer time, that is 9-30, but we are
twenty-five minutes after that, and here
it is not 9-30 but 9-55, or say 10 o'clock.
You put on another hour for summer
time and it becomes 11 o'clock, and the
dew is not off the ground until 11 o'clock.
Last year we were very fortunate,
because we had good weather for the
harvest, but we could not cut our oats
until, very often, 10-30 in the morning
because the straw was saturated with dew.
Seeing is believing, and I said, "All
right, we are going to cut them early
to-day," but although we did start early
and did cut a certain amount of oats, we
had the greatest trouble in continually
tightening the platform canvasses, and
we also had trouble in cleaning the
rollers. To make a long story short, it
was hopeless. We were beaten by the dew,
by the sun. This next year, if we are
fortunate in getting our crops ripened, we
are going to start directly the sun will
allow us, and that is going to be about
11 o'clock or 12 o'clock. That does not
give us much time. You may say, "Why
not go on later?" We may go on later
and work on, but what you will find is
that the men, having started early with
some sort of work, other than cutting
wheat and oats and so on, which is very
important, they are going to be absolutely
tired out at the end of the day, and will
not be worth much the next day.
I do hope this question will be
thoroughly considered by the Leader of
the House and the Government and that
representations will be made to the
Imperial authorities. We cannot beat the
sun.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I should like the
Leader of the House to tell us whether,
when Bills affecting Northern Ireland are
passed or amended by the Imperial Government, the Government of Northern
Ireland is consulted beforehand. One
would expect that the Imperial Government would at least consider the views of
subordinate Parliaments such as this, and
ask for their opinion on any Bill or
Regulation affecting this country which it
was proposed to enact in the Imperial
Parliament, instead of ignoring them, as
they seem to have done up to the present.
I would like to know whether the
Imperial Government have ignored this
Parliament in applying the extension of
Summer Time to Northern Ireland.
Mr. LESLIE: Do I understand that
the Government is prepared to give an
opportunity to this House for making its
representations before the Order, if it is

that position, but he did not tell us
whther the Order had actually been made,

it is made.
Mr. ROBB: I think I stated quite
clearly that the Order has already been

I would like to point out, in reply to

House may be furnished with a copy of
the Order. I asked once before that we
should get these Orders. We have not
seen it. Can it be furnished to us?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Am I not entitled
to have an answer to my question as to
whether the Northern Government was
consulted before this Order was applied
to this country?
Mr. ROBB: I cannot give a direct

know what communications passed in the

Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." This
is quite a short Measure. In the original
Salaries of Ministerial Offices Bill, of
1921, no salary was provided in respect
even of the Minister of Commerce. This
Bill includes a salary for the Minister of
Commerce at the same rate as other
Ministers.
Mr. LESLIE: If I remember rightly,
the reason of the non-provision of a
salary for the Minister of Commerce was
because in 1921 the office of Minister of
Commerce was united with that of
Minister of Agriculture.
That was in
the original Bill, and it is just worth
pointing out that that was so. This Bill
deals with a matter that has since arisen,
and is quite unobjectionable.

Exchequer and Financial Provisions Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be, now read
a First time".-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time". This
Measure includes a number of provisions.
Clause 1 increases the Civil Contingencies
Fund by a sum of £250,000. This is a
fund which is kept available for occasional
sudden calls on the Exchequer in
anticipation of Votes of Parliament, and
it is thought advisable that at this particular time it should be further
increased.
The first part of Clause 2 arises in this
way. A large sum has been raised by
the. Defence Issue of Ulster Savings
Certificates, approximately £3,200,000 to
date, and this has been handed on to the
Imperial Government for defence
purposes. We get interest from the.
Imperial Government on the amount so
lent to them, and we have to pay it out.
But under the Exchequer and Financial
Provisions Act of 1933, provision was
made to set aside. annually to Sinking
Fund the amount required to meet the
estimated liability accruing in any year
in respect of the interest on Ulster
Savings Certificates outstanding, and if
we do not now include a provision on
the lines of 2 (1) of this Clause it would
require an unnecessarily large provision in
our Budget for the Sinking Fund
purposes. The object of this part of the
Clause is, therefore, to short-circuit the
interest from the Treasury direct to the
Sinking Fund instead of paying it into
our Exchequer and out again.
Sub-clause (2) is a really very technical
matter and is not of any great importance.
Sub-clause (3) merely changes words.
As these particular words stand at present
the moneys that are received can be
applied to meet the liability that is
estimated to accrue during the current
financial year. Each year a certain sum
may be set aside to meet interest. You
may estimate that you are going to have
to pay £5,000, and at the end of the year
you may have to pay £5,100. There is
no provision in the existing Statute. to
cover that extra £100, and this sub-clause.
merely alters the words to read, "to meet
the estimated liability accrued up to the
end of each financial year".
Clause 3 is merely a declaratory Clause
and makes it clear beyond doubt that the
Capital Fund is available as additional
security for Exchequer borrowings.
Clause 4 is to make provision for the
payment out of our Exchequer of whatever charges there may come on it under
the War Damage Act, and Clause 5 is the
Consolidated Fund Bill
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
Interpretation Clause. These are the provisions of the Bill, and I beg to move the
Second Reading.

On Question, Motion negatived.

Consolidated Fund Bill.

Question to Minister.
DISPOSAL OF FLAX WATER.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs :-
:-
To ask the Leader of the House.
whether he can state the chemical
analysis of flax water; whether flax
water is injurious to fish, cattle and
human beings; and if so, whether he
can state, what the ratio of river water
to flax water must be in order to render
the resulting solution innocuous.
Major DOBBS: We have been told in
the Gracious Speech from the Throne that
we shall be asked to consider an amendment to the Fisheries Act for the purpose
of amending the law relating to the discharge of flax water into rivers and lakes.
If this amendment is coming before us in
the near future I think it is imperative
that the Members of this House should
be furnished with all the data possible
about flax water and its qualities,
injurious and otherwise, especially the
injurious ones. At the same time I trust
that the Leader of the House will try out
all other methods of disposal of flax water
other than by just turning it down our
streams and rivers.
For instance there is the question of
pumping out the flax water on to the
neighbouring land If this is the method
it is decided to employ I trust that the
small pumping sets necessary will be
placed at the disposal of the farmers in
the various districts. But there is another
method that, it is quite possible, may
completely overcome this question of flax
water. I hope that people like the
Imperial Chemical Industries and other
bodies of expert scientists will be asked
for a chemical that will entirely neutralise.
the injurious effects of flax water. I am
sure it can be done. It may cost a great
deal to find this antidote, but when found
it would be very surprising if that
chemical, whatever it is, could not be
supplied at a very reasonable rate.
a
I have great confidence in it for several
reasons. One is that in this building some
little time ago the question of a local
water supply was being considered and
the expert stated that the Imperial
Chemical Industries
Industries could supply
chemical that would completely purify the
lake. from which the supply was to be
taken The trouble was that the lake
had excreta and impurities washing into it
from the road running adjacent to it. But
the chemical expert told that meeting that
there was no difficulty about it, and consequently I have the greatest confidence that
if research is taken to find that antidote
our expert research chemists will be able
to find the antidote.. I beg to ask the
question standing in my name.

highly technical, and to reply to them in
anything like detail would require something in the nature of a treatise.

find the matter fully dealt with in:—

Committee dated 18th October, 1923;
and

the Ministry of Agriculture for Northern Ireland entitled: "Investigations
during 1928 and 1929 into the effects
of Flax Water on Fish Life" carried
out by Messrs. J. C. Baird, B.Sc.,
B. Agr., A.I.C., and
W.
Duff,
B.Com.Sc., in 1932.

Personal Explanation.
Mr. ROBB: Before the Clerk proceeds
to read the Orders of the Day I have a
statement to make to the House which I
wish to make at this stage.
On Tuesday last in a discussion which
arose on a question by Senator Leslie I
stated that the Defence Regulations extending summer time to two hours during
the summer this year had already been
made, and I undertook to have that Order
circulated. In that statement I made a
mistake-whether I was personally responsible for that or not perhaps does not
concern this House; but I wish at once to
apologise to the House for having made it.
The facts are that the Summer Time
Act of 1922 passed at Westminster specifically applied in terms to Northern Ireland. That was a temporary Act. In
1924 our Parliament passed an Act providing that whatever was prescribed by
Act of Parliament or Order in Council for
Great Britain should automatically apply
in Northern Ireland.
Mr. MCALLISTER: For all time?
Mr. ROBB: For all time. In 1925
summer time for summer months was by
an Act of the Imperial Parliament made
permanent and by Order in Council, No.
1379 of 1939 and No. 1883 of 1940.
Summer time was prescribed for the
winter months as well. This last Order
was the recent Amendment referred to in
the written answer supplied to me, and I
took that to mean and I informed the
House it meant an Order increasing the
difference between solar time and Parliamentary time to two hours. In that I
was mistaken. The intention to make the
change had been announced, but the
Order, so far as we know, has not yet
been made. I again apologise for making
the mistake.
Another matter arose on the same
occasion, and on inquiry I found that no
consultation took place between the
Imperial authorities and this Government
before the intention to make the change
was announced.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not know whether
I ought to express thanks to the right
hon. Gentleman for his statement, and
perhaps sympathy also. It is clear that
no harm has been done, and the opportunity for discussion is still open.
Determination of Needs Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
by
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a second time.” As the
House is aware, our social legislation has
provided, in addition to the unemployment relief under the contract with the
State formed by contributions
employed persons and their employers,
commonly called the dole, and old age
´pensions, two forms of further relief for
poverty in the form of unemployed
assistance and supplementary pensions.
These forms of relief depend on an estimation of the needs of each applicant, a
person who can without assistance maintain a reasonable standard of living is not
given assistance or supplementary pension.
These are given to persons who fall below
this standard, and the amount of help
given depends on the degree of need.
By Section 33 of the Unemployment
Assistance Act of 1934, in the determination of the needs of applicants for relief,
the authority was bound to take into
account "the resources of all members of
the household of which the applicant is a
member." This provision was attacked
very strongly from the start; and it was
even alleged that its application resulted
in the break up of families. Sons or
daughters earning wages preferred to
leave home and live on their own rather
than by remaining at home prevent their
fathers or mothers getting the assistance
Determination of Needs Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
or increased pensions which their parents'
own means or lack of means would have
entitled them to.
The present Bill, which follows strictly
the measure just passed at Westminster,
modifies this provision, and it does so in
this way.
Clause 1 repeals the section of
the Act of 1934 under which the total
resources of a household are to be taken
into account, and by Clause 2 (1) the
Assistance Board are within one month to
submit to the Minister of Labour new
regulations for determining needs, and
those regulations are to provide for the
needs of an applicant who is a member of
a household being assessed in conformity
with the rules set out in the First
Schedule.
It will be noted that the Bill does not
itself make regulations-it only lays down.
in the schedule certain general principles
with which such regulations must comply
-and I may add that the regulations,
when made, must be laid before Parliament, and cease to have effect unless confirmed by Parliament within 12 months.
Turning to the Schedule it lays down.
three Rules. Rule 1 says:
Whether or not the applicant is the
householder, the resources of members
of the household, other than the
applicant, the husband or wife of the
applicant, and any member of the
household dependent on the applicant,
shall not be regarded as resources of
the applicant.
Rule 2 reads:
Where the applicant is the householder or the. husband or wife of the
householder the resources of
the
applicant shall, unless exceptional
circumstances are shown, be deemed to
include such contributions as may be
prescribed towards the expenses of the
household from members of the household who are not dependent members.
Rule 3 deals with cases where the
applicant is not the householder:
(a) if the householder is the father or
mother of the applicant and the
income of the father and mother
is of such amount as may be
prescribed, or if the householder is
the son or daughter of the applicant
and the income of the householder
is of such amount as may be
prescribed, and if in either case
there are no members of the household dependent on the applicant,
the needs of the applicant shall,
unless exceptional circumstances
are shown, be deemed not to
include the need of making contributions towards the cost of his
board and lodging or the other
expenses of the household;
(b) if the provisions of the last foregoing sub-paragraph are inapplicable, then, unless the applicant has
not attained the age of eighteen
years the needs of the applicant
shall, unless exceptional circumstances are shown, be deemed to
include the need of making such
contributions by way of rent as
may be prescribed.
To this extent then the household means
test is modified.
The Bill deals with one other matter.
To encourage savings and their investment in the Government's war schemes it
provides that investments in war savings
to the extent of £375 shall not be taken
into account. The figure £375 represents
savings certificates for £500, and I may
say that where there are three people in a
house each may hold savings certificates to
the amount of £375. If a man is earning
high wages and he saves sufficient to buy
500 certificates for himself, 500 for his
wife, and 500 for his child, these will not
be taken into account if the man makes
a claim for a pension after the war when
he
may
be in need of it.
A Bill
There is this limitation, that where the
war savings were bought with the proceeds of the sale of other investments
made after the 14th August, 1940, the
claim shall not apply. The intention is
to exempt new money and not the reinvestment of old money. The date, 14th
August, was fixed in this way.
was introduced in the Imperial Parliament dealing with this matter, and it was
suggested that war savings certificates
bought in September, 1939, should be
taken into account. It was pointed out
that a lot of people had sold their investments before that date and put them into
war savings certificates on the understanding, so they thought, that they
I would be taken into account. That Bill
was withdrawn and a new one introduced
fixing this new date of 14th August, and
61 Determination of Needs Bill

27 MARCH 1941
Adjournment
it is only after that date that the reinvestment of money is excluded.
Clause 6 provides for the closing of the
Unemployment Assistance Fund. Originally it was contemplated that there
would be contributions from the rates
paid into the Unemployment Assistance
Fund, and that out of that grants would
be made. The provision with regard to
the contribution from rates is wiped out,
and now the only thing that goes into
the fund is the money voted by
Parliament.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to move,
"That the debate be adjourned until
Tuesday next." I do this in view of the
short time we have had to consider the
Bill, and particularly the White Paper
explaining its provisions. Is it the
intention to take the Committee stage on
Tuesday?
Mr. ROBB: No.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Question to Minister
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 1st April, 1941.
The House met at a Quarter past Three

Question to Minister.
BLACK-OUT RESTRICTIONS IN
COUNTRY DISTRICTS.
By
Major DOBBS: My question may be
rather crude, but though it is crude it is
sincere. Personally I do not think that
the black-out restrictions in the country
districts
are properly observed.
country districts I mean districts outside
Belfast. Now, who are the offenders?
First of all there is the bungalow with no
blinds on the window. There is the house
that is excellently blacked-out in front,
towards the road, but shows a light out
on to the fields at the back. Then again
there is the garage, probably suddenly
called upon to do a small job. It is a
blaze of light, although that may be only
for a few minutes. These are the
offenders. Who is responsible? Naturally, the man who allows that beam of
light to be shown from his house, also the
member of the L.D.V.s who does not
report that beam, and then the man in
authority, or perhaps on the bench, to
whom that beam is reported but who
simply regards it as a mote and not as a
beam.
Really we are all rather responsible. I do
not think we take this matter sufficiently
seriously. Twenty-eight thousand deaths.
and 40,000 wounded in England in air
raids! We must be very thankful that
we have had no casualties here, because
we have had no air raids, but though we
have not had air raids, we must not think
we are immune. We have not had them
because the Germans have not thought it
worth their while to bomb us. But I
think we must expect air raids over
Northern Ireland when the weather gets
better, and especially as a preliminary to
any invasion of these islands. I beg to
ask the Leader of the House whether any
steps are being taken to see that the
black-out restrictions are being observed
in country districts.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): The
answer is in the affirmative. All country
police stations maintain a night patrol
which covers every part of the police district at irregular but frequent intervals;
and special attention is paid by these
patrols to the observance of black-out
restrictions. In addition, the "B" Class
Ulster Special Constabulary is at present
in the patrol category, and as a consequence the members of this force engage
in regular patrols in country districts
throughout Northern Ireland.
During 1940, 15,589 convictions for
breaches of the Lighting Restrictions
Order were obtained in Northern Ireland,
of which 10,468 were in respect of
offences occurring outside the city of
Belfast.
Major DOBBS: Arising out of that
answer, I would like to ask whether it is
not a fact that these patrols generally
go out after or about half-past eleven at
night, and that these breaches of the
black-out restrictions are very bad up to
that time, say about nine o'clock, ten
o'clock, and up to eleven o'clock, which
is definitely, to my mind, a very serious
time. Perhaps the Leader of the House
will put that forward to the authorities.
Mr. ROBB: I have no personal knowledge as to when these patrols go out.
Mr. BARCLAY: As far as the houses
are concerned in the country districts,
from what I know most country people
are in bed about ten o'clock, and there
cannot be very much light in the country.
I think the greatest sinners, as far as
light is concerned, are the military themselves. I was coming along last Sunday
evening and passed a military camp. All
the windows were lit up and the boys
were enjoying themselves inside. What
can we do about that?
ORDER OF THE DAY.
Determination of Needs Bill.
Order read for resuming adjourned
debate on Question (27th March), “That
the Bill be now read a Second time."-
(Mr. Robb.)
Question again proposed.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I must confess
that this Bill is a step in the right direction, and a great many amendments
Determination of Needs Bill-
Second Reading
1 APRIL 1941
could be added to it to improve it still
more. However, in the debate in another
place special reference was made to blind
persons. I think the Government would
have been well advised if they had taken
out of the hands of the county councils
the pensions for the blind, because I have
experience of county councils for a good
many years, and I am quite satisfied the
county councils have not done justice to
blind persons in the past. I am further
satisfied that, even with the greatest
pressure brought by the Government,
they will not do justice to the blind persons until they are obliged to do it. The
Government would have been well advised
if they had taken out of the hands of the
county councils altogether pensions for
blind people.
Everyone must admit that it is an
injustice to a man who is physically unfit
to see, that because of his blindness he is
obliged to go before the local council and
practically divulge everything connected.
with his household, his means, and everything else, and at the end he is told he
will not get the full supplementary
pension. I think as far as Armagh
County Council are concerned the most
they give there to the most deserving case
is 5s as supplementary pension. In that
case the blind person is suffering no less
than a loss of 4s a week by the very
reason of his blindness.
I would like to put this point to the
Government in view of the way the
county councils have treated the blind
persons in connection with pensions since
the inception of the Act. Despite the
many advices and protests they get from
the Government, it had to be admitted
in another place that the county councils
were not doing their duty towards blind
persons, and from their experience of the
county councils in looking after the blind
people, the Government will admit that
they would never dream of placing old.
age pensions under the control of the
county councils. That being so, I trust
at some future date the Government will
see their way to bringing in an amending
Bill taking the disbursement of pensions
to the blind out of the hands of the
county councils.
Furthermore, I am sorry that as far
as widows and orphans are concerned
there has been no provision made for
them in this Bill. They are suffering
just as much as the contributory pensioners. Their children are suffering
from the point of view of the cost of
living, and if the wage-earner is gone,
surely the widow and the orphan of the
wage-earner should be looked after and
should at least get a pension on which
they will be able to live.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I think the
majority of people agree that it is
humiliating and degrading for poor
persons to have to undergo a means test
of any description. So far as it alleviates
that position I cannot oppose this Bill.
I would like to thank the Leader of the
House for the very skilful way he opened
the debate on this very complicated Bill
without involving us in complex details.
In my opinion, these social services have
become the most complex code on our
Statute Book, and I shall be surprised if
the Leader of the House does not agree
with me on that point. I mention that
for this reason. I cannot for the life of
me understand why this Assistance Board
is being set up to advise the Minister of
Labour. It is my experience that all this
quasi-administration, boards, and committees have not the independence and
the moral courage of either the courts or
the Civil Service.
After fifteen or sixteen years close contact with the Ministry of Labour, which
adminsters these social services, I feel
bound to say that I have always found
that, following the example of the
Minister himself, the civil servants of that
Department are most human, most
courteous, and most anxious to meet every
grievance and to remedy every injustice
that is brought to their notice. After
fifteen or sixteen years experience, I
gladly pay that tribute to the civil servants
of the Ministry of Labour, and indeed to
all the Ulster civil servants.
That being so, why should these people,
whoever they may be-for I do not know
one of them-who comprise this
Assistance Board, be selected from the
people of Northern Ireland to advise the
skilled officials on the administration of
the Act and on the drawing up of Regulations under the Act. Notwithstanding
all his ability, the Leader of the House
will have difficulty in elucidating to this
House the reason why this Board has
been set up.
I would like him to tell us

Questions to Minister.
BELFAST DIVISIONAL FOOD
INSPECTOR.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether the Government recommended to the Imperial
Government the appointment of James
Gordon as Divisional Food Inspector in
Belfast; whether any inquiry was made
into his character and fitness for such a
post; whether he has seen a report of a
prosecution on 18th December last of a
butcher named Alexander McCaughey for
illegally selling a pound of steak without
a coupon, when Gordon admitted in crossexamination that he had once been a rival
butcher to McCaughey, but failed, and
made a composition with his creditors,
paying 8s in the £; whether Gordon sent
a lady to trap traders into committing a
breach of the Food Control Acts, and
whether he can state what political
organisation or Member of Parliament
recommended Gordon for this responsible
post.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
Mr. Gordon was one of a number of
Divisional Inspectors appointed by the
Ministry of Food after selection by a
Board set up by the Ministry of Finance.
By administrative arrangement between
the Government of Northern Ireland and
the Imperial Government, the machinery
of recruitment of the Government of
Northern Ireland is used for the selection
of officers for service with local branches
of Imperial Departments. The responsibility for the appointment rests with the
Imperial Department concerned.
As regards character, I am informed
that the usual inquiries were made in this
case, and that, after obtaining a full
explanation of the circumstances in which
the family business made a composition.
with its creditors, it was considered that
there was no valid reason why Mr.
Gordon should not be employed in
the post.
No recommendation in Mr.
Gordon's favour was received either from
a political organisation or from any
Member of Parliament.
The enforcement of the Food Orders
and the methods used are matters for the
Ministry of Food. I am told that in the
case cited by Senator McLaughlin, the
inspector acted wholly within his instructions, and the defendant was convicted.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I would like to
ask if this Department of the Ministry
of Food was aware of the fact that this
particular inspector had prosecuted a
business man who was in the same type of
business as the inspector himself was
formerly engaged in.
Mr. ROBB: Certainly. Surely you
would set a thief to catch a thief, and a
butcher to catch a butcher.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: You would set
a rival to catch a rival. This is the kind
of gentleman you appoint as a food inspector. Here is an extract from the
cross-examination of the food inspector
in this case:-
You were in the same line of business
as Mr. Caughey?—Yes.
You have dropped that business?-
Yes.
And you got off by paying less than
you were entitled to?-That does not
affect the case.
You were a successful butcher and
rival in business of the defendant, and
you paid 8s in the pound?
Gordon-I refuse to answer.
The Resident Magistrate made him
That is the type of man who is
appointed a food inspector.
answer.
Mr. ROBB: I have read the account
given by Mr. Gordon of the circumstances
under which his business failed. It was
a
family business inherited by the
family. The family fell out, and the
business had to be wound up. I am prepared to let Senator McLaughlin read the
account on the file touching the winding
up of that business, and if he is not satisfied, I invite him to move a Motion in this
House.

urgency to engage suitably-qualified men

The following question stood on the
Order Paper in
in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government are prepared to
make representations to the British.
Government on the importance of the full
feeding ration for poultry in view of the
fact that the egg producing season has
now arrived; that an extra ration should
be granted up to 30th June next; whether
he has considered the position of farmers
and farmers' wives who were not aware
that they had to apply for ration cards
for their poultry, and when they did
apply they were told by the Feeding
Stuffs Supply Department of the Ministry
of Agriculture that they had not applied
in time, and would get no ration
allowance, and whether, as a result, in
many cases the poultry flock had to be
destroyed, thus curtailing very seriously
the production of eggs.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In asking this
question, I would like to pay a tribute to
the civil servants of this Government for
their efficiency and courtesy. I venture
to say they are second to none, but I
cannot say that with regard to the new
Departments set up under this Government. I raised this matter before, and I
hope the Gentlemen sitting on the bench
over there will listen to me.
As I say,
I raised the matter of a certain Department I had written some letters to and
from whom I got no reply. It would be
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister

THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
well just to mention that afterwards I got
a letter from one of the members of that
Department on House of Commons notepaper. It was not even stated at the top
from what Department it came, and I will
offer a wager to anyone who could
decipher the signature of the gentleman.
I cannot.
I refer particularly to this as far as
poultry are concerned. It will be agreed
that at the present time egg production is
a very necessary industry, and a very
valuable one, too. I think anyone engaged
in the poultry business and in egg production should be encouraged, but I must
confess that we are not getting very much
encouragement from the Ministry of Food
or Supply Department. I was in the
country a few days ago, and a countrywoman was complaining of not having
received a ration card for her fowl. I
asked her why, and she said she did not
know that she had to apply for rations.
She had never heard of it until someone
mentioned it to her. She had gone to her
grocer's and was not able to get the foodstuffs. She wrote to the Department
asking for a ration card and got a curt
reply dated February-it did not give the
day-telling her that she had not applied
before, and the Department could not
consider her application.
I
Perhaps it will not be infringing too
much on the courtesy of the House if I
give my own personal experience. I sent
in the usual way for a ration card, and
after a while I got one for twelve head
of fowl. I wrote pointing out that was
not correct, and that on my return to the
police concerned it was considerably more,
at least two and a half times more.
wish it to be understood as far as the
officer and police in my district are concerned, we have, I am very happy to be
able to state, nothing but the highest
praise for them. They are competent and
do their duties courteously and efficiently,
but we do know isolated cases in the
country where the police, instead of
paying a visit to the house, put down on
paper in some instances what fowl they
consider might be there.
In my case I wrote to the Department
concerned, and got a reply suggesting
that I should get a letter from the constable concerned verifying my figures. I
got a letter from that constable verifying
them. Then I got a reply in Februarythe day not mentioned-telling me the
information was incorrect and that no
alteration could be made in the number
of livestock unless I could satisfy them
that the police returns were incorrect. I
explained that, and asked for a reply.
I got no reply. They had not even the
courtesy to acknowledge my letter. I sent
two letters, and they had not the courtesy
to reply, as is the usual custom in other
offices under the Government. Eventually
I got a letter dated the 1st April, which
was posted on the 2nd April and delivered
on 3rd April, again telling me that my
figures were not according to facts.
I wish to say that I dispute those facts
-I say they are incorrect. I go further
and say if the Government are not satisfied they should order an investigation
into it. After all is said and done the
public have a right to be considered. If
it were not for the public, farmers and
poultry producers, there would be no call
for this feeding-stuffs department, and
these gentlemen who are treating the
public with such discourtesy would not be
in the positions they are in. However, I
will go no further except to say that I
dispute entirely the figures concerned in
this case.
I suggest to the Department
concerned that in the case of that poor
woman who, they stated, had not applied
for her ration card in time, they should
take the census returns of June, 1940,
and see how many head of poultry she
had. If they are so particular about the
1940 returns why do they not look them
up? These people cannot have it both
ways. We are prepared to stand by the
census returns. When a request comes
from an ordinary individual, no matter
how humble, he or she is entitled to a
reply. I consider that if the 1940 census
is taken at all it should be taken in the
case of that poor woman in the country.
If you treat one in a particular way you
are entitled to treat them all alike. There
is that woman in the country and she
never was without fowl, and it was suggested that it was only recently that she
started to keep them. Where are the
census returns? Stick to the position and
treat everyone fairly as he should be
treated.
If the Ministry concerned are not satisfied with the facts I have given them I
am anxious that the whole position should
be investigated. I am prepared to sub-

Mr. ROBB: Perhaps after that lengthy
speech I may be allowed to read the
question I am asked:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government are prepared
to make representations to the British
Government on the importance of the
full feeding ration for poultry, in view
of the fact that the egg-producing
season has now arrived; that an extra
ration should be granted up to 30th
June next; whether he has considered
the position of farmers and farmers'
wives who were not aware that they had
to apply for ration cards for their
poultry, and when they did apply they
were told by the Feeding Stuffs Supply
Department of the Ministry of Agriculture that they had not applied in time,
and would get no ration allowance, and
whether as a result in many cases the
poultry flock had to be destroyed, thus
curtailing very seriously the production
of eggs.
We have had a relatively lengthy
speech from the hon. Senator dealing
with a number of matters-he alleges they
are facts-of which I have had no notice
and to which, of course, at the moment I
cannot be expected to reply. What he
says may be quite true, but, on the other
hand, there may be another version of it.
Now, to reply to the question I am asked.
The fullest possible consideration has
been given by the three Agricultural
Departments in the United Kingdom and
the Ministry of Food to the position of
both the pig and poultry industries. I
can hold out not the slightest hope of any
improvement in the supply of imported
feeding stuffs to this or any other class of
stock. As the hon. Senator is doubtless
aware, a further reduction of 50 per cent.
in the ration of imported feeding stuffs
to pigs and poultry has had to be made.
this month throughout the United Kingdom because of the supply position.
Every farmer in the country was warned.
in the autumn of 1939 by a personal
letter from the Minister of Agriculture
as to what lay ahead, and in December,
1940, all farmers were told, by means of
a further personal letter from the
Minister, that they must face the fact
that they would have to maintain their
pigs and poultry on the produce of their
own land.
As regards the second part of the hon.
Senator's question-that is with regard
to notice I cannot agree that farmers
were not aware that they had to apply
for ration cards for their poultry. The
Ministry of Agriculture conveyed this
information to poultry owners by advertisement in all Northern Ireland papers,
country as well as town-
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: All?
Mr. ROBB-and on the wireless on
no less than four occasions since November
last. On each of these occasions the
latest date of application was extended,
but notwithstanding this, ration books
have now been supplied to all persons who
made application up to the 14th March.
It is not possible, in the light of the
supply position, to entertain any applications made after that date, but it may be
possible to do so when the second ration,
ing period commences about the middle of
May. I am not aware that poultry flocks
have had to be destroyed, but if that is
the case, the blame must rest upon those
who failed to comply with the Ministry's
instructions.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am thankful
to the learned Leader of the House for
his reply. He states this was published
in all the Press?
Mr. ROBB: So I am informed.
Mr. SPEAKER: Is the hon. Member
asking a supplementary question ?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Yes. If that is
so I am quite satisfied. This woman
reads the papers, and she did not see it.
If the Department concerned had been
frank with regard to the situation in
accordance with the reply of the learned
Leader of the House this trouble would
not have arisen. There is no use in going
round matters. All they had to do was
to say they could not manage the extra
ration. That would have obviated a lot
of trouble.

Treatment of Flax Water
Royal Assent
8 APRIL 1941
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 8th April, 1941.
The House met at Ten Minutes past
Twelve of the Clock noon,
Mr.
SPEAKER in the Chair.
Papers Presented.
Ministry of Finance:
New Industries (Development) Act,
1937. Statement of Loans made by the
Ministry of Finance up to 31st March,
1941.
Ministry of Labour:
Unemployment Assistance (Prevention
and Relief of Distress) (Refugees) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1941.
Unemployment Fund of Northern Ireland. Accounts for the year 1st April,
1939, to 31st March, 1940.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act), and ordered to lie on the Table.
Question to Minister.
TREATMENT OF FLAX WATER.
Major DOBBS: May I remind the
House that a fortnight ago I asked a
question with regard to the chemical constituents of flax water. The Leader of
the House referred me to two documents,
and said I would find them highly technical. Well, I found them highly
technical, as he said, but I also found
them extremely interesting as far as they
went.
The first dealt practically entirely with catch dams, and the second
with field and laboratory experiments with
fish in flax water and the dilution of flax
water. But I think we ought, in trying
to get at this question of rendering flax
water innocuous, to dig very much deeper.
If one suffers from rupture or varicose
veins, one goes to a surgeon and the surgeon says: "You can have treatment."
That is only a partial cure. He will
probably strongly advise an operation for
a complete cure. As far as catch dams
are concerned we are giving treatment,
but if we can get a chemical that will
neutralise the toxic qualities of flax water,
then we have got a complete cure.
I am sure our scientists could find a
chemical that would do this. Science has
advanced enormously in the last few
First of
years.
I will take three cases.
all there is the case of the searchlight out
in the desert, hundreds of miles from any
human being. It starts when night falls
and stops when the sun rises. It is done
by means of solenium cells. Then we
have the case of the astonishing effects of
the new explosive for our bombs that our
scientists have given us. That explosive
has a disruptive effect five times as powerful as any bomb that has been used up
to now. In the field of surgery and
medicine the discovery of sulphanilamides
has completely revolutionised the treatment of war wounds and of acute
infection. Having these astonishing discoveries of science, I cannot see why, if
the question is put to our scientists, a
chemical should not be found that will
render flax water completely harmless to
fish, cattle, and to human beings. I beg

: Delays in Postal Censorship.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to move,
That in the opinion of this House the
Government should make representations
to the Imperial Government to ensure
that in future steps are taken to avoid
delays similar to those which have
occurred in the Postal Censorship Department." I move this motion for various
reasons, generally with regard to the
great delay in postage between the Six
Counties and Britian due to the censorship, and in particular I want to bring
to the notice of the public the holding up
by the postal censors of the Lenten
pastoral of his Eminence Cardinal
MacRory from the 19th February until
3rd March, and the disappointment
and inconvenience caused to his clergy
and subjects in that part of the Archdiocese in Eire-Louth and Meath.
On 19th February last, Messrs.
Bennett, agents for his Eminence in
Armagh, sent through the post papers and
the pastoral in the usual way to arrive in
Eire on the 20th. On the morning of
Saturday, 22nd February, his Eminence
got a 'phone call from his administrator
in Dundalk asking about his pastoral,
saying it had not reached Dundalk or any
of the priests in Eire. His Eminence at
once 'phoned the office of his agents. The
agents 'phoned Belfast, and were informed that the pastoral was there and
held up, that the chief had just gone out,
but they would try to get in touch with
him. Here is the correspondence that
passed between the censor and his
Eminence's agents, Messrs. Bennett,
printers, Armagh. On 16th February
they wrote to the censor office as follows:
Dear sir,-On Wednesday, 19th
instant, at 5-45 p.m., we handed in to
Armagh G.P.O.--
Mr. ROBB: What is the date?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: 26th February.
We handed in to Armagh G.P.O. a
parcel cotaining a number of printed.
booklets addressed to all the Catholic
clergy throughout County Louth.
The booklet, the content of which was
the Lenten pastoral of his Eminence
Cardinal Mac Rory, Archbishop of
Armagh and Primate of All Ireland,
to be read last Sunday, 23rd
was
February, in all the Catholic churches
of the Archdiocese of Armagh, of which
County Louth is a part.
On being informed on Saturday evening last, 22nd instant, that the clergy
in County Louth had not up to then
received the pastoral, the writer got
into telephone communication with the
censor office in Belfast and was informed later that evening by an official
that the packets were still at the censor
office in Belfast. I pointed out to the
officials concerned that the packets
were at the censor office from the Wednesday night or Thursday morning and
that the Cardinal's pastoral was of very
great importance and should have been
delivered to the addressees that
Saturday.
I have been informed this evening
that the pastorals have not yet been
received by the clergy concerned in
County Louth.
Will you please let me know, per
return, the cause of the great delay in
holding up the packets, and when they
are to be released from the Belfast
Censor Office.
Yours faithfully,
(Signed) OWEN J. BENNETT.
P.S.-Enclosed in the parcel was the
necessary censor certificates containing
lists of names and addresses of the
addressees, and each packet was marked
with our permit number and stamped.
The following reply was received ten
days afterwards:
I.P.C. 339.
Ministry of Information,
Permit Branch,
13 Donegall Place,
Belfast.
3rd March, 1941.
Gentlemen,-Referring to despatches
of "Lenten Pastoral" received at this
branch, these packets have now been
released by censor and despatched to
destinations.
I am, gentlemen,
Your obedient servant,
(Signed) OFFICER IN CHARGE.
On 4th March Messrs. Bennett wrote
the Censor's office as follow:
Dear sir,-With further reference to
our letter of the 26th ult., and your

8 APRIL 1941
Delays in Postal Censorship
reply of 3rd inst. (your reference
I.P.C. 339), informing us that the
"'Lenten Pastorals' have been released
by Censor and despatched to destinations," we would be obliged for an
explanation which we can submit to his
Eminence Cardinal MacRory re the
holding up of these packets at the
Permit Branch, Belfast, from the 19th

A reply by return is requested.
On the 5th March the Ministry of
Information at Belfast replied:
Gentlemen,-I am in receipt of your
letter dated 4th inst., and would inform
you that the matter referred to therein
is receiving attention.
OFFICER IN CHARGE.
On 15th March Messrs. Bennett again
wrote:
Dear sir,-We will be obliged for a
reply to our letter of the 4th inst. (your
acknowledgment of 5th inst., reference
I.P.C. 339).
The matter is urgent.
From that day to this there has not
been a single word from the Permit
Officer at the Ministry of Information in
Belfast. There is the pastoral. It contains nothing that any reasonable censor
could find fault with. In it he exhorts
his subjects, amongst other things, to
pray for peace, to help in the production
of food, to grow more wheat, oats,
potatoes, etc. He also condemns the
action of the Six County Governmentand perhaps this is the snag-when he
states:
"Here in the Six Counties, where I
write, we have at the moment what I
consider a great grievance. Numbers
of boys and men, even fathers of young
families, are being driven out of the
country, under pain of starvation, to
labour and risk their lives in Great
Britain. I doubt whether the banishment of a man from his own country
in such cruel circumstances is law; it
certainly seems not to be justice; yet I
regret to say that large numbers,
especially of our Catholic young men,
have been condemned to it, and are
now in Great Britain risking their lives
day and night.”
Is it that the Government here were
anxious to suppress this, and was the holding up of the pastoral by the postal censors in keeping with the statement of Lord
Halifax, British Ambassador to America,
when, at the Pilgrims Dinner in New
York on the 25th March last, he declared
that part of the British war aims were:
"The right to think, speak, and act
freely within the law and to have free
access to the thoughts of others, the
right of free association, both national
and international, with their fellowmen,
the right to live without fear of
aggression, injustice or want, the right
to believe and worship as conscience
may dictate."
The clergy of Eire belonging to the
Archdiocese of Armagh, were deprived
on the 1st and 2nd Sunday of Lent,
owing to the censor's action, of the
pastoral. But the Catholic Church being
used to persecution, the clergy found a
way out and the pastoral was read in full
from the newspapers on the altars on the
2nd Sunday of Lent. The action of the
censor was, to say the least, most unreasonable and stupid as well. Why?
Because on Monday, 24th February,
when the censor still held up the pastoral,
it appeared in full in all the newspapers
of Ireland, North and South. What his
reason for the hold up was is to me a
mystery.
Moreover, take the correspondence.
The censor has been asked for an explanation, and so far he has refused to give
one and has simply ignored all letters. Is
that a nice way to treat the pastoral of
the Cardinal Primate of All Ireland?
Irish Catholics may be, and are, divided
on political principles, but speaking as a
Catholic and a public representative on
their behalf, I state emphatically that the
solidarity and unity of the Irish Catholic
people, both at home
home and abroad,
behind the leadership of their illustrious
Cardinal Primate of All Ireland, their
hierarchy and clergy, are as solid as a
rock and cannot be broken. They have
but to command and we, their loyal and
dutiful subjects, obey.
Lastly, let me say that should there be
any attempt, either by the Government
or their censors, to interfere in any way
between them and their subjects, it will
not only be strongly resented but resisted
by every legitimate means at our disposal.
It is significant that in such an important
service as the Post Office our Government

I am informed that the average timeand this is all I am prepared to deal with;
I am not prepared to deal with a particular case-between the posting and
delivery of letters between Great Britain
and Northern Ireland, including censorship, is three days. Letters are cleared
by the Postal Department in Belfast
within two or three hours, and particular
letters are only held up if it is considered
that particular inquiries should be made
into them for censorship purposes. On
the whole, the arrangements have been
working satisfactorily, and generally
speaking, I do not think the general
public is dissatisfied with the amount of
the delay. As a specific case has been
brought to my notice I will undertake,
on behalf of the Government, to see that
it is brought before the proper authorities.

Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): In
connection with the publication of
Government advertisements, I may state
that:
(a) the practice as to the precise
amount of publicity to be given to particular advertisements is based on a
desire of the Government to divide the
advertisements among as many newspapers as possible, and the primary
selection is made from a roster of
the
newspapers which is kept in
Ministry of Finance;
(b) consideration is also given to the
views of the Ministry whose advertisement is being placed as to the area to
be covered and the no less important
consideration of the circulation of the
newspapers concerned;
(c) the ordinary commercial consideration of value for money has also
to be borne in mind.
It follows from this that the newspapers with the largest circulation receive
a proportionately greater share of the
advertisements. The Ministry does not
give weight to political considerations.
The advertisements referred to by the
hon. Senator were, at the request of the
Ministry of Agriculture, inserted in two
provincial papers circulating in each
county, and in accordance with the roster
the advertisements automatically fell to
be inserted in the following newspapers:

Constitution," "Mid Ulster Mail,"

DELIVERY OF FLAX SEED
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can explain the cause of delay in delivery of
sowing flax seeds by the Ministry of Agriculture to seed merchants; whether he can
state when this seed will be available in
ample quantities and in the hands of seed
merchants so that farmers will have it in
good time for sowing; and whether he is
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
aware that sowing time in most districts
is now very far advanced and almost over.
There seems to have been great delay
and some mis-handling so far as this flax
seed is concerned. I know that a very
large number of farmers, particularly in
County Armagh, had their ground ready
for sowing flax, and when they were not
able to get the seed they were obliged to
Sow oats. There is something wrong so
far as the delivery of flax seed is concerned. I made inquiries and I found
there is something wrong so far as the
cleaning of the seed is concerned.
It
seems that they were not able to get the
seed cleaned in time for early delivery.
Whether it is cleaned in Northern Ireland or in England I do not know, but
I suggest that Northern Ireland is the
proper place in which to clean this seed,
and if it is not cleaned here it should be,
and I hope it will be in future.
With regard to the delivery of flax
seed, here is a letter, dated 3rd May, from
a wholesale firm :-
"There is no news yet about English
seed that is supposed to be on its way
to us. We are thousands of bags short
of the allocation which the Government
made to us. You will therefore understand that it is impossible for us to
complete orders under such circumstances. We hope to have some news
next week about English seed, but we
have a big lot of customers who have
not got more than 50 per cent. of their
seed yet."
That was the position on 3rd May, and
yet we were told by the. Government that
they had ample supplies of flax seed. The
farmers accordingly made arrangements
on their land, but there was no seed and
they had to sow oats instead.
Mr. ROBB: The answer to the first
part of the question is that there has been
no delay on the part of the Ministry of
Agriculture. in delivering flax seed to
seed merchants in Northern Ireland as
the seed was distributed immediately it
was available.
Supplies of flax seed for sowing in
Northern Ireland were obtained from
three sources -Canada, Great Britain,
and Northern Ireland. From Canada and
Northern Ireland the estimated supplies
were up to expectations. In the case of
Great Britain, the Ministry of Supply
promised a total of 1,738 tons of flax
seed in ample time for sowing in Northern
Ireland, but in the middle of March, this
figure was reduced to 1,388 tons. Owing
the
to a variety of causes, for which
Ministry of Agriculture was not responsible, the quantity of seed received from
Great Britain up to 30th April amounted
to only 1,223 tons.
Owing to an embargo on shipments
from Great Britain, there was a period
towards the end of April and the
beginning of May when no supplies
reached Northern Ireland, but as a result
of strong representations made by the
Ministry of Agriculture, deliveries of flax
seed were resumed. It is appreciated
that, owing to the late arrival of seed
from Great Britain difficulties have been
caused in early districts but, generally
speaking, the Ministry has been able. to
meet the demand for seed. I believe that
flax is normally sown some time before
the end of this month.
With regard to the cleaning of the
seed, I had not any notice of that question.
Sir EMERSON HERDMAN: It is not
often that I am in agreement with
Senator McLaughlin, but I think it is a
very good thing for him to ventilate this
question of the. want of flax seed. As far
as I understand the position, I do not
think it is so much a question of a
shortage of flax seed as a want of knowledge of the early places in Ulster in
which it can be sown. In my own part
of the country, which is a very early part,
the seed was there but it did not come
in time and the farmers, willy nilly, had
to give up the idea of sowing flax and
sow corn. In future districts which begin
early should receive priority in getting
flax seed and then such a mistake as
occurred this year would not happen. We
all live and learn, and that has really a
good deal to do with the unfortunate
position that arose in places like Armagh
and Tyrone which wanted to grow flax
but were not able to do so on account of
not being able to get the seed.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I understand
there was an ample quantity of seed available, but owing to their experience of it
the farmers were not anxious to have
Canadian seed. It would have been a
good thing if the Ministry of Agriculture,
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
27 MAY 1941
when they found that their allotment of
English seed was not available, had made
the farmers of Northern Ireland aware of
that fact instead of keeping them waiting
until the English seed arrived. I think
I am right in stating that as far as the
last consignment from England is concerned, it got here too late, and a great
deal of it was left in the hands of the

Mr. ROBB: I am informed that seed

Mr. STEVENSON: I agree with

two months ago I wrote to the Minister of

to be a shortage of flax seed, and pointing
out means by which that shortage could
be overcome.
The Minister-I presume
on the advice of his staff-thanked me and
assured me there would be ample seed. If
the measures I suggested then had been
taken I am perfectly sure there would not
have been any shortage.

CIGARETTE SUPPLIES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Joseph
Cunningham:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the Government has been notified of the release of
an increased amount of tobacco for the
manufacture and sale of cigarettes;
and, if so, whether they will endeavour
to secure a fair proportion of this
release to increase the supply for
workers in Northern Ireland.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: This
question explains itself. Its object is to
draw the attention of the Government to
the present shortage of cigarettes, and to
solicit their sympathetic consideration of
the position. In Northern Ireland the
shortage of cigarettes for working-class
people has been very bad, and I have been
very sorry for men who were going to and
returning from work calling at shops
trying to obtain what they call their
"fags." Everyone knows that if it is
not possible to get a smoke one develops.
a craving and longing for it. It is the
same with these men who are employed in
the shipyards and in A.R.P. and
Auxiliary Fire Service work. They must
feel very uncomfortable when it is impossible to obtain cigarettes.
