Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Superannuation (Teaching
Services) Bill and Superannuation (War
Service) Bill when they have been brought
from the House of Commons."—(Mr.
Robb.)
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Superannuation (Teaching Services) Bill, and
the Superannuation (War Service) Bill,
to which they desire the concurrence of
this House.
Superannuation (Teaching Services)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: This Measure can be
briefly explained. Certain Teachers'
Superannuation (Amendment) Schemes
were framed in the year 1936, to provide
for the payment of a lump sum benefit to
teachers on retirement, to be calculated
on the basis of one-thirtieth of the annual
salary for each year of service after 1st
July, 1936.
A further concession to certain
teachers was, however, made last year.
This provided that, in return for an
additional contribution of 1 per cent.
from the teachers, for certain defined
periods, full-time contributory pensionable service between 1st February, 1922,
and 1st July, 1936, should also be taken
into account for the purpose of calculating the lump sum payment.
But it was stipulated that the additional payments to teachers, involved by
this further concession, should not be
made until the end of the war, although
the teachers' additional contributions
were to be payable as from 1st July,
1939.
109 Superannuation (War) Bill
Delays in Postal Censorship 110
27 MAY 1941
The position is, therefore, that until
the end of the war the Superannuation
Fund will receive considerable annual
additions to its income, whilst the
liability on the fund, towards which these
contributions are made, will not emerge
until after the war. It is desirable that
these additional sums accruing to the
fund should be treated as capital and
invested so as to provide a "nest egg" to
be
used in due course to meet the
additional liabilities.
. Sub-clause (1) of Clause 1 provides for
the investment of the additional income
received each year, but the amount to be
invested is, for convenience, taken as the
excess over £80,000-the approximate
contribution-income of the fund in the
years immediately preceding 1st April,
1940.
Sub-clause (2) empowers the Ministry
of Finance to realise, after the war, all
or part of this investment, and to use it
as income of the fund towards meeting
payments to teachers, who retire during
the war years, of the arrears of additional allowances.
I beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Superannuation (War Service) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. ROBB: This Bill is a Measure
designed to make easier, if necessary, the
way of persons joining the Crown Forces
during the present war or undertaking
some other form of war employment. It
has the additional merits of being brief,
non-contentious, and, to a large extent,
self-explanatory.
Superannuation schemes for their staffs
have been organised by a number of
employers-private firms as well as
public or semi-public bodies and full
participation in the benefits is usually
contingent on the workers' remaining
continuously in the firm's employment.
The prospect of forfeiting some, if not all,
of his rights might, therefore, make a
man hesitate about leaving his employment to join up or undertake war work.
I think it will be unanimously agreed
that, in view of the vital importance of
furthering the war effort, any deterrent
factor of this nature should be removed;
but I understand that in some cases the
statutory rules or other conditions
governing some of the superannuation
schemes render it impossible, as matters
stand at present, for the workers' rights
to be preserved.
The purpose of the Bill is to remove
this barrier by giving power to the
trustees or other persons administering
the schemes to make the necessary arrangements for preventing loss of benefits.
Sub-section (2) of Clause 1 grants these
enabling powers, and hon. Senators will
observe that it is retrospective in effect.
It is a fairly common practice for
superannuation schemes to include not
only pension payments but also life
assurance benefits and payments in the
event of premature incapacity. The
definition of superannuation schemes has,
therefore, been drawn very widely.
Moreover, some of the schemes are
operated by insurance companies under a
of
system
group insurance.
The
companies who undertake this class of
business entered into a satisfactory agreement with the British Government as to
the manner in which they will deal with
any policies covering any employees
affected, and I am glad to be able to say
that the undertakings which they gave
will apply equally to Northern Ireland.
It only remains for me, to add that the
Bill is on the same lines as an Act which
was passed for Great Britain last year by
the Imperial Parliament and which was
fully discussed beforehand with the
National Advisory Council-a body which
represents both employers' and workers'
organisations. I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
whole House for Tuesday next.
Delays in Postal Censorship.
Order read for resuming adjourned
debate on Motion-(8th April, 1941)-
"That in the opinion of this House
the Government should make representations to the Imperial Government
to ensure that in future steps are taken
Delays in Postal Censorship 112
Delays in Postal Censorship
THE SENATE
to avoid delays similar to those which
have occurred in the Postal Censorship
Department."-(Mr. McLaughlin).
Mr. LESLIE: As may be remembered,
I moved the adjournment of the debate
on the last occasion, and I think it will
also be remembered I did so purely for
convenience of debate in order to afford
the right hon. Gentleman time to look
into the question, which was rather
sprung upon him. The House was not
in a position to finish the discussion that
afternoon, and it seemed better that the
matter should stand over until our next
meeting. I have really nothing more to
say about the subject of the Motion itself.
Mr. ROBB: I have received from the
Ministry of Information a statement with
regard to this matter. With regard to
the general principle of the supervision of
matter sent by post, a permit by the
Postal Censorship Branch of the Ministry
of Information is necessary for the transmission of printed typescript and similar
matter from Northern Ireland to Eire.
It is the duty of the Permit Branch of
the Postal Censorship to examine a proportion of the packages despatched under
permit to see that the conditions of the
permit are observed, and that the matter
sent thereunder is not detrimental to
national security; that is, that it does not
contain statements calculated to impede
the national war effort.
Having regard to the gravity of the
statements contained in the Pastoral, and
the exalted position held by the author of
those statements, the Censorship Officer
in Charge at Belfast deemed it proper to
submit the Pastoral to the Headquarters
of the Ministry of Information in
London, that is, to the Press and Censorship Bureau.
In so doing the Censorship Officer in
Charge acted correctly in the performance
of his official duty. The Censorship Officer
in Charge would be expected to take
similar action in future with a complete
impartiality, and without regard to the
religious or political connections of the
writer of any
article intended for
publication.
No delay in dealing with the matter.
occurred within the Postal Censorship
Branch of the Ministry of Information
itself. Such delay as did occur is attributable to the length of time necessarily
taken for communications between Belfast
and London.
In connection with this matter I have a
list of dates. On February 20th the
packages containing the Lenten Pastoral
were received by the Permit Branch.
Belfast, and referred to the officer in
charge for his ruling. On February 21st
the Pastoral was despatched from Belfast
to the Press and Censorship Bureau,
On
London, and the packets detained.
February 22nd Messrs. Bennett Bros.
of
telephoned the Officer in Charge
Censorship asking for the release of the
Pastoral. The Officer in Charge consulted
the Regional Information Officer at Stormont, who confirmed that the Pastoral
should be retained until the ruling of the
Press and Censorship Bureau had been
received. On February 25th the Pastoral
was received by the Press and Censorship
Bureau, London. On the same day &
letter in reply was despatched by the
Press and Censorship Bureau. This was
not received in Belfast until 3rd March.
That is where the delay came in.
In connection with this matter I see
that, following the statement made in the
Imperial House of Commons, Cardinal
MacRory made a speech at Knockbridge,
County Louth, in which, referring to the
action of the Imperial Government in
detaining this particular Pastoral, he said
this:-
"A bishop's pastoral letter is written
with scrupulous regard for truth,
justice, and freedom; it is an intimate,
important, and exclusive document
which a civil government has no right
to withhold from the people to whom it
is addressed."
I happened yesterday to take up, by
accident, the "Fortnightly Review." I
found that the first article in it was
entitled "Inside Ireland." I do not
know the author, and I do not remember
whether his name was given, but I would
just like to read one paragraph from that
article. The writer is discussing the
censorship as it operates in Eire, and this
is the paragraph:
"The censor recently suppressed a
portion of the Lenten Pastoral of Dr.
Morrisroe, the Roman Catholic Bishop
of Achonry, in which it is reportedfor the public has not been allowed to
see it that the bishop spoke of the
sufferings of Poland, the persecution of

religion, and said that it was right for
his people to feel sympathy with those
who were fighting Nazi aggressors.
sentence that was allowed to appear indicates the probable strength of the
suppressed portion. 'Not since the days.
of Herod had there been such disregard
for the lives of those who were guiltless
of any crime.' Simultaneously the
Pastorals of Cardinal MacRory and two
other Catholic bishops in Northern
Ireland appeared with strong criticisms
of the Northern Ireland Government,
which indicates a remarkable contrast
in the liberty of speech permitted
Catholic bishops under the respective
Protestant and Catholic Governments."
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: If I had referred
in this House to another State in the way
the Leader of the House has done I
should have been ruled out of order. I
think it will be admitted that in dealing
with an important Pastoral such as this
a delay of ten days was rather too much.
It was the Cardinal's Pastoral to his
flock, and surely when they had got it in
London it would have been easy to have
telephoned or wired to Belfast instructing
the Postal Censorship Branch in Belfast
to release it. Then the Pastoral could at
least have been read on the second Sunday. We all know that His Eminence
Cardinal MacRory is a most distinguished
Churchman, and we should all be very
proud of him. A little more attention
might have been paid to a gentleman in
his position. He is one of the greatest
of Irish Churchmen, diplomats and statesmen of the English-speaking world of the
present age.
He is a Cardinal Prince of the Catholic
Church, ranking next to His Holiness the
Pope. In 1933 he was appointed Papal
Legate to England for the laying of the
foundation stone of Liverpool Metropolitan Cathedral. In the year 1934 he
was specially selected as Papal Legate to
the great Dominion of Australia for the
Centenary celebrations where, on arrival
in Melbourne, he was received with all
the honours due to Royalty. Every Irishman, and particularly Ulster Irishmen
worthy of the name, are proud of such an
illustrious Ulsterman. Yet his Lenten
Pastoral to his subjects is held up for an
unpardonable time, and this Six County
Government, within whose jurisdiction he
resides, refuse to make any representations on the plea that they have no control
over these services. I say they have some
measure of control, because no appointment in the Post Office of any substantial
weight is made without the approval of
the Six County Government.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: That is not
right.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN : On the last
occasion this matter was debated I was
told that the majority of the staffs in the
Censorship Offices are Catholics. That is
not so. I know for a fact that there have
been ten appointments of censors made
along the Border, and only one was a
Catholic. That is my reply to that argument. Surely the Government have a
duty to their subjects, and as custodians
of their subjects rights, not alone in
postal and censorship matters, but in
every other matter that affects their
everyday lives. However, I suppose I
must take it that these privileges are to
be denied to His Eminence and his people,
who are 35 per cent. of the population
under this Government.

Ministry of Commerce:

Laid before the House (pursuant to

Question to Minister.
PETROL ALLOWANCES FOR
REMOVAL OF EVACUEES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in
Paper in the name of Mr.
Maguire:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the Petroleum
Officer has permission to make allowance for petrol used by private motor
owners in carrying evacuees from
Belfast to outlying districts in the Six
Counties, and, if so, what allowances
are permitted under the regulations.
Mr. MAGUIRE: The reason I have
put down this question is that I have
heard a great many complaints from
private motor-car owners who gave
voluntary service during the recent blitz
in Belfast by conveying many homeless
women and children to different parts of
the Six Counties. The petrol allowance
made to some of these motor-car owners
was very, very small. In one case a private
motor-car owner made two journeys from
Belfast to Kilcoo, two journeys from
Belfast to Dromara, and two journeys
from Belfast to Cushendall with homeless women and children. He did not
look for free petrol but only for coupons,
and he received coupons for five gallons
of petrol, which would not cover him for
one of those journeys. I feel that private
motor owners have been treated very
scantily for the great services they
rendered on that occasion.
Another private motor owner spent
several days in taking women and
children to homes in the country in the
Cushendall area. He also conveyed food
and clothing. For those services he was
allowed only two gallons of petrol. That
is certainly very poor treatment, and
very poor encouragement for voluntary
workers. I now come to the personal
element. I myself gave a good deal of
service during that particular period. I
I called with the Chief A.R.P. Warden in
the Cushendall area and asked him if I
could get coupons for the petrol I had
used in making those journeys. He had
no coupons, but he said he would give
me a letter to the Petroleum Officer in
Belfast. I got this letter early in May,
but I carried on as long as I could with
my petrol ration, thinking that I might
last out until the end of the month without having to ask for additional coupons,
but on Wednesday last I had practically
no petrol left, and all my coupons had
been used.
I accordingly went to the Petroleum
Office at Armagh House, Ormeau
Avenue. I saw a young lady in the office,
told her who I was and my business, and
asked for an interview with the Petroleum
Officer or his deputy. The young lady
read the letter I had got from the chief
A.R.P. officer in Cushendall, and then
told me I could not see either the
Petroleum Officer or his deputy. She
said those were her instructions. I think
that is very poor treatment for a public
representative to receive from a public
office. There is not much courtesy shown
in that office. I will read the letter I
got from the Chief A.R.P. warden at
Cushendall:-
"This is to certify that Senator
Joseph Maguire, Lake Glen, Andersonstown, Belfast, has obliged the
billeting authorities in this area by
doing innumerable runs with his car,
helping us to distribute evacuees to
suitable billets, distributing straw and
bedding, food, etc. He placed himself
and his car at our disposal for two days,
and I strongly recommend that he be
given an allowance of petrol coupons to
cover the work done.
"He and his car are at our disposal for
any future emergency, and I cannot
expect him to help us unless he be
given the petrol coupons recommended."
I thought that that letter from the
Chief Warden would have been sufficient
Superannuation (Teaching) Bill 120
Question to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. Maguire]
to ensure me being given a few coupons
to carry me on until the end of the month.
I did not want free petrol, but merely
coupons to purchase petrol. I appeal to
the Leader of the House to do something
in this matter; otherwise the voluntary
services that have been given in the past
will not be repeated in the future.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
In circumstances of grave emergency,
such, for example, as those obtaining
immediately after the recent heavy airraids on Belfast, it is within the discretion
of the Northern Ireland Petroleum Officer
to grant special issues of motor spirit for
specific purposes. Such purposes include
issues to individuals whose houses have
been rendered uninhabitable and who
wish to evacuate their families. The
amounts of such issues are not governed
by regulations, but are determined on the
facts of each case.
May I add that I shall bring to the
attention of the Petroleum Officer the
facts stated by Senator Maguire.
I should
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I would like to

ALLOCATION OF GOVERNMENT

SCHOOLS AND WAR DAMAGE

Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That this
House do now adjourn until such date
as Mr. Speaker may appoint.'
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I desire to bring
before the House a question with regard
to an answer given to me on Tuesday last
in connection with the issue of Government advertisements to the Press in
County Tyrone. We,
as the representatives of the minority here, expect
at least fair play. I cannot see much
fair play in the answer we received from
the Leader of the House this day week.
I have several times in this House raised
a question regarding the issue by the
Ministry of Agriculture of their
announcements and regulations, and I
have simply asked the Ministry to allow
our people the right to read in their own
newspapers announcements of the issue
of Regulations by the Ministry of
Agriculture.
On this day week I put a question
regarding expenditure on advertisements
by the Ministry of Agriculture during
the year 1940. The answer I received
was that in County Tyrone the
expenditure on advertisements in
Unionist newspapers was £211 5s 5d.
These Unionist newspapers represent
a Unionist population of 57,000. I
was informed that the Nationalist
newspapers in County Tyrone received
the small sum of £59 15s 11d,
although these Nationalist papers publish
for a Nationalist population of 70,596.
In County Fermanagh the Unionist
papers received £157 9s 5d, and they
serve a Unionist population of 24,373.
The Nationalist papers received the very
small sum of £44 7s 7d, and they represent a population of 30,196 Nationalists.
There is the position in Counties Tyrone
and Fermanagh. Here is the Ministry
of Agriculture preaching fair play,
justice, and liberty, and there is the fair
play, justice, and liberty that is given
to our people and to the Nationalist
Press in County Tyrone and County
Fermanagh. I ask if it is fair that
where we have a population of 70,596
the sum of £59 should be spent on
advertising in the Nationalist Press
compared with £211 expended on
Unionist papers representing the minority
population of County Tyrone of 57,000.
I go further and I say it is not fair
to those 70,000 people to deprive them
of the Regulations and notices issued by
the Ministry of Agriculture. They are
part and parcel of Northern Ireland.
They are citizens under this Parliament,
and they are entitled to the same treatment and the same information as anyone else. I do say that this is an unfair
state of affairs. It is also unfair as far
as County Fermanagh is concerned. For
the year 1940 the Unionist Press in
that county received £157, against the
Nationalist Press £44, although there is
a Nationalist population of 30,000
against a Unionist population of 24,000.
I go further and I say that that expenditure is merely subsidising the Unionist
papers in that district, while it deprives
123 Government Advertisements

THE SENATE
Government Advertisements
[Mr. McLaughlin]
the majority of the population in each of
those two counties of information to
which they are justly entitled. I find
that the "Fermanagh Herald," which has
a much larger circulation than any other
paper in the district, is still kept under.
Last week there was an invitation given
to the Tyrone and Fermanagh farmers to
cut peat, and I find that the only papers
in which the announcement appeared
were the Unionist papers.
The Leader of the House stated last
week that it was the desire of the Government to divide the advertisements among
as many papers as possible. I agree with
that, provided the advertisements are
fairly distributed, but surely to goodness
in an area such as Fermanagh and Tyrone
the Ministry should take into consideration the views of the majority of the
inhabitants of both those counties. I was
also informed that another factor in the
allocation of the advertisements was the
circulation of the papers. I respectfully
suggest that the Government should send
a public auditor round and let him
inquire into the circulation of these
papers. If he inquires from the Paper
Controller he will be able to tell the
auditor what paper is supplied to all the
papers in the Six Counties.
As I have already stated, only last
week, in their issues of 28th May, both
Fermanagh Unionist newspapers carried
advertisements urging the public to "Cut
More Peat." Evidently they want their
supporters to cut more peat, and they
want to deprive our population of the
opportunity of seeing the notice. It is
all right so long as they give it to the
Unionist Press, and their Unionist
supporters get the information. Our
people do not count. I wonder whether
this is deliberate or not? I shall be very
anxious. to find out. In the same
week there was approximately 56 inches.
of Government advertising in the
"Fermanagh Times"; in the "Reporter"
there were nearly 30 inches; and the
"Herald," which is the only Nationalist
paper in the district, was cut off with a
miserly 8 inches.
During the four weeks ending in this
same week-28th May-the "Fermanagh
Times" was given 94 inches of Government advertising; the "Reporter"
received over 100 inches, and the
"Herald," our Nationalist paper, had to
This
be content with less than 20 inches.
is the Government's idea of dividing the
advertisements and seeing that they are
justly distributed. The readers of the
"Fermanagh Herald" are to know
nothing of the terms of the War Damage
Act, but the Protestants of Fermanagh,
through their two papers, are to have it
twice published for their benefit. Milk
producers prices are to be known to
Protestants, but from Catholics they
must needs be hidden, because the process
of enlightenment might involve the
Government in giving sparingly of its
funds to a newspaper for whose politics
the Government has hatred.
That is a state of affairs that should not
exist. We are supposed to be living in a
democratic country, and we should have
fair play all round. The Leader of the
House should see to it that in future the
Ministry of Agriculture give at least fair
play in the matter of issuing their
advertisements, and that their notices and
regulations should be placed fairly and
squarely in all the papers. I have no
objection to them appearing in the
Unionist newspapers. Everyone is entitled to this information, but I say that
we are entitled to have that information
in our papers. I suggest it is time the
Ministry of Agriculture wakened up and
paid less attention to the speeches that
are put out from political platforms
denouncing the Ministry of Agriculture
every time they put an advertisement in
the Nationalist Press.
I do not know what other Government
Departments are doing in the matter of
issuing advertisements, but if this is a
sample of the justice meted out to our
people by the Ministry of Agriculture,
we must take it for granted that every
other Ministry deals with the Nationalist
papers in the same way.
The MAYOR OF DERRY: In my
opinion the speech of Senator McLaughlin
is a political speech, and I did not know
that political speeches were allowed in

people Senator McLaughlin represents are
at all anxious to get the information he

wish to have this information, which is
issued to the people who would be likely
to make use of it. It is not fair to take
advantage of an occasion such as this to
make a political tirade, as the hon.
Senator has done.

Major DOBBS: A few days ago I
wanted a question tabled in my name,
but owing to the restricted postal facilities
at Whitsun it was not possible to have
it tabled in time. I want to ask the
Minister of Education whether he will
consider the advisability of issuing a short
memorandum to school authorities advising them on the right course to pursue
with regard to the new War Damage
Insurance scheme. The memorandum
published by the Board of Trade is very
informative and, on the whole, easy to
understand, and it gives us a lot of information. But what any of us who have to
do with schools would like to know is not
so much how we are to insure but what
percentage of the value of the furniture
in schools we should insure. I am sure
members of Regional Committees will be
very glad to have the opinion of the
Minister of Education on this point.
There are some other schools besides
elementary schools, namely, secondary
schools, and the governors of those schools
are also trustees of the schools. Insurance is heavy-30s up to 30th September.
If they were private people they would
probably say it was not worth while
insuring, but as they are trustees of those
schools they would really like a higher
authority-and I cannot think of a
higher authority than the Ministry of
Education to give them a lead and tell
them whether to insure up to the full
value of what they think their furniture
is worth, or whether to insure for 10 or 20
per cent. I think that any lead from the
Ministry of Education would be of the
very greatest service to governors of
schools, and I hope the Minister of
Education will be able to tell us exactly
what he thinks we should do.
Mr. ROBB: With regard to the question
raised by Senator Major Dobbs, I will
consider the suggestion he makes, that a
circular should be sent to the trustees

policy they should pursue under the

what the position is, but I will try to
have it clarified and advice given.

Mr. SPEAKER: I should like, first of
all, to thank my hon. Friend the Mayor
of Derry and my hon. Friend, Mr.
McLaughlin, for the very kind words they
have spoken about me-all too kind.
should also like to thank all the Members
of this House for their concurrence.
When this House first did me the honour
of electing me Speaker, now nearly eleven
years ago, the hon. and learned Member
who was kind enough to second my
election, and who now sits in another
place, gave me two pieces of advice. First
of all he said he thought it was essential
to preserve a sense of humour, a sense very
well developed in himself. I think he
was quite right, because a sense of humour
very often carries one over many difficult
places. The second piece of advice he
gave me was this. "Remember," he said,
"that a soft answer turneth away wrath,"
or, as he expressed it, "a soft answer
breaketh wrath" which, I believe, is the
rendering in the version of the Scriptures
used in his Church. I think that is a
very good piece of advice, and that it is
applicable to all of us.
Every Member of this House has his
convictions and, I trust, the courage of
his convictions, and I do hope all our
discussions here may be carried out in a
spirit of friendliness and forebearance,
and without rancour, for bitterness carries
us nowhere. It has always been my aim
to try to give hon. Members as much
latitude as possible, but there are
Standing Orders and there are Rules of
Procedure, and we must keep within their
limits and not try to jump over fences. If
we do that our business will be conducted
smoothly and well, and in accordance with
the dignity of this House, and with the
high standard of procedure which was
originated and handed down to me by my
predecessor, and which it has always been
my earnest endeavour to uphold and
promote.
Once again I should like to thank hon.
Members for the great honour they have
done me in electing me once more to this
Chair. I am deeply sensible of that
honour. This is the third time I have
been elected to the Chair of this House. It
almost reminds me of Dick Whittington.
But that achievement has been utterly
eclipsed by the Lord Mayor of Belfast,
whom I am sorry not to
see in his
place to-day. He has broken Dick
Whittington's record many times over,
and I hope he will continue to do so.
I am afraid I have got a little bit
beyond the allotted span of life which is
laid down in the Book of the Psalms,
although I must say the Psalmist might
have extended it a little bit. But, after
all, one is as old as one feels, and if hon.
Members are kind enough to think I am
still capable of carrying on I will try to do
my duty to the best of my ability, always
buoyed up with the feeling that I shall
have the loyal support of Members in the
future as I have had in the past. As I
think I mentioned on a former occasion,
I shall also have the very pleasant feeling
that I am amongst friends, for I have
always looked upon every individual
Member of this House as a personal
friend, and that has made my task such
a light one, and my duties so agreeable..

137 Fisheries (Flax Water) Bill--

8 JULY 1941
Second Reading :
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 8th July, 1941.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Paper Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Agriculture:
Listing of Potatoes (Prohibition) (Northern Ireland), 1941.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Fisheries (Flax Water) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move the Second Reading of this
Bill. I should like at the outset to
indicate briefly its object and the methods
by which it is hoped to achieve that
object. Hon. Members will know that
farmers were called upon this year to
grow 100,000 acres of flax, a considerable
increase upon the acreage that was grown
in 1940. It is hoped that the response to
this call will come up to expectations and
it is the belief of the Government that
the partial relief afforded to the farmer
by this Bill will be found in the end to
have had a considerable share in the
attainment of the Government's aim. This
is the purpose of the Bill-to give confidence to the farmer that he will not be
subjected to vexatious prosecution in a
court of law for what he regards as a
technical offence against the existing
fisheries laws.
The law as it stands at present, so far
as it relates to the farmer who rets his
own flax-and 75 per cent. of this year's
crop will be dam-retted-was passed in
the year 1842. Section 80 of the Fisheries
(Ireland) Act of that year reads as
follows:-
"No person shall throw, empty, or
cause to run or flow into any river or
lake any dye stuff, or other deleterious
or poisonous liquid, or shall throw into.
such river or lake any lime, spurge, or
other deleterious or poisonous matter,
or shall steep in such river or lake any
flax or hemp, and if any person shall
so offend he shall forfeit and pay for
every such offence any sum not exceeding ten pounds."
It has been held that water in which
flax has been steeped-what we ordinarily
call flax water-is a deleterious or
poisonous liquid within the meaning of
that Section, and consequently, no matter
whether any fish are harmed or not, if
such water is allowed to flow into either
a lake or stream the farmer commits an
offence. Very often such an offence produces no harm, but if a prosecution was
brought the magistrate was bound to
convict. Considering the call the, Government has made upon the farmer to grow
as much flax as possible, the Government
feels a certain responsibility to the
farmers; that they will be free. from
prosecution where no harm in fact is done
or is likely to be done, and if the farmer
has taken every reasonable precaution to
see that he does not commit an offence.
The Bill proceeds to safeguard the
farmer in two ways. In the first place,
it says it shall be a defence for the farmer
who is prosecuted for turning flax water
into a stream or lake if he proves that
he used all reasonable
means, within
reasonable cost, to prevent what he is
doing, doing any damage. The Bill provides that before he is prosecuted the fiat
of the Attorney-General must be obtained
for the prosecution. It also provides that
before the Attorney-General gives any
such consent he shall take into consideration any representations made to him
either by the Ministry of Commerce or
the Ministry of Agriculture. In order
that either of those Ministries may be in
a position to make such representations
the Bill further enacts that any person
proposing to bring a prosecution must
give notice to those, two Ministries. Those
are the provisions intended to relieve the
farmer from prosecution for an offence
which really does no harm. But if a
farmer deliberately causes damage, of
course he is not protected.
There is one other supplementary provision. Under the original Act one-third
of the fine went to the water bailiff who
prosecuted. This Bill provides that that
incentive to promiscuous prosecution
shall be withdrawn, and that for the
future the whole of the fine, where the
prosecution is by a water bailiff, shall go
to the Board of Conservators, and that
that portion of it which would have
gone to the prosecuting policeman shall
139 Fisheries (Flax Water) Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Robb]
be paid to the Ministry of Public Security
and go as an appropriation-in-aid of the
cost of the R.U.C.
Those are the main features of this
Bill. Its intentions are quite clear.
Whether it will carry out those intentions
is, of course, a matter that remains to be
seen, but at any rate it is an honest
endeavour on the part of the Government
to meet what is in fact a grievance on
the part of the farmer, and a grievance
that the Government feel is a deterrent
to the response the farmers may make to
the call to grow more flax. I need not
point out to this House how important a
supply of flax is to our linen industry,
and what a large part its export plays in
preserving the balance of trade. and
exchange at the present time. I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time."
As a
Sir EMERSON HERDMAN:
member of a family which has been connected with the linen trade for over one
hundred years I should like warmly to
support this Bill. The Leader of the
House has told us how necessary it is
for the winning of the war that the flax
industry should be supported by all of
us. In the last war it was stated that the
war was won on the linen wings of
Belfast, but in this war it has been
asked, what is the good of linen wings
made in Belfast when aeroplanes have
metal wings? While that is quite true,
it is also true that a very important part
of the present day aeroplane is still made.
of linen. Linen is also wholly used in
training machines, and there are now
more training machines in this country
than there were fighter or bombing
machines in the last war.
Another thing for which linen is vitally
important is in the making of the tape
which holds up parachutes and paratroops. It is the only textile that has
been found to support the weight of the
young men who bale out after they have
been shot down, or the paratroops who
in increasing numbers have got to be
supported in the air. The only textile
which is capable of doing that is linen.
I am not accustomed to public speaking, and as I have certain points to put
before the House I hope the House will
excuse my reading them.
The linen industry, whose millions of
capital and capacity for the employing of
many thousands of workers have contributed materially to the peace-time prosperity of the Province of Ulster for
nearly two centuries, is now, as it was
during the Great War, a vital factor in
the huge machine which has been
assembled for the prosecution of the war
in which Britain and her Dominions are
now fighting side by side for the crushing
of Nazism.
The industry is engaged in supplying
linen goods urgently required for the
fighting services and for the maintenance
of the export trade of the United Kingdom, for which it is the second largest
producer of the dollars so necessary to
Britain for the purchase of armaments
and essential war supplies.
The British Ministry of Supply has
rigorously confined the linen industry to
the manufacture of goods for the two
purposes aforementioned, but the Trade
can only successfully meet the national
requirements if it has adequate supplies
of flax.
The linen trade has had to face a
serious shortage of flax, inasmuch as supplies from Russia, the Baltic provinces
and the Continent are cut off; therefore,
it has become necessary to concentrate
mainly on home grown flax, which is suitable for the manufacture of the bulk of
the goods now required, and the Flax
Production Committee, appointed by the
spinning trade early last year to endeavour, by every means in their power,
to increase the acreage in Northern
Ireland, have visited all the flax growing
centres in the Six Counties, and have
learned at first hand of the many difficulties facing flax growers.
One of these difficulties is the disposal
of flax water, which is governed by the
Fisheries Act (Ireland), 1842. Farmers
have been urged by the Government to
do their utmost to increase the flax
acreage, and this year there has been a
splendid response to this appeal, with the
result that the area under flax is in the
neighbourhood of 100,000 acres. In view
of this flax growers have been greatly
perturbed owing to the greater difficulty
in which they will now find themselves in
the matter of complying strictly with the
law relating to the disposal of flax water
as the result of their patriotic response
to the call of the Government.
Linen manufacturers are fully aware of
the desirability of preserving fish life and
141 Fisheries (Flax Water) Bill-

8 JULY 1941
Second Reading
in
of preventing pollution by flax water,
view of the detrimental effect such pollution is said to have on commercial undertakings whose plants, situated on river
banks, depend on supplies of clean water.
They are also aware that catch dams are
regarded as being effective in preventing
rivers from being polluted by flax water,
but they point out that farmers cannot
always provide catch dams, by reason of
the impracticability of constructing such
dams, having regard to the levels at which
the retting ponds are situated.
It is reasonable to suppose, however,
that farmers as a whole will take every
reasonable precaution to dispose of their
flax water in a way that will do least
harm to fish life. What they have
objected to all along is the fact that they
have been looked upon by the authorities
as potential law breakers, simply because
they have flax dams on their land.
That the Bill, in its passage through
the House, has been well received by the
farmers' representatives is a matter for
great satisfaction, and should have the
effect of removing from the farmer's
mind all fear of the prosecution which
he hitherto dreaded should any of his
flax water have entered a river in spite
of his most careful precautions. His
position is rendered more secure by the
fact that under the new Bill no prosecution
be undertaken without the
consent of the Attorney-General, who
will examine the case and hear representations from the responsible Ministries.
can
It is to be hoped that this new concession will be so well honoured by the
farming community, now that they will
be freed from the immediate menace of
prosecution, that they will take every
step in their power to protect fish life,
and not the least important step will be
that of providing suitable catch dams
where possible.
Certain of the Clauses inserted in the
Bill are for the duration of the war only,
but it is hoped they will be found to
work so well that they will become part
of our permanent legislation.
I have great pleasure in supporting
the Bill.
an
Mr. STEVENSON: I wish warmly to
support this Bill. It is
honest
attempt to remedy an injustice and to
remove a grievance the farmers have had
for generations. If this were peace time
the Bill should receive the favourable
consideration of this House, but we are
not at peace; we are in the midst of a
life and death struggle for our very
existence, and we must look at this Bill
entirely from the point of view of the
national interest. The British Government require more and more equipment
made from linen. They require more
and more money to wage the war, and we
can help them very much by increasing
our exports of linen goods, which means
more flax. We have 100,000 acres of
flax this year, and, if the Government is
wise, it will press for 200,000 acres next
year. In this coming year, if we are
to continuing growing flax in
country, we must get the good-will of
the farmers. That is absolutely necessary.
The farmer is not asking for any favour;
he is just asking for justice.
this
One danger I foresee in this Bill is that
a great many farmers may be of the
opinion that the Bill exonerates them
entirely from any liability for wanton
damage. I believe the Ministry have the
names of all growers of retted flax, and
I suggest to the Minister of Agriculture
that a circular should be sent to each
farmer pointing out his position under
the Bill. That would serve a double
purpose. The farmer would learn what
the law was, and he would be careful
very
to keep out of its clutches. I am not for
one moment arguing that fish are not
going to be killed even when this Bill
becomes an Act. You cannot jump from
20,000 acres of flax to 100,000 acres without doing damage, but we must have
flax, and I think the Bill as it stands
gives sufficient guarantees against unjust
prosecutions.
There is another point arising out of
all this, and that is the awakening that
has taken place in the linen trade with
regard to doing something with this flax
water. I can assure the Senate that
already the leaders of the linen trade are
looking at this problem from two points.
of view. Engineers are trying to dispose
of this deleterious matter mechanically,
and chemists are trying to render it innocuous, and I honestly believe that
inside the next ten years, if the problem
is followed up, the waters in our
rivers and lakes will be purer than they
have been for a long time past, and consequently there will be much better fishing.

THE SENATE

Mr. LESLIE: Looking back over the
last two speeches one is constrained to
remark that nobody has ever suggested
that we did not want linen, or that linen
could be produced without flax. I confess I am not convinced either of the
necessity for this Bill or of the advantages
which it is claimed it possesses.
Minister of Agriculture hopes to get this
year 100,000 acres of flax. I rather think
he has got beyond the stage of hoping;
he has got to the stage of expectation,
and I hope he will not be disappointed.
One hundred thousand acres of flax is no
doubt a great achievement, and will no
doubt have some bearing on what is called
the war effort, but this is not the first
war we have been engaged in. There was
one before. It started in the year 1914,
and went on for between four and five
years. There was, as has been admitted,
a rather greater demand for flax for more
immediate effective war purposes then
than there is perhaps now.
In the year 1918 in Northern Ireland
110,700 acres of flax were produced, and
in the year 1918, as some of us will
remember, the farmer was oppressed by
the requirement that he should save a
certain proportion of his flax seed. He
objected to it very much at the time,
but I have never heard that it did him
very much harm. At all events, 110,700
acres were grown at that time, when this
unfortunate Section 80 was still on the
Statute Book, and when the Government
grants which are now available for the
making of catch dams were not available.
I confess I cannot see why what was done
then cannot be done now, even if it may
cost a certain amount more money and
a little more trouble. Accordingly, I
have to make the first half of the
customary Motion, that the word "now"
be left out of the Motion. I do that
because I am not convinced either of the
necessity or the advantages of the Bill.
A good deal has been said about
Section 80 of the Act of 1842. I hold no
brief for that Section. I will speak about
it again a little later on. One of the
advantages claimed by the Bill is
that it will apparently help the war
effort. I am not satisfied about
that. I do not think this Bill will have
any influence whatever on this year's
crop, and if any sort of promise behind
the back of Parliament has been made to
the farmers I have my own opinion about
that, and I think everybody else will
have the same. Farmers, speaking
generally, are in my part of the country
very reasonable about this matter of flax
water. They know what is required of
them and try to do it, but you do get
here and there some disgruntled people
who will not take reasonable precautions.
This Bill, if it is properly administeredas to which I have my doubts will bring
home to these people their responsibilities.
Another advantage said to be claimed
for the Bill is that it will bring something
in the nature of peace as between the
farmer, let us say, and the person more
directly interested in the water. I do
not believe that either. The Bill, of
course, only touches the criminal offence,
at present punishable by a fine of £10.
It does not seek to interfere with the civil
remedy of anybody whose water is
injuriously affected. Does it not occur to
anybody that in the event of a man desiring to proceed, having regard to the
obstacle this Bill puts in his way, he will
rush for his civil remedy if he is capable
of suing in the civil court, and will it not
be infinitely more expensive and troublesome for the farmer? You can get
through a petty sessions case at considerably less expense than in the case of an
injunction in the High Court, with possibly heavy damages to pay in addition.
I have spoken of the quantity of acres
of flax produced in 1918 when, as I have
said, 110,700 acres were in flax. That
110,700 had fallen in 1931 to between
7,000 and 8,000 acres. I think it is
obvious that it was the demand that produced the extra supply in 1918, and I
think that that theory is supported if one
looks back a little further.
In the year
1853 it appears that 174,000 acres of flax
were grown all over Ireland. In 1863
that had risen to 214,000 acres, and in
1864 to 301,000 acres. Those two years,
1863 and 1864 were, as some of my
hearers may remember, two of the years
of the War of Secession in the United
States. The demand for flax was
enormous, and the demand produced the
supply with or without such influence as
that Act of 1842 might have. I suggest
that for all practical purposes that Act
has no influence whatever, and that the
supply of flax depends on demand,
supported, of course, by price.
Parenthetically, one may remark as
regards the future and as to whether flax
145 Fisheries (Flax Water) Bill-

8 JULY 1941
Second Reading
will go on being grown in great quantity
in Northern Ireland, there are some
people who maintain that growing flax is
not very good for the soil. Others point
out that every acre devoted to the growing
of flax is an acre filched from the production of food. Others again point out that
the work of damming by means of the
ordinary dam of to-day is extremely unpleasant, and that people do not like it.
Consequently one is almost driven to the
conclusion that the future of flax-growing
in Northern Ireland depends rather on
the extension of these new retteries and
the gradual extinction of the common flax
dam. The rettery is the living force
to-day, and it would appear that 25 per
cent. of the flax grown this year will be
treated in that way. That method of
treatment was not available in 1918 or
1863. That again is something in favour
of leaving the law alone.

heard something about
this dissatisfaction on the part of the
farmers. I have. no particular brief for

those cases the law does sometimes, as the

a right to which he is by law entitled

the consent of the Attorney-General to
a bil of indictment is not an absolutely

person if he has the leave of a judge

has been committed for trial. The
leave of the Attorney-General is not the
sole criterion in those important cases,

Attorney-General should be obtained.

brought in. The hon. and learned

Questions to Minister.
NURSING SERVICES FOR
EVACUEES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what arrangements have been made to provide
nursing services (1) for persons who
have been evacuated to country areas
under the Government scheme, (2) for
persons who have voluntarily evacuated
themselves; whether, if any arrangements have been made, he can state.
the nature of those arrangements, and
if not, whether prompt consideration
will be given to the matter, in view of
the serious extra burden which will
otherwise be thrown upon the existing
District Nursing Associations during
the winter months.
Mr. LESLIE: I am putting this question on behalf of my hon. and gallant
Friend. I think I need add nothing to
what appears on the Paper. The difficulty
referred to is, of course, common to all
District Nursing Associations in Northern
Ireland which work under the Queen's
Institute of District Nursing. I believe.
a circular was sent to those societies to
the effect that an appeal had been made
to the Ministry for assistance so long ago
as the month of May last, and nothing
has yet transpired.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The question of providing additional
nursing services for the persons referred
to has been receiving the attention of the
Government. Medical inspectors of the
Ministry of Home Affairs have been visiting the districts chiefly concerned to assist
and advise local authorities, and others,
of the steps to be taken to deal with the
immediate medical problems of evacuation,
The Ministry of Home Affairs has
already, by circular letter dated 25th
June. last, advised local authorities and
other bodies associated with health and
welfare services in the reception areas,
including district nursing associations,
operating maternity and child welfare
schemes, of the policy of the Government
in the matter, that is to say, that the.
general principle by which it will be
guided is that no additional charge should
fall upon the rates of local authorities as
a result of the increased obligations which
they are undertaking. And the. Ministry
has indicated, pending the issue of more
detailed proposals for dealing with the
problem, that it will be prepared to give
consideration to
any proposals for
immediate necessary extensions to health
and welfare services submitted by particular local authorities.
Mr. LESLIE: Is it recognised that
the bulk of the funds of these societies is
provided by private subscription and
not from the local rates? The normal
practice is for the rates to make some contribution, but that is a very small part
of the local expenditure of the society.
At present, I understand, nurses have to
keep special lists of the extra patients
they visit and so on. Apparently they
are getting nothing for it unless it may
be ultimately some slight increase in that
proportion of expenses provided by the
local authority.
Mr. ROBB: My impression is that the
intention is that no extra charge of that
sort shall fall upon the societies, but that
it shall be borne by the central authority.
I say that with all reserve, but I believe
that is correct.
ACCOMMODATION OF EVACUEES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:-
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the Government
are yet in a position to give any information as to the measures taken to
provide accommodation (1) for persons
evacuated from raided areas, (2) to
regulate the use of accommodation by
persons who have voluntarily evacuated
themselves, and (3) the preparations
made to accommodate further persons
who may be evacuated, or evacuate
themselves, as the result of future air
raids; whether he can state what steps
have been taken (1) to prevent overcrowding of buildings, (2) to ensure
that a dangerously excessive load is not
thrown upon the water supply or sanitary services of any particular locality,
and (3) to direct the flow of future
evacuation towards areas where accommodation exists and away from areas
which are already overcrowded.
Mr. LESLIE: Again in putting this
question I am acting as deputy for the
hon. and gallant Senator. The question
is of a somewhat complicated character,
and I do not think I need trouble the
House by reading it. It divides itself
into sundry parts.
Mr. ROBB: When the responsibility
for the evacuation of Belfast and the rebilleting in the country and any other
portions of the city, of persons rendered
homeless as the result of enemy action,
was taken over by the Ministry of Home
Affairs shortly after the second air
attack, the Ministry realised that the
first essential was to obtain accurate information immediately regarding the
number of evacuees in various parts of
the country and of the available
accommodation.
In order to secure this information as
quickly as possible a number of billeting
officers and enumerators were appointed
by the Ministry to carry out an intensive
survey of Northern Ireland. The object
of this survey was threefold to obtain:
(a) a comprehensive list of all persons residing in Northern Ireland;
(b) a comprehensive list, segregated
into various classes, of the evacuees
and refugees;
(c) the amount of accommodation
available in Northern Ireland.
This survey is almost complete, and
when the results have been scrutinised it
should be possible to obtain a complete
picture of the billeting situation in the
country. The more remote country areas
will be reserved for evacuees of the
priority classes, that is, unaccompanied
children, women with children, aged and
infirm persons, and blind persons,
leaving Belfast and its immediate
vicinity for others.
The Ministry will then at once proceed
to transfer evacuees, who are not in the
priority classes and who are in billets in
the country, to suitable billets in the city
area, and will try as far as possible to
remove from the city all persons who are
in one of the priority classes; at the same
time the surplus of individuals living in
other crowded areas will be removed to
areas where there is more accommodation.
If, in the opinion of the Ministry, the
accommodation available is inadequate to
meet the number of persons who might be
rendered homeless, and for whom accommodation would have to be provided, the
Ministry is prepared to requisition houses
and buildings and these have already been
listed by the various billeting officers. The
Ministry also intends to set up hostels and
to erect huts, as they may be required,
in various parts of the country, and to
provide accommodation in particular for
classes of evacuees who are difficult to
billet or whom it is undesirable to billet
in a private household. In deciding upon
the capacity of any house, or area, for the
accommodation of evacuees, the existing
water supply and sanitary facilities must
be taken into consideration, and the
Ministry will not consider any premises or
area as capable of accommodating a larger
number of evacuees than could be dealt
with by the existing supply and sanitation.
The Ministry hopes, with the cooperation of the Welfare Branch of the
Belfast Civil Defence Authority, to control the evacuation more satisfactorily
than was the case after the last two air
attacks, and by the use of rest centres,
dispersal centres, and clearing centres, to
ensure that only homeless persons will be
evacuated after a raid, and that these
individuals will be sent only to places
where there is accommodation. If individuals who are not homeless, or who are
not of the priority classes, evacuate themselves, billeting allowances will not be
paid in respect of their accommodation,
and all possible steps will be taken to
secure their return to their homes.

Mr. MCALLISTER: Before the Leader
of the House replies I would like to say
-and I hope I shall not be guilty of any
indiscretion-that I I entirely associate
myself with the protest contained in
this question. The administration and
management of the electricity supply in
County Antrim, from the householders'
point of view, could not be more unsatisfactory. When the Bill establishing the
Northern Ireland Electricity Board was
introduced in this House some years ago
I can well remember that small electricity
concerns were swallowed up by the
Government although they were giving a
thoroughly satisfactory service to local
consumers, who were promised a highly
efficient service under the new Board. It
is not only a question of frequent breakdowns in the electricity supply, but the
whole question of the administration and
management of the service. For instance,
it is practically impossible to get a plug
fitted for a reading lamp. I had one
experience last week in connection with
the switchboard in my house. The Electricity Board is responsible for all switchboards. I was informed by my own
independent contractor that this switch
was defective. He made it right temporarily, and he brought with him a
representative of the Electricity Supply
Board. The switch was all right while
he was on the scene, but immediately he
disappeared the current went off.
I am very sorry to have to make this
protest, but I do say that the administration of this Board is most unsatisfactory.
I have no hesitation in saying that if this
Board was an ordinary business in competition with other businesses it could not
possibly exist for one month. There is
no attempt to consider or facilitate consumers, and the whole thing seems to me
to be administered and managed in a
most unsatisfactory manner. I am sorry
to have to make this protest, but in the
interests of electricity consumers I have
no alternative.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I must pay a
tribute to the Electricity Board from the
point of view of my own experience in
County Armagh. I have always found
the Board courteous and anxious to
remedy anything that went wrong, and I
think it is only right and just to state that
here. I have had practical experience of
the Electricity Board, and I have always
found them most helpful and anxious to
do whatever was required, and to do it
immediately.

Unemployment Assistance (Determination of Need and Assessment of Needs)
(Amendment) Regulations (Northern
Ireland), 1940.
Mr. ROBB: The Senate will observe
that I am to move for approval to three
sets of Regulations. The first one is:
"That the Regulations made by the
Ministry of Labour on the 4th day of
November, 1940, under Sections 33 and
46 of the Unemployment Assistance Act
(Northern Ireland), 1934, authorising increases in the assessment of needs of
applicants for unemployment assistance be
hereby approved."
The matter arises in this way. Under
the Act of 1934 Regulations covering the
distribution of unemployment assistance
were made. They ceased to be in force
unless approved by Parliament within a
period of 12 months.
The Regulations were made on 4th
November, 1940, but before the formal
motion to approve them came before the
House the proposal to amend the provisions of the Act of 1934 came before the
House. The Act was passed and received
the Royal Assent in April last. The
result was that the approval of the first
set of Regulations was left over until
both sets of Regulations could come before
the House at the same time.
The third Motion deals with Supplementary Pension Regulations made under
the same authority. These Regulations
have been made in this way. The Unemployment Assistance Board formulates
them, they then come before the Ministry,
the Ministry approves them in their final
form, and they have been laid before
Parliament. I now ask Parliament to
give them its formal approval.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Fisheries (Flax Water) Bill.
Order for the House to be put into

Resolved, "That this House do now

In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Offences and prosecutions).
(1) It shall be a defence for a person
who is charged with a flax water offence
alleged to have been committed on any
occasion after the passing of this Act
to prove to the satisfaction of the
Court before which he is charged that
he had used the best practicable
means, within a reasonable cost, to
prevent the flax water being discharged
into the waters affected in such condition or quantity as to be likely to do
any injury to fish therein.
(2) A prosecution for a flax water
offence shall not be instituted except
with the consent of the Attorney-
General for Northern Ireland, and the
said Attorney-General, before giving
165 Fisheries (Flax Water) Bill-

15 JULY 1941
Committee
or withholding his consent to such a
prosecution, may take into consideration any representations which may be
made to him by the Ministry of Agriculture or the Ministry of Commerce in
the matter, and, in particular,
representations as to whether any
actual damage appears to have been
done to fish life.
(3) Any board of conservators,
officers or other person intending to
apply for the consent of the said
Attorney-General to the institution of
a prosecution for a flax water offence
shall give notice of his intention to the
Ministry of Agriculture and the
Ministry of Commerce within a period
of ten days after the day on which the
offence. is alleged to have been committed.
(4) In this section-
(a) the expression "flax water" means
water which has become deleterious
or poisonous by reason of the
steeping of flax therein, and
(b) the expression "flax water offence"
means an offence against section
eighty of the Fisheries (Ireland)
Act, 1842, consisting of throwing
or emptying flax water into, or
causing flax water to run or flow
into, any river or lake, or of steeping any flax in a river or lake.

Clause 1, leave out sub-clauses (2)
and (3).
Mr. LESLIE: This is the matter of
the introduction of the Attorney-General
to which I devoted some little attention
when the Second Reading was before the
House last week. I do not want to
repeat more than I can help of what I
said then, but I must say definitely that
there is no precedent I know of for the
introduction of the Attorney-General into
police court proceedings. There is, of
course, the precedent I instanced of the
Vexatious Indictments Act of 1859, and
of the other Acts since passed to which
that Act
was applied, and there is
perhaps something of that nature in
Crimes Act prosecutions. But in the
ordinary way the Attorney-General does
not require to give his consent to police
court proceedings.
In the case before us the power given
to the Attorney-General, if Parliament
decides to give it to him, can only be
given after what amounts to a hearing
of the case, and an ex parte hearing at
that. If that goes through the only
ultimate result can be that the court
which hears the case will be embarrassed
by the knowledge of what the Attorney-
General has done. It will be very much
in the position of a court which so far
forgets itself as to look at the endorsements on a motorist's licence before the
case is heard. The effect of a refusal is
surely to deny or defer justice to the man,
and that is expressly dealt with in Magna
Charta.
Moreover, what is the Attorney-
General to do? He has practically got
to hear the case. Is he to consider this
question of reasonable cost? What is he to
do except listen to any representations he
may receive from these two Ministries.
There is also the delay which would
necessarily be entailed. In fact, while
the Attorney-General was
was considering
one case half-a-dozen other cases of
alleged pollution might occur. Whether
you would get any extra flax grown seems
to me to be extremely problematical.
On the Second Reading I compared the
acreage of flax grown in 1918 with the
acreage grown to-day, and I rather
think we were to be told this afternoon
whether the 110,000 acres grown in 1918
were grown in what is now Northern Ireland, the Six Counties, or in the nine
counties as was suggested, although it
was not definitely stated last week.
think we were also to be told the number
of prosecutions that had taken place, and
it might be convenient for the Leader of
the House to give that information on
this Amendment.

It seems to me that if the proposal in

Mr. McALLISTER: I had not an
opportunity of reading this Bill before
the Second Reading, but I have done so
since, and I must oppose the Amendment
on this ground. As I understand it, a
substantial portion of the fine inflicted in
flax water prosecutions at the present time
goes to the prosecutors. We are all human,
and I suggest that that is a principle that
may lead to vexatious and needless litigation. Because of that danger it is
highly desirable that the public should
have the protection of the fiat of the
Attorney-General before any trifling,
vexatious, and unnecessary prosecutions
are brought. For this reason I oppose
the Amendment.
Major BOYLE: The Minister of Agriculture last week informed us that the
object of this Bill is to do away with petty
prosecutions. I come from a flax growing,
flax retting, and flax scutching country
where there is also a great deal of fishing,
and I am not aware that in recent years
there have been any prosecutions whatever
for flax water offences. I should like to
know if there have been many of these
prosecutions. In the country where there
is trout fishing there are small associations
that have done a great deal to improve
the preservation of fish by re-stocking
small rivers with fish which grow to
maturity. I understand that the Government approve these associations to the extent of paying fifty per cent. of their outlay on this re-stocking, as a result of
which fishing is very largely increased and
improved.
can
I, myself, cannot see how the farmer
be persecuted. In the ordinary
course the case will go before the resident
magistrate. Formerly there were such
things as packed benches and the farmer
did not think he got a fair crack of the
whip, but with a resident magistrate he
will certainly get a fair hearing. Farmers
are now guaranteed about 20s a stone for
their flax. They are assisted by grants
for ploughing up their old grass, and
facilities are given them for securing
pulling machines. The farmers are given
every assistance and I do not think it
is too much to ask them to dig small catch
dams so that flax water can be put into
these catch dams instead of being turned
into the little drains or water courses
which flow into the small rivers. I think
there is no physical difficulty in pumping
the water into these catch dams. I should
like to see the water spread over the land
because it is very good for the land and
then there would be no danger of
it getting into the rivers and killing fish
life. For these reasons I feel bound to
support the Amendment, and I hope the
Government will leave the legislation as it
is and not go in for what I might almost
describe as Heath Robinson legislation
through the Attorney-General.

want to wreck the Bill.

Mr. ROBB: There is, of course, the
academic point that if you steep flax in
a lake the first Clause would apply. But
we are dealing with flax water offences,
and this is merely a reproduction of what
is a flax water offence in the Act of 1842.
It is the intention that the assent of the
Attorney-General to a prosecution for a
flax water offence, including this offence,
shall be necessary. You might steep your
flax in a lake and then cut an opening
into a stream and discharge that flax
water into it. That would bring it into
sub-clause (1), but we want to make a
flax water offence cover everything that is
defined as a flax water offence in the Act
of 1842. In that Act steeping flax in a
river or lake is included, and we wish to
include all such offences within the
general scope of this Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: It is specifically included in the section of the Act of 1842,

over, because if we had succeeded in
carrying a subsequent amendment I
should not have bothered moving this
amendment. The difficulty is, who is
going to say whether the cost is reasonable
or not, and if it is the court, is the
Attorney-General going to consider it at
the start. Presumably he is, but apart
from that, what line, if any, are you
giving the court as to "reasonable cost."
I think I reminded the House of the
English Salmon Act of 1861, which did
say definitely that a man, before he got
this protection, should show that the cost
exceeded £100, because if the cost of
making a permanent distribution exceeded
£100 the complainant was to bring it
before the High Court, and the High
Court might have the case heard by a
jury. That was removed by the later
Act of 1923, and there was nothing about
the £100 in that Act. There is now no
guide to the resident magistrate, in whom
the noble Lord appears to have so little
confidence, as to what is or is not reasonable cost, and it might be better to leave
out that question of reasonable cost.
However, the Bill is now so bad that it
does not much matter, but I move the
amendment.
Mr. ROBB: These words are taken
from another Act of Parliament. We do
not give the resident magistrate any idea
of what is reasonable cost, but I think we
may leave it to the common sense of the
court.
Mr. LESLIE: What Act of Parliament does the right hon. Gentleman
refer to?
Lord GLENTORAN: The Salmon and
Fresh Water Act of 1923.
Mr. LESLIE: That is the Act I referred
to as repealing the Act of 1861. I said I
should have preferred Section 8 of the.
1923 Act in place of what we have before
us. I do not think anyone cares much
about this question, and I ask leave to
withdraw the amendment.
*
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

Question to Minister.
OPENING OF GROUSE SHOOTING
SEASON.
Mr. LESLIE: I am sure it will be
appreciated that the question standing in
my name has its origin in representations
that have been made to me perhaps somewhat belatedly. I do not think it necessary to dilate on the subject. Everybody
knows the objections to anticipating the
shooting season, and everybody also
knows that the legitimate owner of shooting rights has power to protect himself.
I beg to ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government is aware that
some misgiving exists lest the date for
the opening of the grouse shooting season
should be advanced in Northern Ireland,
and whether he is prepared to make any
statement on the subject.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): The
question of the date for the commencement of the grouse shooting season in
Northern Ireland has already been
settled.
An Order entitled The Defence (Game)
Regulations, 1941, was made on the 18th
instant under the Emergency Powers
(Defence) Acts by
Acts by His Majesty in
Council reducing the close season in
Northern Ireland as respects 1941 for
moor-game, grouse and heath-game.
Under this Order the shooting of the
birds named may commence on the 1st
August.
The proposed advancement of the
opening of the grouse shooting season was
referred to this Government by the
Imperial authorities. some time ago.
Although it might have been preferable
not to alter the date for Northern Ireland
it was decided, as it would be most
undesirable to have different dates for the
opening of the shooting seasons in Great
Britain and Northern Ireland, that
Northern Ireland should have the same
commencing date as Great Britain.

Thursday, 31st July, 1941.

Death of Senator Long.
Mr. SPEAKER: It is with very great
regret that I have to announce to hon.
Members the death of one of our oldest
Senators, Senator Long, which took
place yesterday. Senator Long was one
of the original Members of this Senate,
of which there are now very few. He
was also for a very long time, I should
think over 50 years, identified with
public life in this Province, and he will
be very greatly missed. Senator Long
had been Deputy Leader of this House
for the past eleven years, and I feel sure
the sympathies of every Member of the
House will go out to his widow and
family, who mourn his loss. As a token
of sympathy I would ask Members to
stand for a moment.
Members accordingly stood in silence.

give his assent to the following Bills
agreed upon by both Houses :-

SUPPLY OF LIVE STOCK FEEDING
STUFFS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Maguire :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if, in view of the
delay in supplying live stock owners.
with their ration of feeding stuffs,
which, in some cases, amounts to three
or four weeks' supply, and that this
delay is explained by the merchants as
being due to their not getting supplies,
arrangements can be made whereby
the merchants could be provided with
a sufficient stock of feeding stuffs to
enable them to issue the weekly ration
without delay; and whether exact information can be supplied to owners
of live stock as to the quantities of
feeding stuffs available, or likely to be
available, so that they might be in a
position to regulate or reduce their
stock.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I put down this
question because of the serious shortage
of feeding stuffs being supplied to
live stock owners. They are being
put to great inconvenience and caused
great hardship because the weekly
ration which had been apportioned to
them has not been delivered for a period
of from three to four weeks; in fact some
of the very heavy feeders are short in
deliveries up to 50 tons. At this time of
the year, when the farmers are very busy
gathering in the harvest, the Ministry
should endeavour to supply them with
their weekly ration of meals. This time
of the year is a very lean period for
farmers as their stocks of feeding stuffs
Questions to Minister
Business of the House
23 SEPTEMBER 1941
from last year's harvest are very depleted
so that they have more or less to depend
upon their weekly supply from the
merchants.
The farmers are blaming the merchants
for withholding supplies from them, and
accusing them of supplying some of their
favourite customers, but I am told that
this is not so, and that the merchants
are not getting supplies from the mills.
We are coming to the lean period between
the two harvests, and in the course of
five or six weeks the farmers will not
feel the shortage of feeding stuffs so
badly as they do now. Owing to the
shortage of labour and adverse weather
conditions in August harvesting operations were held up, but in the course of
five or six weeks farmers will be in a
position to make up some of the shortage
by green feeding.
In my
Even in the past the ration of feeding
stuffs a live stock dealer was entitled to
included many types of feeding stuffs
which did not suit the particular kind of
stock the farmer kept on his farm. In
filling in his ration form he set out the
different types of feeding stuffs he
required in order to make a complete and
varied ration for his live stock.
own case I feed a good many pigs, and I
have not got a delivery of feeding stuffs
from the merchants for the past four
weeks. In consequence I have been very
badly handicapped, and prior to that a
good portion of my allotment included
several meals which were not suited to
pig feeding. Cotton cake meal and linseed cake are meals that are not suitable
for pig feeding, yet they are included to
make up the small ration we are allowed
weekly. I appeal to the Ministry of
Agriculture to make a special effort
during this lean period to supply farmers
with the meals required.
As regards the latter portion of my
question, it would be a great convenience
if the Ministry would let us know exactly
how we stand in the future as to our meal
ration, because in certain events. it will
enable us to reduce our stocks before they
die on our hands. During the past week
I have had to keep stock alive with kale
and grass and a few potatoes. I had no
meal feeding to give them, and I am not
the only one in that position. I have had
several complaints from people in any
district. The present is a very difficult
period for farmers, and I hope the Ministry will take immediate steps to relieve
the situation.
Mr. ROBB: During recent weeks
merchants and farmers have not been
able to obtain their proper supplies of
feeding stuffs under the rationing scheme,
owing to a shortage in the arrivals of
wheat offals.
This difficulty-which was due to war
circumstances outside the control of the
Ministry of Agriculture-has now been
overcome by the Ministry, and steps have
been taken which should result in all
deliveries being brought up to date in the
immediate future.
Every effort is being made to ensure
that merchants have sufficient stocks to
meet the requirements of their registered
customers.
The third rationing period will begin
on the 29th September and details of the
allocations to the various classes of livestock have been issued through the Press.
In addition, particulars of these allocations are recorded on the ration books
which are in the possession of owners of
eligible livestock.
I shall bring to the notice of the Ministry what the hon. Member has said about
the type of feeding stuffs.
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of that,
may one ask whether it has been considered if it would not be possible
