War Damage Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
(War Damage) Bill when it is brought
from the House of Commons."—(Mr.
Robb.)
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Landlord
and Tenant (War Damage) Bill, to
which they desire the concurrence of this
House.
Landlord and Tenant (War Damage)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." I
would like to say frankly at the outset
that this is a very difficult Bill, and it is
pre-eminently a lawyers' Bill. Whether
that is an advantage or a disadvantage
I am not quite sure, but that it is a difficult and complicated measure nobody will
deny for one moment. I do not propose
to go through every detail of the Bill,
but only to state in general the principles
which the Bill seeks to carry out.
Part 1
deals with a covenant to repair imposed
either in a lease or in any other way upon
any particular person. It enacts roughly
that a covenant to repair shall not bind
any person to repair damage that is called
war damage. Roughly speaking, it
means air raid damage or damage which
might be done by any form either of
enemy action or protection against enemy
action. It goes on to say that if part of
any premises suffer war damage then the
covenant to repair the remainder will be
suspended if repair at a reasonable cost
is impracticable, or if such repair being
made would be of no substantial advantage to the covenantee.
It further provides that the tenant and mortgagor must
notify the landlord of war damage as soon
as it occurs, or within a reasonable time
afterwards, and permit the landlord or
mortgagee into possession to inspect.
Part II deals with premises under lease
which suffer by war damage. It deals
with premises under lease which are rendered unfit for occupation. Roughly
speaking, that means not suitable by
reason of the war damage for the purpose
for which they were let. It might be a
house or a shop, but if it is bombed so
that it is rendered for the time being unsuitable for the purpose for which it was
let then these provisions apply. When
that happens, the tenant may do one of
two things. He may first of all serve a
notice of disclaimer or a notice of retention. The first notice means that he
intends to give up the whole thing, and
the second that he means to keep the
premises. If the tenant does not serve
that notice within a reasonable time, his
landlord may serve notice upon him
requiring him to say whether he intends
to keep the premises or not. There is a
further provision that when a tenant is
served with a notice, if he has sub-tenants
he is bound to pass on the notice to them.
Similarly, if the landlord is served with
a notice of disclaimer he must serve his
superior landlord or give notice of such
service. Further, anyone who is interested may apply to the court either to
extend the time or to say that the premises are not in fact suitable.
Suppose premises have been bombed
and rendered unfit and the tenant serves
a notice of disclaimer. Then he must
notify his landlord and every sub-tenant,
and anyone who fails in that duty must
indemnify any person who suffers as a
result of that omission. Where there is
a notice of disclaimer the landlord may
retort with a notice to avoid disclaimer.
If he does not serve such a notice then,
on a notice of disclaimer the lease is
deemed to be surrendered. Likewise,
all sub-leases are deemed to be surrendered except the one entitling a person to
the actual possession of the premises,
unless that particular person himself
serves notice of disclaimer. The person
entitled to the ultimate possession of the
land has the right to say, whatever his
intermediate landlord may do, whether
he will hang on to the premises or not,
and if the disclaimer does operate all
interests are extinguished.
Difficulties, of course will arise in
different ways, and the Bill gives the
court ample powers to modify the provisions of the Bill on application by anyone
interested, in respect of date of surrender
and imposing terms as to removal of
fixtures or otherwise.
Mr. LESLIE: I rather think the right
hon. Gentleman misused a word. He said
the court had ample powers to "modify
the provisions of the Bill." Surely he
was referring to Clause 23?
Mr. ROBB: Does it not virtually
amount to modifying the provisions of the
War Damage Bill-
Second Reading
23 SEPTEMBER 1941
Bill? The court may impose conditions
on date of surrender and the removal of
fixtures.
Mr. LESLIE: That is in the Bill?
Mr. ROBB: It is in the Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: But the court has no
power to modify the terms of the Act?
Mr. ROBB: No. It can alleviate
grievances that may arise. Supposing
premises have been blitzed and the tenant
serves notice of disclaimer, but the landlord wants to hold on to that particular
tenant. He may in that case retort with
what is called a notice to avoid disclaimer.
If he does that then the landlord is
deemed to have entered into a covenant
with the tenant, and if he does so no rent
is payable until the premises are rendered
suitable for the purpose for which they
were let. After he serves such a notice
the lease is not surrendered. It continues
to hold, but its provisions are suspended
until the landlord, wishing the tenant
to hold on to the lease, says, "I will not
let you give it up, but I will put the
premises in repair in due time.”
That
is where the tenant thinks he would like
to get rid of the whole thing. But he
may want to retain the lease and then,
instead of serving a notice of disclaimer
he may serve a notice of his intention to
retain, what is called a notice of retention.
Before he is blitzed he is under covenant
to keep the premises in ordinary repair,
but if they are blitzed and he serves a
notice of retention the Bill says he is to
be entitled to retain them, but he is not
bound to repair them until it is practicable to do so. The tenant is deemed to
covenant with the landlord to do necessary
repairs as soon as is practicable, and even
though he says, "I elect to keep on the
premises," no rent is payable until the
premises are fit to occupy.
There is
provision that either party can come
to the court if they disagree with
regard to making the premises fit, or
whether what has been done has made
them fit. Those are the general provisions with regard to the lease of
premises which have been blitzed.
Clause 13 contains a special code for
what are called short tenancies. These
are weekly, monthly, or quarterly
tenancies, and though the provisions are
not in terms confined to dwelling-houses
the Clause covers the greater part of town
dwelling-houses which are not owned by
the people who live in them. In those
cases the general provisions with regard
to disclaimer and retention are to apply,
and where premises are let on short
tenancies and they are rendered unfit by
war damage and are not occupied by the
tenant no rent is to be payable. If part
only of the premises is occupied by the
tenant the tenant is only to pay such
rent as he agrees with his landlord or as
the court may fix. If part of the
dwelling-house is blown away and the
tenant continues to occupy the other
part he can go to the court and have his
rent fixed for that part of the house he
occupies. Sub-clause (4) provides that
a dwelling-house let on a short tenancy
which has been rendered unfit by
war damage shall be deemed at any
time to be fit if it has been repaired
to such extent as is reasonably
practicable at that time, having regard
to the circumstances prevailing in the
locality, and as is sufficient to render
the dwelling-house reasonably capable
of being used for housing purposes,
and continues to be in that state of
repair.
If the landlord puts part of a house
that has been rendered unfit into such a
condition that it can reasonably be lived in,
then he can notify the tenant that that
is so and the tenant is bound to pay such
rent as the court may fix, even though
he does not go into the house. If it is
fit he ought to go into it, and the court
may order him to pay a reasonable rent
whether he goes into it or not. On the
question as to whether premises have
been rendered fit or unfit the council of
a borough or urban district can give a
certificate which is prima facie evidence
that it is fit, although provision is made
for an appeal to the county court.
There is another provision. It has
been found both in England and Northern Ireland that very often when
houses have been blitzed and the tenant
has left, and the landlord puts them right
again he cannot find his tenant. There
is a provision in this Bill covering that
case. If premises let on short tenancy
have been rendered unfit and are repaired,
but for not less than one month the

THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
tenant is not in occupation, has paid no
rent, and the landlord, having made
reasonable efforts, cannot find him, he can
go to the court and ask the court to put
an end to the tenancy, and the court may
do so.
Clause 14 deals with multiple leases
and gives the court power to split them.
Clause 15 similarly enables the court to
do justice between the parties where land
is let at rack rent.
Part III is probably the most difficult
part of all of this very difficult Bill. It
deals with what is to happen to compensation over the war period. Under the
War Damage Act compensation may take
one of two forms: a value payment, which
is a lump sum; or a cost of works payment, that is, an undertaking to pay the
cost of reinstatement. Clause 18 says
that a tenant who serves a notice of
retention may state in it that the notice
is a conditional notice and is to be treated
as a disclaimer if the Commission awards
a value payment and not a cost of works
payment. A tenant may say: "Well, if
the War Damage Commission will reimburse me the total cost of putting the
premises back into the position they were
in before it will be worth my while hanging on to them, and I will serve notice of
retention, but if they are just going to
pay a lump sum of money I am not going
to take the risk of repairing them." In
that case he can serve a notice of conditional retention.
The subsequent clauses deal with complicated matters with regard to the
apportionment of the War Damage Commission determinations, and I can only
say that as far as I apprehend, and I say
so advisedly, they seem to me to be common sense and equitable. They are, at
any rate, the clauses that have been
passed in England after their experiences.
It is the law that is in operation in
England, and we have only adopted it
here. There are a number of miscellaneous clauses, and then there is the
definition clause, where we get a definition
of all the various terms that are used.
"Lease" in Northern Ireland includes a
fee farm grant.
Mr. SIMMONS: Does it also include
a verbal arrangement. I understand it
does?
Mr. ROBB: I believe it does.
Mr. LESLIE: Whether in writing or
oral?
Mr. ROBB: Yes.

how is he to find out who has a mortgage

Mr. ROBB: He has only to serve

Mr. LESLIE: As the right hon.
Gentleman has said, this is a
very
difficult Bill, but I think the difficulty
disappears to a great extent if you try
to remember what the Bill is really intended to do. I do not think the Bill is
intended to deal with every question that
may possibly arise, but it is intended to
point the way along which you may proceed in order to get that difficulty settled.
I think that is a fair remark to make on
the Bill. It is, of course, dealing with
a very old problem, the old problem which
has faced the courts from time immemorial,
of two innocent parties in misfortune who
is to bear the loss.
The method taken by the Bill seems to
me to be on the whole fair. The landlord has given a covenant for quiet
enjoyment in part consideration for
his rent, and the tenant, owing to
circumstances over which he has no
control, has ceased to have that quiet
enjoyment, and he should not be expected
to go on paying for it. The landlord, on
the other hand, may have such remedies
as he can get under other War Damage
Acts, and, to some extent, he has protection under this Bill, the protection
referred to under Clause 1 relieving him
of the necessity to repair, but there is no
protection for the landlord's income.
is not entirely without the possibility of
protecting his income, but we cannot go
into that at the moment. At the same
time I do not think there is any general
unfairness as far as that goes.
Another point that must be remembered is that the Bill is brought in to
deal only with damage by war. Damage
caused by riot-and damage has been
caused by riot in one or two towns in
Northern Ireland in the past-will not be
covered by this Bill, nor does anybody

Various points have no doubt been

One of them is the question of a judicial

would be inclined to say
there

question put to me is whether a judicial

I rather think the intention is that

tenancies as there are. They mostly
remain in connection with land which was
excluded from the operation of the last

There are a certain number of those, and

The provisions of this section shall

I think the intention is clearly explained, and I do not think it is necessary

the Bill as being an honest and painstaking attempt to mitigate some of the

Mr. McLAUGHLIN : I trust the

the tenant is in a position to serve a

Mr. McALLISTER: I intend to make
a few remarks on this very long and
complex Bill. I desire in the first place
to thank the Leader of the House for the
very lucid way he has placed a very difficult Bill before the Members of this
House, and for the trouble he must have
taken to put himself in possession of all
the material facts. There is one thought
that occurred to me with regard to this
measure. The Leader of the House has
referred to the different courses the War
Damage Commission may take. The
term he used was that they could pay a
value payment, which is a lump sum; or
a cost of works payment.
The thought that occurred to me was
that serious injury might be done to the
community if that were left undefined in
the Bill, as it is at the present moment.
We know that industries come and go.
They have their times of prosperity and
their periods of adversity. Suppose there is
a large business employing hundreds of
people, employing the whole district,
perhaps, adjacent to it and employing,
perhaps, in some provincial towns the
whole town and the area around it;
supposing business is bad and has been
for a number of years and suppose that
the business has not been an attractive
proposition for the Owners as not a
distinctly paying concern.
I offer no opinion at the moment because I have not considered it, but I do
say we should consider the fact as to
whether in any particular case like that
where the bombed-out building is a big
mill or factory or source of almost
universal employment in the district-I
say we should consider if in that case the
war council should have the option of
paying that firm a lump sum and not confine it to restoring the building and compensating him for such things as he may
have incurred. Because, after all, you
can visualise the case in a provincial town
or in many suburbs in the city where the
business has become for many years
looked upon as an established fact and
generation after generation. of families
have grown up and looked upon that particular kind of employment as their
source of employment. If this mill is
damaged the proprietor is getting the
advantage of the lump sum on a business
that has not been paying for a number of
years, and would not draw in the open
market a lump sum. I think that is
worth consideration as to how the whole
community surrounding should be protected.
There is another feature of the Bill.
The learned Leader of the House has
referred to the fact that local councils
can give certificates which are prima facie
evidence that the property is fit to live
in and the work has been sufficiently done
or is not sufficiently done as the case may
be. From my experience of local councils
on the question of expenditure and the
coercion they impose on the small property
owners to expend out of all sense of proportion on repairs to property, I think
that the council by its officials should not
be in a position to give even a prima facie
certificate of fitness or to say what should
or should not be done to make the property
fit within the meaning of the Act. No
doubt the Leader of the House will say
that it is only a prima facie certificate, and
that the certificate comes before the court
merely as the finding of an official.
certainly will take strong excention to
that part of the Bill. I think that
should be simply left as a question
of fact to the court without any
prima facie certificate, without any outside interference. It should be left to
the court to determine on the evidence
presented to the court. I think it is a
great matter-I do not know whether the
Government are to be congratulated or
whether it is owing to the exceptional
nature of the legislation and they had to
do it that here we have a Bill, the first

Clogher Valley Railway Bill 204
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 7th October, 1941.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Paper Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Agriculture:
The Tillage (Northern Ireland) General
Order, 1941.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act), and ordered to lie on the Table.
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Repair of War Damage
Bill when it is brought from the House
of Commons."—(Mr. Robb.)
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Repair
of War Damage Bill, to which they desire
the concurrence of this House.
Repair of War Damage Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."--(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time." It is a short Bill
to amend the Housing (Emergency
Powers) Act, 1939. Under that Act, if
a local authority is satisfied that any
building, whether a house or not, is
rendered unfit for housing purposes by
reason of war damage, and the building
is capable, at a reasonable cost, of being
rendered fit for housing purposes, and
the owner of the building is unable or
unwilling to carry out the necessary
repairs, then the local authority serves.
notice that they intend to execute the
necessary repairs themselves. Under the
present Bill this requirement is removed.
Where it is necessary that alterations or
repairs should be made the local authority
will serve notice that they will do the
work, and unless the person owning or
having control of the building, within 14
days, says that he is willing and able to
do the work, the local authority may go
in and do it. Moreover, the Bill provides that a local authority need not wait
until the expiration of the period specified
in the notice if the owner gives earlier
intimation in writing to the local
authority that he does not object to the
repairs being carried out. It is a mere
question of saving time. The other
provisions of the Bill are merely
consequential.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Landlord and Tenant (War Damage)
Bill.
Order for Third Reading read.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."-(Mr. Robb.)
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill
without any amendment.
Clogher Valley Railway and Roads Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: This railway, originally
called the Clogher Valley Tramways, and
now called the Clogher Valley Railway,
was constructed under the authority of
a Provisional Order made under the
Tramways and Public Companies (Ireland) Act, 1883, and was opened for
traffic on the 2nd May, 1887. The railway has now outlived its usefulness, and
it is, therefore, proposed that it should
be closed down. The Measure to which
I invite the House to give a Second Reading to-day is intended to give statutory
effect to this decision.
In order to attract the capital necessary for the continuation of the undertaking, the Grand Juries of the counties
of Fermanagh and Tyrone, predecessors
of the County Councils, guaranteed the
payment by certain baronies of interest at
the rate of 5 per cent. on the paid-up
capital amounting to
to £123,331. In
addition, these baronies were pledged to
make good any deficiency between
revenue and expenditure on working and
maintaining the railway.
From the beginning the railway has
never been a financial success; it is best,
205 Clogher Valley Railway Bill-

7 OCTOBER 1941
Second Reading
therefore, that it should be discontinued.
In eleven years only, all prior to the
year 1914, the undertaking succeeded in
making revenue in excess of its expenditure on working and maintenance,
but revenue has never been sufficient
even to approach the sums required to
meet both ordinary expenditure and
interest on capital. The baronies were,
therefore, called upon to fulfil their
guarantees from the railway's inception.
It was recognised that in its early
years the railway might not be able to
pay its way, and the Government undertook under the original Order to contribute, in respect of the guaranteed
dividends, a sum amounting to one-half
of the sums paid by the baronies up to a
maximum of 2 per cent. on the paid-up
capital. The Government has, however,
always paid the maximum grant under
this provision. The burden imposed on
the ratepayers in the guaranteeing
baronies was so heavy that further
assistance could not be avoided, and,
under the Local Government (Ireland)
Act, passed in 1898, it was prescribed
that where the amount required to be
raised in any area in any financial year
to meet a railway charge connected with
the guarantee exceeded a sum equal to
sixpence in the pound on the rateable
value of the area, the Government might
pay out of the Local Taxation (Ireland)
Account a sum equal to one-half of that
excess. On the winding up of this
account in the year 1924 the Government
liability was continued by statute, but
was limited to 90 per cent. of one-half
of the excess. When agricultural land
was derated under the Act in 1929, the
liability of the ratepayers was further
reduced and transferred to the taxpayers.
Like most of the other railways in
Great Britain and Ireland, this railway
was operated under Government control
during the last war, and by the terms of
the settlement of Claims Act, 1921, a
sum of £16,723 was paid to the railway
by the Imperial Government. This sum
was used to defray the loss on working
and maintenance during the years 1922-
1925 inclusive, when it became exhausted.
The baronies were then faced with the
certainty of having to meet both very
heavy losses on working and interest on
the dividends.
A section of the Provisional Order
prescribed that where the baronies had
been called upon to pay during a period
of three years any money for maintaining or working the undertaking, that the.
undertaking and all the property of the
company connected with it should become
the property of the two county councils
and would, therefore, be operated by a
Joint Committee of Management. This
transfer of ownership was effected by an
Order made in 1928, and the railway
company has no longer any voice in the
management of the concern.
Notwithstanding the most rigid economy
no improvement in the financial position
of the undertaking could be effected. The
loss on working has exceeded £6,000 per
annum for many years, and, taken in
conjunction with a sum amounting to
£6,166 to pay the dividends, the railway
has had to be subsidised to the extent of
some £13,000 a year. Of this total, the
Government has been obliged to find some
£12,000, leaving a balance of some
£1,000 to be met by the ratepayers.
The effect of the policy of economy has
been reflected upon the condition of the
rolling stock, permanent way, workshops,
equipment and premises of the undertaking, and if the railway were continued a
large capital expenditure would immediately be required. It is, therefore, clearly
in the public interest that the line should
be closed. Alternative transport facilities
are already provided in the area by the
Northern Ireland Road Transport Board,
and a clear assurance has been given in
writing by the chairman of the Board
that the Board will be fully able to cater
for the traffic which is at present carried
by the railway.
The Bill now before the House provides for the discontinuance of the railway and the dissolution of the Joint Committee of Management. The affairs of
the undertaking will be wound up by a
liquidator, who will be required to see
that all rails, sleepers, posts, and other
property will be reinoved from the roads.
The expenses of the liquidator in performing all the duties imposed by the Bill will
be defrayed from the net proceeds of the
sale of the physical assets. The parts of
the public road taken originally for the
construction of the railway will be
restored to the highway authority in a
safe condition and any other land of the
undertaking which may be required for
road purposes will also be vested in the
county councils. Any land which is not
207 Clogher Valley Railway Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Robb]
wanted or cannot be disposed of in any
will revert to the original
way
other
owners.
The discontinuance of the railway will
of necessity entail the dismissal of the
employees, but, although the officers and
servants of the undertaking are not included in any superannuation scheme, the
Goverment proposes to make liberal provision for them in this respect. The cost of
compensation will be defrayed from the
proceeds of the physical assets, but, if
sufficient funds are not immediately available from this source, or should the
amount of the proceeds be ultimately inadequate to meet such liabilities the sums
so required to pay the compensation will
be paid out of moneys provided by
Parliament.
In addition, the Government proposes
to provide the money-£6,166-needed
for the payment of interest, and the
Tyrone County Council has offered to
undertake the distribution of the interest
to the shareholders.

un-
When this railway is closed, an
economie undertaking will have ceased to
exist, and a material saving will accrue to
the ratepayers of some £1,300 a year,
and ultimately to the taxpayers of some
£5,500 a year. I beg to move.
?
Mr. LESLIE: The discontinuance of
this railway has been twice recommended
within the last 10 years, and each time
I myself was a consenting party to the
recommendation. Therefore I need
scarcely say that in principle, at all
events, I support the Bill. The right
hon. Gentleman gave us
a very good
account, if I may say so, of the earlier
history of the railway, and I may,
perhaps, remind the House that if anyone
wants more information they will find it
in the Appendix to the Report of the
recent Transport Committee in the form
of a memorandum prepared by the
Ministry of Finance under the signature
of Mr. Duggan, and dated 18th March,
1939. There they will find any additional
information they are likely to require.
That being so, we have nothing to do but
to consider, to some extent, the details of
the measure before us.
First of all there is the question of the
discontinuance of the railway, not in
principle, because I think the House will
all agree that the sooner the railway is
discontinued the better, but the question of the time of the discontinuance. It is provided in the Bill
that discontinuance is to be at such time
as may be determined by the Ministry of
Home Affairs, and, therefore, having
regard to the present difficulties of transport, one is inclined to wonder whether
the intention is to do it at once or wait
until the conclusion of the war. The
difficulty is this. If you discontinue at
once and accept the assurance of the
chairman of the Road Transport Board
that facilities exist for carrying the
traffic, as no doubt in normal times they
do, you have to remember that at the
moment there is an acute shortage of
petrol, and the Transport Board may not
be in a position at the moment to implement their guarantee.
The discontinuance is to be from such
a date as the Minister of Home Affairs
may appoint, and that date is to known
as the appointed day. Hence it seems
to me to follow that the liquidator to be
appointed will not be able to commence
his operations until that appointed day;
that is to say, I am afraid he will have
no power to start his work and carry on
the railway meantime. The question is,
are you prepared to shut down the railway immediately or ought it to be kept
alive, so far as it can be kept alive, in
order to perform its carrying duties
during the present abnormal circum-
That is a point that ought to
be considered, and a word or two might
be added to the Bill to give the liquidator
I am
power to come in and carry on.
afraid that, as the Bill stands, he cannot
do this. That is the first point.
stances.
The second point is the question of the
vesting of this land. I do not myself
know the route of this railway, but is
there any land concerned the bed and soil
of which still belongs to a private owner,
because if there is it seems to me that as
the Bill runs you are proposing to transfer
some of that land to the county council,
and that you cannot do. That is another
point that requires attention. We have
had this question of bed and soil at least
once before in this House.
The only other point is this: that in
the rather unlikely event of a dispute
arising as to right of access to the new
or altered road, any difficulty that may
arise is to be settled by the Ministry of

Major BOYLE: The route of this
railway is probably better known to me
than to some other Members of this House.
In many places it runs alongside the road,
and there are many level crossings. I
would like to support what Senator Leslie
has said regarding the appointed day for
closing this railway. In Northern Ireland we are very short of transport, and
it occurs to me that any transport that
is in being should be kept in being
instead of our being entirely dependent on
the Northern Ireland Road Transport
Board. No doubt that Board is doing its
best, but it does not satisfy all of us on
all occasions, if I may put it in that way.
It appears to me to be desirable that
this railway should be kept running for
the present, as it carries a good deal of
produce and a good many people. The
trains run three or four times each way
each day, and they connect with the Great
Northern Railway at Maguiresbridge, and
at Tynan at this end. I often pass the
train, and I often get out of its way.
Mr. ROBB: Do you ever ride in it?
Major BOYLE: I have travelled in
it, but not since motor traffic became
available. I think the railway should be
kept in being until the present emergency
is over, and more transport is available.
The Road Transport Board tell you they
will give you more facilities, but they
cannot do it. I have seen their storehouses
packed with merchandise and the Transport Board hold it up for their lorries.
If that can happen in normal times these
unfortunate people will be greater
sufferers than they are at present when
this railway is closed. Therefore, I hope
the Government will consider the
advisability of keeping the railway in
operation until more transport facilities
are available.

will be a lot of disputes over the closing

person to settle matters finally. If there
is a dispute the public and the owners
of the land affected are entitled to a legal
decision. I ask the Government seriously
to consider this point and to strike out
the word "final" in the appropriate clause.
Mr. ROBB: With regard to the hon.
and learned Member's point about the
bed and soil of the land through which
this railway runs, all the land is vested
in the County Council. It is not a
question of way-leave. The land itself
is vested.
Mr. LESLIE: Throughout the route?
Mr. ROBB: Yes. With regard to the
fixing of the appointed day the position
is this. It has been left fluid in the Bill
for this reason: that the permanent way
and rolling stock are in a very bad state
and it is not possible at the moment to
say for how long it can be carried on in
its present state. It is not contemplated
that any sum worth mentioning should
be expended on the railway, and the date
of the appointed day will be controlled
by the view taken by the expert officers
of the Government as to when it will be
desirable to close the line.
Major DOBBS: Having regard to the
importance of the petrol question?
Mr. ROBB: All the facts will be
taken into consideration, but the state of
the rolling stock and the permanent way
is such that risks are being run at the
moment, and the Government is not prepared to run these risks indefinitely.
With regard to the question of who
should arbitrate in disputes that may
arise between the owner and occupiers of
adjoining land and the County Council
as to the works that will be performed, it
is only on that question that the Ministry

Questions to Minister.
SHORTAGE OF MATCHES.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to ask the Leader
of the House whether he is aware that
there is an acute shortage of matches in
Northern Ireland, and whether any steps
are in contemplation to mitigate the inconvenience. arising from such shortage.
I think this question explains itself.
I am not asking anything as to the cause
of the shortage of matches. I presume
the right hon. Gentleman is aware that
there is a shortage, and I am asking what
steps, if any, are being taken in order to
mitigate the inconvenience, which is very
great. Subject to anything the Registrar-
General may have to say regarding his
view of the situation, we were always
under the impression that Northern Ireland was a match-making area, and the
recent shortage in country districts is not
understood. Hence, my question.
con-
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST:
Perhaps you will allow me to say a few
words on this question in view of the fact
that I have knowledge which the Members
of this House do not possess, as I am a
director of Messrs. Maguire & Patterson,
the match manufacturers. As far as
Messrs. Maguire & Patterson are
cerned, they have large supplies. There
are a few thngs they are short of, but
they have sufficient materials to carry
them on for a considerable time. But
the position is this. The match trade is
entirely under the control of the. Board
of Trade, Manchester and London Control
Office, and deliveries can only be made
under licence. All customers' orders
have to be submitted direct to the Match
Control Office in London for their
approval, and deliveries cannot be made.
without their instructions.
The match quota is only 45 per cent.
of the customers' purchases for the 12
months ending March 1st, 1940. We
have had the matter under discussion
time and again, but we are in exactly
the same position as those across the
water. We are controlled by the Board
of Trade, and we have to comply with
their regulations, and, as I have already
said, while we have supplies and could
make larger deliveries we cannot do anything but comply with the decisions of
the Board. There is exactly the same
shortage in London as there is here, and
I cannot hold out any hope of any further
deliveries here until such time as the
Order is altered and a further release is
made in Northern Ireland.
Mr. ROBB: Matches are in short
supply throughout the United Kingdom,
and in this respect the position in
Northern Ireland is certainly no worse
than in Great Britain.
About 50 per cent. of the United Kingdom's peace-time consumption of matches
was imported, and the stopping of this
trade has necessarily resulted in a
shortage of supplies. The regulations
made by the Match Control Board are
designed to ensure to retailers a specified
percentage of their purchases during the
basic period. So far as is known,
further regulation is contemplated.
no
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
14 OCTOBER 1941
Mr. LESLIE: Is it proposed to make
any representations to the Control Board
in London, because it would seem from
what has been said that there is an artificially produced shortage.
Mr. ROBB: If I am asked whether
this Government is going to make representations to the Government in England
to give Northern Ireland a larger proportion of matches than is given in England,
the answer is "No."
COMPULSORY TILLAGE ORDER.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Ministry of Agriculture
will consider the advisability of varying
the requirements of the Compulsory
Tillage Order, according to the several
districts of Northern Ireland, having
regard to the absence of sunshine, and
the consequential non-ripening of the
grain crops.
Major DOBBS: In asking this question
I am speaking for the farmers in those
districts of Northern Ireland where the
climate is anything but kindly. When
the Compulsory Tillage Order came out
in 1939, provided the tractors could be
obtained there was no reason why the
huge acreage aimed at could not be
ploughed, but personally I was very
doubtful if that huge acreage could be
harvested. In England, harvesting can
sometimes be done in one day. It is possible to cut wheat and thrash it the very
same day. That is an absolute impossibility over here. Harvesting here is a
very long process-cutting, stooking up
the sheaves, throwing them down again to
get the sun into them. It is not a question of a day; it may be a question of
days and days, and even weeks. However, last year we had a wonderful
autumn, and the harvest was saved. In
1938 I had only three acres of oats, and
to save those oats we took the sheaves and
threw them over the hedge into a grass
field, and carried them from there and
stooked them in the hay barns. By that
means we saved that crop. That is quite
possible in the case of three acres, but it
is quite impossible for 100 acres, or even
20 acres. It is a question of sun and
daylight.
Thirty years ago there was no wheat
grown here, and if you asked a farmer
why he would probably answer you in
these words, that his father grew wheat
but he could not; that in his father's
time there was a great deal more sun to
ripen the wheat than there is in his time.
There is not the same sun now as there
was sixty years ago. In England you
can start cutting your wheat probably at
the end of July. In County Antrim you
certainly cannot start cutting it until
the end of August-this year not before the beginning of September. The
earliest oats were not ripe until the
middle of September. What exactly
does that mean? It means this: that in
those favoured parts of England if the
weather is not for the moment good they
can have August and September to do
their harvesting in. But we have not
got a month extra to do it in. We have
not got a week, and it becomes
calamitous.
Take my own case which is also the
case of others. I had the whole of the
grain crops cut and carted and put into
stacks by 3rd October this year. At the
present moment I have still some barley
and oats to cut. I have ninety per cent.
of barley and oats to cart; that is, they
are not saved. I was through Islandmagee on Friday and it was pitiful to see
the fields of oats not cut, and oats
knocked down by the rain. I do not
know if they will ever be able to cut
them. These farmers, who are very
hard-working, intelligent, and go-ahead,
are going to be faced with great losses.
It is all a question of sun. People may
say, "Why did you not cut your grain
crops when the weather was good?"
answer is that we cut as soon as we could.
You cannot cut grain until it is ripe.
While it was fine we cut on the Saturday
half-holidays and we cut on the Sundays.
I am glad to say that arrangements were
made in that way and the men were willing to work on Sundays.
My
It is a question of sun and weather
that the farmers are up against, and it is
about time the Ministry of Agriculture
realised that it is not so easy to carry
out their instructions. It may be easy
to say on paper that you must plough up
40, 50, or 60 per cent. more land this
year, but in practice it is not so easy.
Mr. ROBB: After the most careful
consideration, the Ministry of Agri-
Clogher Valley Railway Bill 220

culture has not found it possible to vary,
by districts, the terms of the Tillage
Order covering the 1942 season.
I may say that the Order had the
unanimous support of the Council of the
Ulster Farmers' Union.
Mr. LESLIE: Is it really suggested
that the Ulster Farmers' Union represent the farmers of Northern Ireland?
GRASS LAND AS TILLED GROUND.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the Leader
of the House whether the Minister of Agriculture in his Compulsory Tillage Order
considers that ground laid down to grass
in 1941 will be reckoned as tilled ground
for 1942.
Mr. ROBB: I am asked to make it
clear that land laid down to grass in 1941
I will not be reckoned as under cultivation
in 1942. The new Tillage Order contains
an express provision to this effect.
Major DOBBS: I think that is the
most astonishing thing I have ever heard
in my life.
Mr. SPEAKER: Does the hon. and
gallant Member wish to ask a supplementary question?
Major DOBBS: Yes, although it is a
little hard to put what I would like to
say in a supplementary question. One
is rather placed in the position of not
trying to lecture, but I think the Ministry of Agriculture should be lectured by
farmers on this matter. I am really so
surprised at the answer to my question
that I am unable at the moment to frame
a suitable supplementary question.
Mr. LESLIE: Can any reason be
given for this departure from previous.
Tillage Orders? Hitherto land sown out
to grass, land sown out to oats in order
that a seed hay crop can be taken in the
succeeding year has always been regarded
as tillage. In the days of the late
T. W. Russell, who was Minister of
Agriculture for the whole of Ireland during the last war, the Tillage Order then
provided that grass should be reckoned
as tillage in the second year. The wording of the Order does not seem to carry
out exactly what the right hon. Gentleman has said. The phrase used in the
Order is "land laid down to grass and
I
hay," not hay and grass, and I think
there is a very distinct difference.
cannot but believe that land sown out
with a view that grass seed may be saved
in the ensuing year is under cultivation,
whatever view the Ministry of Agricul
ture may take, and, therefore, under the
Order that is the view that will be taken
unless we can have some further information.
It makes a very considerable, difference.
If a man is working on a 30 acres of
ploughing plan-and that is a rather
large acreage for most farmers in Northern Ireland-it will follow that he will
have coming forward for grass seed 30
acres next year, and if that is not going
to count as tillage. it will make a surprising difference. It will raise the whole
question of the compulsory area. It
cannot be contended that a 40 per cent.
requirement with hay cut out of it is a
feasible proposition. Therefore I would
like to ask if there is any reason for this.
Mr. ROBB: I do not know, but if a
further question is put down I will
endeavour to have the information. I
would have liked to have had the Minister
of Agriculture here, but unfortunately
it is not possible.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I would like to
ask if this Order has the approval of the
Ulster Farmers' Union; what percentage
of the farmers of Northern Ireland the
Ulster Farmers' Union represents; and
the amount of subsidy paid to the Ulster
Farmers' Union by the Government.
Mr. ROBB: I would need notice of
that question.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Hear, hear.

In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSES 1 (Discontinuance of the
railway), and 2 (Cesser of the Joint Committee and appointment of a liquidator
to wind up the undertaking) ordered to
stand part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 3 (Powers and duties of the
liquidator.)
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, at the
end of Clause 3, to add as a new subclause:
"(4) Subject to the approval of the
Ministry of Home Affairs and notwithstanding anything contained in subsection (1) of section one of this Act,
the liquidator shall have power to
continue, as a temporary expedient,
such working and maintenance of the
undertaking to such extent as may,
during the winding up of the undertaking, appear to the liquidator to be
in the interests of the public."
This suggested amendment is intended.
to deal with a matter to which I directed
attention on the Second Reading. That
is, without in any way extending or prolonging the life of the railway, which is
not at all the intention, to provide for
what is to happen between the date of
discontinuance as per Ministry Order, and
the final winding up under the supervision
of the liquidator. The liquidator does
not come into being until the Order for
the discontinuance of the railway has been
made, and although the Ministry can do
a good deal in the meantime, such as to
give public notice that on and after such
a date the railway is to be discontinued
and no more trains will run, and then
perhaps get the stock sorted about up
and down the line, but immediately he
has discontinued the railway the liquidator is to come in and take steps for
realising the assets and so on, but I
cannot find that he has got any power
to do anything else.
It may be that he will want to move his
trucks from point A to point B for convenience of sale, but he does not seem to
have the power to do that or anything
else. According to the Bill, the only
powers he seems to have are those given
under Clause 3 and Clause 7, and I do
not think there is anything in Clause 7
that will enable him to do the necessary
carrying on from day to day. The
powers by Clause 3 as it stands are distinctly limited to the collection and discharge of debts, and the sale of the
property and assets of the undertaking.
There does not seem to be any power to
do any of the other things required. I
have prepared my amendment in such a
way that anything the liquidator did do
must have the approval of the Ministry
of Home Affairs. The Minister has
power to close the railway, but he has not
power to carry it on in the meantime.
The only powers would seem to be those
under this Joint Committee Order of
1928, which I have not seen. It was in
my mind to wonder if someone could see
in that Order power to enable the liquidator to do something apart from the
accounting part of his duties. To that
end I put down the amendment, which is
rather verbose in some ways and might
perhaps be shortened a little, but it is
intended to confer powers on the liquidator which I think are necessary, because
he must have some powers of that sort.
There is a second amendment which I put
separately, and in that second amendment
there is a misprint. It should be Section
3 and not Section 2. That is really consequential on the first amendment, and,
of course, if the first amendment is not
accepted it will not be necessary to move
the second. It does seem to me necessary
that we should do something to clothe
the liquidator with powers to wind up
the business on the physical side as distinguished from the book-keeping side,
which is all he has got at present.
Mr. ROBB: The hon. and learned
Senator has put an interpretation on his
amendment that I had not thought of
when I read it before. I thought his
amendment was directed to the suggestion
that the liquidator might be enabled to
carry on the railway as a railway carrying
passengers and goods for remuneration.

The liquidator shall, for the purposes

CLAUSE 6 (Execution of works for

Any difference or dispute arising
between an owner or occupier of
adjoining land and a county council
as to the necessity for, or the nature
or extent of, any works required by a
notice under this section shall be
determined by the Ministry of Home
Affairs, and the determination of the
said Ministry shall be final.
Major BOYLE: I beg to move, in
Clause 6, sub-clause (2), to leave out the
word "final" and add the words,
"subject to an appeal to the County
Court Judge of the county in which the
lands concerned are situate." This
Amendment requires little explanation.
As the Clause stands, when any dispute
or difference arises between the railway
and the neighbouring owners the
arbitrator will be the Ministry of Home
Affairs. The largest shareholder of the
railway is the Ministry of Finance, which
has been purchasing the shares and is
now the owner of about a quarter of the
shares. Therefore the Ministry of Finance
is practically the owner of the railway,
and is still offering to buy the shares at
par value. The other parties to any dispute may be any of the adjoining owners
of the land on this 37 miles of railway
with thirty stations, passing in and out
of farms and running on and across the
road. In the case of four villages the
railway runs right down the street. It
is sometimes above the roadway and
sometimes on the surface, so that many
cases of dispute may arise.
As the Bill now stands the. Ministry
will be the final arbitrator. My Amendment asks that the Ministry should not
be the final arbitrator, but that any
people who are dissatisfied with the deci
sion of the Ministry should have the right
to appeal to the. county court judge. I
would ask this Committee to accept the
Amendment so
as to give these small
farmers and these small
owners and
occupiers of houses on this 37 miles of
railway the ordinary right of appeal to
the county court in which they have
every confidence. There may be no cases
225 Clogher Valley Railway Bill-

14 OCTOBER 1941
Committee
where people are dissatisfied with the.
decision of the Ministry, but, on the other
hand, there may be some, and I do not
see why the Government should take
away their civil right of appeal.
Mr. ROBB: The Government does not
wish to accept this Amendment, and I
think it is unnecessary. What the Section
says is this:
Where any land forming part of the
railway has reverted to, or become, part
of, a public road under section four of
this Act, the council of, the county in
which such land is situated shall, if
required, by notice in writing, by any
person being the owner or occupier of
any land which adjoins the first mentioned land, execute such construction
or repair of fences, gates, conduits and
other works as may be reasonably necessary for the enjoyment, or security of
the adjoining land owned or occupied
by that person, in consequence of the
first mentioned land having reverted to,
or become part of, a public road; and
the county council shall execute such
works at their own expense with all
convenient speed.
Any dispute that is going to arise is a
dispute between the county council and
the owner of the adjoining land as to
what sort of fence or gates should be put
up. These are all very small matters,
and what is proposed is that the Ministry
of Home Affairs, which has the general
superintendence of roads, should be the.
arbitrator as between the county council
and the owner. Although there cannot
be much objection to carrying the matter
further to the county court, the matters
in dispute must be so small that they can
safely be left to the arbitration of the
Ministry of Home Affairs.
It is not an
Amendment I would oppose very
strenuously, on principle, but I really do
not think it is necessary, and on that
ground the Government is not prepared
to accept it.

think that these small farmers in Tyrone
and Fermanagh have the same, confidence

Laid before the House (pursuant to

Questions to Minister.
ACRES UNDER THE PLOUGH.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major Dobbs :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state the total number
of acres under the plough, and the
number of acres respectively sown with
wheat, barley, oats, a mixed grain
crop, and flax, in the County of
Antrim, or in Northern Ireland.
Major DOBBS: In asking the questions
I have put on the Paper I am hoping
they will bring forth two or three very
important points. I am sure we are all
extremely glad to see present the Minister
of Agriculture, and if the Minister will
take the very critical farmers of Northern
Ireland into his confidence as far as he
can I am sure it will be the very best
thing for the national effort and for us
here. The first point I want to put is
with regard to the growing of wheat.
The growing of wheat in Northern Ireland
is different to what it is elsewhere. It
should be sown in September or October,
and therefore it is not much good to the
farmers of Northern Ireland if the
Minister of Agriculture tells us in
October that he would like wheat to be
grown, or tells us the quota. We want
to settle in July or August what we are
going to do in the ensuing year.
The second point I want to refer to is
the question of the cleanliness of the
ground. We are growing at the present
time an immense acreage of flax and
cereals. You cannot go on growing
cereals until your land becomes foul. It
was all right for 1940; I think it is all
right for 1941; and I think it will be all
right for 1942, but what about 1943 and
1944 ? The land will then have to be
cleaned. How are you going to do it?
There are two methods of cleaning land.
One is very largely used in the South of
England and is called summer fallow.
That is, you grow nothing on the land
for a whole year. You use the whole
year for harrowing the weeds and trying
to get the land clean. But could you let
all that land that has during the past
two or three years been sown in cereals
go to waste, those thousands
thousands and thousands of acres?
and
The other chief method is to put in
roots. Supposing in 1943 we all put in
roots. It is going to be a pretty difficult
thing to do because of the labour
problem; but, suppose it is done, you are
going to have in 1943 thousands and
thousands of acres of roots. What on
earth are we going to do with those roots?
Up to now farmers have always been
used to working on some sort of very
definite crop rotation. The policy adopted
in the years 1939, 1940 and 1941-and
I do not think anything else could have
been done has entirely smashed every
known crop rotation. Has the Minister
of Agriculture thought out this point?
What does he mean to do? I hope he
will tell us. I really think that we
farmers of Northern Ireland are entitled
to this information, and it would
certainly be a very great help to us.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The answer to the hon. and gallant
Member's first question, with which I
have been supplied, is this:-
The Minister of Agriculture regrets
his inability to furnish the figures requested by the hon. and gallant Senator.
Under war conditions Departments have
been requested by the Imperial Government not to publish such official statistics.
PROPORTIONS OF CEREAL CROPS.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can
state what are the proportions (in percentages of the total acreage under the
plough) of wheat, barley, oats, roots,
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
potatoes, and flax, that the Minister of
Agriculture will consider as satisfactory
for the years 1942 and 1943.
Mr. ROBB: The Ministry's aim is an
all-round increase in the ploughed area,
but it is not intended to interfere with
the farmer's right to choose the crops
which he will grow. It is hoped that
there will be increases in the areas of all
the crops mentioned, but I would not
care to attempt to forecast the result of
the free choice of farmers in this matter.
Farmers in Northern Ireland have been
asked since the outbreak of war to make
their farms self-supporting, and that
means big increases in the acreage of all
crops which are used for live stock
feeding.
SUCCESSIVE GRAIN CROPS.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can state
the maximum number of years that any
field in the British Isles, outside Rothamstead, and other experimental stations,
has successively and successfully carried
a grain crop; and how many successive
grain crops does the Minister of Agriculture consider can be successfully grown
on average arable land in Northern
Ireland.
Mr. ROBB: I regret that the information sought in the first part of the question is not available. The Ministry is of
opinion that, where facilities do not exist
for putting the land through a rotation
of crops, not more than two successive
grain crops should under ordinary circumstances be grown on arable land of average quality in Northern Ireland before it
is again seeded down. In the case of
first-class pasture three grain crops can
be taken, but the decision in any
particular case will naturally depend upon
the quality of the land, the extent to
which manures are applied to the grain
crops and the freedom of the land from
weeds.
PERENNIAL RYE GRASS AS
TILLAGE.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major Dobbs:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the first year sowing of perennial rye-grass will count as tilled
ground in 1942; and whether it is a
fact that perennial rye-grass is extensively grown for its heavy feeding value
in parts of Scotland, being cut as a hay
crop.
Major DOBBS: With regard to this
fourth question, I am asking about perennial rye-grass to cut as hay, not to use as
seed, either to cut as hay or to graze. It
is quite different to the next question on
the Paper. I hope the Minister of Agriculture will see his way to allow this
ground that has been sown up to perennial rye-grass or some other grass of that
description to be counted as tilled ground.
I want to put a very important point to
the right hon. Gentleman, and it is this.
A great number of farmers in Northern
Ireland are faced with the difficulty of
the weather. The weather has been very,
very bad, and if you allow this land to
be counted as tilled ground it will enable
the farmer, when he puts in his oats or
wheat, to sow it down to grass, and
directly he has cut it as grazing he will
in the early part of the autumn be able to
turn it up and put it straight into wheat.
What the farmers in Northern Ireland,
and especially those in Co. Antrim, are
faced with is this; that they have not
been able to harvest their crops. A
great glut of oats and other cereals is
still out in the fields of Co. Antrim,
uncut and battered down. In an excellent harvest year this land could have
been ploughed up and put into winter
wheat, but this year it has been quite
impossible to do this. I hope the Minister
will give the farmer this chance so that
he will be able to plough up these wheat
fields early in the autumn and put down
winter wheat, which will be very impor
tant next year.
Mr. ROBB: I would refer the hon.
and gallant Senator to the answer given
last Tuesday to the effect that grass or
hay, whether it be first crop or not, will
not count as cultivation for the purposes
of the Tillage Order covering the season
1942. The high feeding value of perennial rye-grass in both pastures and hay
is generally recognised, and it is a fact
that this grass forms an important constituent of most crops of first year hay
in Northern Ireland and in
Scotland.
both
Major DOBBS: May I ask the Minister
of Agriculture if he will consider at his

Questions to Minister
21 OCTOBER 1941
diffe

to E
leisure the points I have put and try to
help the farmers-not all the farmers of
Northern Ireland, but some of them.
Where the harvest is good I do not think

are very hard hit at all times when the

The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Lord Glentoran): I need not tell you
that I have the greatest sympathy with
the statements made by the hon. and
gallant Senator, because we all know that
there never has been, probably within the
memory of man, a worse harvest than
that our farmers are now struggling with.
There is a grain crop out, and we know
that no matter how much help we have
we can do nothing for it. All farmers
know that if they had a million hands all
they could do would be to cart it in and
turn it into manure. As it is, the only
safe place for it is in the field. A lot of
the old farmers say: "God is good.
There will always be a harvest, and if we
only have faith we will find that we shall
get in much more of this crop than we
think we shall." I have not got to look
far on my own land to see the sad sights
the hon. and gallant Member has mentioned, because I am suffering from all
the difficulties he has referred to.
The hon. and gallant Member raised
one or two interesting points. The first
was the sort of crop to be grown. In
England, as hon. Members know, it is
compulsory to grow a particular crop.
Officials of the Ministry of Agriculture
go round the farms and tell a man what
he shall grow in a particular field. I
have much more regard for my Ulster
farmers. I think they know better than
I do what they can grow on their own
land, because no one knows better than
the farmer himself what he can successfully grow on his own land. Therefore,
we have adopted that policy, and it has
worked very well up to now. The other
point he raised is the very difficult one
of how often you can grow a grain crop in
any field. I am old-fashioned and I think
two grain crops is plenty in any field, but
it must be remembered that we are now
at war, and if the Government want
grain for the people we have got to get
it for them.
In a race we often ask a horse to do
what we would not think of asking it to
do if we were riding down a lane. If
that is so in racing, it is a million times
more so when we are at war. It is a
matter of much concern to me as to what
will happen to these dirty fields, particularly on strong growing land such as
there is in County Antrim. There is one
thing that saves us here more than it
does in the case of the tillage lands in
England, and that is that we have a
great deal of grass to get over before it
is exhausted, and what the farmers are
doing is to take two crops before sowing
grass again. If the time comes when we
cannot do that, then we shall be faced
with a great deal of difficulty.
On my own farm I have experimented
in growing catch crops. As soon as the
flax was done I put down a catch crop
and fed sheep on it this year. Then I
ploughed it up and sowed it down in
grain, and that experiment was very
successful. That may be a way out of
our difficulty, but that is only one of
many ways in which we will have to
experiment to get the land clean.
The other point the hon. and gallant
Member raised was very much what I
am speaking of now, because land is constantly growing a perennial crop as a
catch crop. They clean the land, put
grass down and either graze or cut it,
and then plough and manure it. I have
been asked by the hon. and gallant
Member if I will sympathetically consider
the points he mentioned, and I most
certainly will. It is a very great pleasure
to me to come to this House and to see
that there is such interest being taken
and such knowledge shown in these
matters, which are of very great
importance.
FIELDS SOWN FOR GRASS SEED.

scribing the area of tillage required in

THE SENATE
Questions to Minister

Questions to Minister
does not seem to be much difference, but
the word tillage is not used in that sense.
My question is whether it is in contemplation not to continue to regard a
field sown for grass-seed as being a field
in tillage.
Mr. LESLIE: The House will recognise the difficulty of this question,
because since I put it down it has come
to my notice that the Order to which I
refer has been made, and this afternoon
I have been able to obtain a copy of it.
But the Order does not correspond
altogether with my recollection of the
notice that appeared in the newspapers
to which reference was made when we
last met when we spoke about grass and
hay. I raised the question as to whether
the expression "grass and hay" was intended to apply to what is commonly
called seeds, that being the first year's
sowing out. The first part of the question disappears, because the Order has
been made and laid on the Table and is
now available. It appears to be dated
1st October. This is the 21st, but it was
not available last Tuesday.
The other part of the question is,
whether it is in contemplation in that
order to depart from the established practice of regarding a field sown for grassseed as being a field in tillage. That
contains the assertion that it was the
established practice, and I think those
hon. Members who know will support me
in saying that it was so regarded last
year and has been so regarded throughout
this war. In the last war not only was
the first year's sowing regarded as tillage,
but the first year's grass after that was
also considered tillage. The noble Lord's
predecessor abandoned the first year's
grass, but he did not abandon the first
sowing of the seeds, and you ought not
to change your practice in that respect
without giving about a year's notice. I
sowed out land with a mixture suitable
for taking grass next year and I imagined
that that would be reckoned as tillage.
If that is upset it makes it very difficult.
If you do not have grass-seed sown you
will presently run short of grass. That
is fairly obvious.
If you change your horse when crossing
a stream you will-I cannot exactly say
break faith with the farmers, because
that is an expression too casually thrown
about, but you will do something not expected of you, and you will be departing
from what has become the established
practice. A field sown for grass-seed is
just as much in cultivation as a field sown
for winter wheat. You sow winter wheat
in October this year and you reap it next
year. As far as tillage is concerned there

There seems to be some confusion with

Up to last year a man was allowed to
count grass-seed as tillage if it was in his
regular rotation-if he had oats, then

regular rotation. If it was in the

Mr. LESLIE indicated dissent.
Lord GLENTORAN: In the case of a

Lord GLENTORAN: I think the hon.

You may very well ask why
we have done this. I will tell you. We
are asking 40 per cent. more tillage this
year. If we had allowed all this grass
land to count as tillage we would have
had to ask the farmers to plough 45 per
cent. more in order to get the amount of
tillage we require. Therefore, we would.
have been striking a heavy blow at the
little tillage farmer, who is least able to
bear it. In Northern Ireland the little
tillage farmer is pretty well tilled up to
the teeth, if I may put it that way. To
save him five per cent., it was worth
while to make the grazier plough more.
That is the way the Ministry and our
farmers look at it, and that is the reason
we have done this. It certainly was
important to the small regular tillage
farmer that he should have the benefit of
this five per cent., although it means that
the bigger men, who ploughed up their
grass, are caught.
Mr. LESLIE: Is your small farmer
more or less expected to till 50 per cent.
of his holding? The difference between
45 per cent. and 50 per cent. is not very
much to the small farmer of, say, 50
acres.
Lord GLENTORAN: I was referring
to the 20-acre man.

encourage people to grow Italian rye
grass I think they did. I strongly

Road Transport Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time.' 22
It
arises in this way. Under Section 16 of
the Road and Railway Transport Act, the
power to carry merchandise for hire and
reward in Belfast and Londonderry is
limited to the Belfast Corporation, the
Northern Ireland Road Transport Board,
and local carriers licensed by the Ministry
of Home Affairs. Under Section 16,
sub-section (5) of that Act it is provided
that the rates paid by local carriers and
the conditions of employment of their
employees shall be not less favourable to
these persons than the wages which would
be payable and the conditions that would
be observed under contracts complying
with the fair wages clause. It was clearly
the intention of Parliament that the
penalty provided by sub-section (7) of
Section 16 of that Act should be applicable where successful prosecutions were
undertaken for failure to pay proper
wages or for imposing conditions less
favourable than would be observed under
contracts complying with the fair wages
clause, but in a case that came before the
Deputy Recorder of Belfast it was held
that the Section was defective in its wording and the penalty did not apply in
cases where obviously it was intended to
apply.
Mr. LESLIE: The Section, or the subsection?
It was
Mr. ROBB: The sub-section.
intended to apply to both offences, but
in the wording of the sub-section the
word "user" only was used, and the
penalty could not be imposed, although it
was found that the man had not observed
the fair wages clause. This Bill is intended to remedy that.
Mr. LESLIE: I think everybody will
be in agreement with what the right hon.
Gentleman has said. It was clearly the
intention of Parliament when Section 16
was passed that these wages should be
paid, but it is not quite so plain now
when apparently you have had a judicial
decision that that particular sub-section
is unenforceable, and does not apply. But
it is not clear what the intention of this
Bill is. The main intention is to ensure
the payment of these wages in future,
but is it the intention, or is it proposed
that this Bili should treat the learned
Deputy Recorder in the same way that
Parliament treated the Court of Crown
Case Reservation in the Preamble to the
Territorial Waters Jurisdiction Act which
as the right hon. Gentleman is aware
followed the Franconia case. The effect
of that was to say, "Whereas so and so
exists and has always existed.' Is it the
intention to say that the law was always
as this Bill makes it, or is it intended to
make a new law?
Mr. ROBB: To make a new law.
Mr. LESLIE: In one or two instances
I do not agree with proposals in the Bill,
and in one particular case I do not think
steps have been taken to produce what is
intended. If the House will look at the
Bill I will point out the defects I see.
One of them is, I am afraid, rather
important, but the others, I think, will
be matters for consultation. At the end
of the last line on the first page there is
this fine of £100. Undoubtedly £100
was the maximum penalty provided for a
breach of Section 16, but it has been held
apparently that that penalty does not
Road Transport Bill-
Second Reading
21 OCTOBER 1941
apply to a breach of the conditions as to
fair wages, and now it is proposed to do
something to correct that.
If you are going to do it that way,
your maximum penalty of £100 is out
of proportion. The penalty for a similar
offence under the Agricultural Wages
(Labourers) Act is £20. The penalty
under the Imperial Road Haulage Act,
to which this Bill owes a great deal, is
£20, and I think that really £20 is the
proper figure in this Bill, and not £100.
As it is a maximum penalty and will be
administered by a court of summary
jurisdiction, I cannot help thinking that
you might get over that by merely recording the fact that we have noticed
that, but I should not like it to be taken
as a precedent.
Section 16 broke down. There was no
doubt about our intention about those
wages. Whether it is right or wrong
does not matter, but we provided in that
section 16 not only for this penalty of
£100, but also that licences should be
issued to these men to do their carrying,
and that licences should be on such conditions, and subject to revocation under
such conditions as the Minister of Home
Affairs might issue by Regulations.
I wonder if the Minister of Home Affairs
did issue Regulations, and, if not, I
wonder why he did not do so. Section
16 became law in 1935. In 1937, what
is called the Baillie Committee had
reported. Those of us who were members
of the Road and Rail Transport Committee will remember that, and also the
recommendation of that Transport Committee, paragraph 190, to the effect that
representations should be made that these
wages were not being paid. There was
no method to enforce them, and in the
opinion of the Committee the wages ought
to be paid, and there ought to be a
method of enforcing them. The Committee recommended that arrangements
should be made accordingly.
The Transport Committee reported.
on 27th June, 1939. This is 21st
October, 1941, and it is only just going
to be done.
rather fancy
I
that
some people went on strike in Belfast,

of whom there are about 400, including

I do think we ought to insert here, now
if necessary, that there should be power
to revoke a licence if the proper wages
are not paid. The sub-section I have
quoted was in the Act of 1935, and does
not appear to have been used for this purpose, and it may be that it was held
by the Deputy Recorder that if the
penalty did not apply, neither did the
power of revocation. If we are making
a new law, it is our duty to provide for
that power of revocation by adding a few
words in Committee. That is all I have
got to say about the actual Bill.
We now pass to the Schedule. The
Schedule did not make any serious
difference, but it does support the right
hon. Gentleman's suggestion that we are
making a new law. If we look at paragraph 2 of the Schedule, it seems to me
there is a difficulty there. If the employer
fails to pay the recognised wages, and
notice of intention to do so has been served
with the summons, evidence may be given
that he has done the same thing at any
time during the preceding six months,
and the court may order the employer
to pay the difference between the wages
he should have paid and the wages
actually paid during those six months.
Is it intended that this paragraph should
be retrospective? The Bill has not yet.
become an Act, but it will shortly, and
anybody who does not pay the proper
wages after that will be liable to these
pains and penalities. But is it intended
that it should go back to the last six
months, which may be four months
before the Bill was passed. If that is
the intention we ought to say so. The
right hon. Gentleman will remember
another criminal case in which it was
held that the penalty should be retrospective, but we ought to make it clear
whether we intend that this penalty
should operate retrospectively or not.
The next paragraph I want to draw
attention to is paragraph 4.
The Ministry of Labour may appoint
such officers as it thinks necessary for
the purpose of investigating complaints and otherwise securing the
proper observance of this Act.

Repair of War Damage Bill 246
Finance Bill
21 OCTOBER 1941
Resolved, "That this Bill do now
pass."
(Mr. Robb.)
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill without any amendment.
Finance Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: The purpose of this Bill
is to give legislative effect to the minor
changes in taxation which were announced
by the Minister of Finance last April,
and to certain other concessions which
follow the British Finance Act.
Clause 1 amends the existing provisions
relating to the taxation of agricultural
vehicles. The Bill proposes to allow a
farmer to obtain a licence for 5s for his
tractor, for use on the public road in
hauling, between different parts of the
agricultural land in his occupation, agricultural produce or articles required for
his land. An amendment, made in
another place, has extended the scope of
the original Bill by allowing the farmer
also to haul to his own farm turf for his
own domestic, as well as for agricultural,
`use, from a place where he has turbary
rights.
The farmer who only uses his tractor
on the public road for passing between.
different parts of his land, and does not
exceed a distance of six miles on the
public road in a week, can, of course,
continue to have his tractor or motor
lorry exempted from tax under the
Finance Act of 1937.
Sub-clause (b) is intended merely to
make quite clear the legal position regarding the exemption from taxation of
A.R.P. trailer pumps.
Clause 2 extends to members of the
Merchant Navy the concession in regard
to short-term motor vehicle licences
granted last year to men of the fighting
services home on leave. A licence may
be taken out by them, for a maximum
period of three weeks, on payment of 10s
for a motor car and 2s for a motor
bicycle.
Clause 3 exempts from estate duty
gifts to H.M. Treasury during the war.
Clause 4 allows to all persons killed by
war operations the same death duty relief
as is at present given to members of the
fighting services and the Merchant Navy.
This section also provides that where the
same property passes on successive deaths
due to war operations, the estate duty
payable on that property at the later
death will be completely remitted, and
the property concerned will not be aggregated with any other property for the
purpose of ascertaining the rate of estate
duty payable.
Clause 5 exempts from death duties any
amount which may be due to a person
by way of a credit in the Post Office
Savings Bank under the recent income
tax proposals of the Chancellor of the
Exchequer, provided the death occurs
before the date on which the amount was
actually credited. This date, as hon.
Senators will recollect, is to be fixed as
soon as possible after the end of hostilities.
These proposals are in relief of taxation,
and I do not suppose the House will care
to interfere with them. I beg to move,
"That the Bill be now read a Second
time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committeed."-(Mr. Robb.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Tuesday next.
