Questions to Minister.
LETTERS AND PARCELS BY POST
AND TRAIN.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the Leader
of the House whether he is aware that
letters to the Midlands of England take
about four days, whereas parcels by
passenger train take only about 24 to 26
hours.
This question is based on letters and
parcels that I myself have sent by post
and passenger train to the Midlands of
England. It boils itself down to this.
If you post a letter in Belfast, say before
three o'clock in the afternoon, so as to
catch the English mail, it will be delivered
in Leamington, a town in Warwickshire,
on Thursday, Friday, or Saturday. An
uncensored letter may be delivered in
three days, while a censored letter will
take one day more. If, on the other
hand, you go to a railway station of the
London, Midland and Scottish Railway
this afternoon and send off a parcel by
passenger train, it will be delivered to
the same place, Leamington, to-morrow
evening. It seems very strange that that
should be so.
It seems impossible to find an answer
to that problem. I do not think it can be
due to the censoring of the letter, because
surely parcels via passenger train are
censored just as much as letters are,
otherwise it seems to me that it is quite
easy for an evilly disposed person to send
a document in some parcel by passenger
train, and it would get perfectly safely
to London or anywhere in England far
quicker than it would if sent by post.
Before the war, a letter posted to-day in
Belfast to catch the English mail would
be delivered in that same town of Leamington by the afternoon post to-morrow.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): My
right hon. Friend, the Minister of Commerce, has caused enquiries to be made
from the Post Office authorities in Belfast
and in London.
It is the case that, depending upon the
place and time of posting, some letters
from Northern Ireland may take four
days in transit to the English Midlands,
but the Post Office authorities state that
this is due to causes outside their control,
ROAD TRANSPORT BOARD AND
ECONOMY.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major Dobbs:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether it is considered necessary in
the public interest that the Northern
Ireland Road Transport Board should
waste petrol by sending a motor-car
round to collect accounts; and whether
the Government is taking any steps to
see that the business of the N.I.R.T.B.
is being carried out in an economical
manner.
Major DOBBS: If I may be allowed to
give the details of the transaction on
which this question is based I think it will
be of interest to the Members of this
House as showing efficiency and inefficiency. On Thursday, 25th September,
I ordered from the local Farmers' Cooperative Society five tons of manure.
The manager at once said, "Will you
make it six tons so as to make a full
load," and I said "All right." The next
day I got in triplicate forms to fill up and
sign for the Northern Ireland Road
Transport Board to take up that six tons
of manure from a warehouse in Belfast
and bring it out to me. I took the completed forms straight to the local agent of
the Transport Board, and hoped to get
that manure in the early part of the next
week. But it did not arrive, and on the
Wednesday I made inquiries and discovered
to my amazement that the Transport
Board were for some reason or other
unable to take up those six tons of manure
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
at the firm's warehouse and bring it
straight out to me.
They had to wait
for the Belfast
carriers or some body of that description
to collect it from the warehouse, take it to
the collecting station of the Transport
Board, dump it down there, and the
Transport Board would in due course load
it up again and bring it out to me. The
consequence was that instead of getting
it, say on the last day of September, or
the first or second day of October, I did
not get it until a week afterwards, and
instead of coming in one load it came in
two loads. That seems an extraordinary
state of things when we are at war and
trying to economise. If it had arrived
on the Wednesday after I had ordered it
I could have sown it straight away, but
I have still got it there and I have not
been able to sow it.
Then, to add insult to injury, without
sending me any bill for the carriage of
that manure, and without sending me any
invoice, on the 22nd a clerk from the
Transport Board came down to me in a
motor car with the Bill. I think he
might quite well have come on a bicycle.
I asked him what he had to do, and he
said he had to go to two other people in
the village near by, and then to two or
three others. But he had come a distance of about three and a half miles in
a motor car, and I think that was an
unnecessary use of petrol. Again, I do
strongly protest against the Road Transport Board trying to ride roughshod over
all the farmers of Northern Ireland in
this way.
Mr. ROBB: The hon. and gallant
Member divided his question into two
parts-injury and insult. As he did not
refer to injury in his question, I have no
information on that point. As regards
the insult, I am informed by the Road
Transport Board that, as a matter of
experience, the use of a car in the collection of accounts in country districts is a
necessity.
Collection by correspondence in many
cases does not produce results, and if the
Board were dependent on this method
they would have no alternative but to
confine acceptance of much of the traffic
to a "carriage-paid" basis, which would
cause grave inconvenience to many of the
public, particularly farmers.
The responsibility for seeing that the
business of the undertaking is carried out
in an economical manner is placed by the
Act of Parliament on the Board, and the
Government has no reason to believe that
the statutory obligations in this respect
are not being carried out.
Major DOBBS: May I ask if the right
hon. Gentleman will put the point about
injury to the Transport Board?
Mr. ROBB: Certainly.
Major DOBBS: With regard to the
insult, I think the business is at present
being carried on very much on a cash
basis, because as a rule they come round
and ask for cash on delivery. I have a
receipt for cash paid on delivery. The
Board's system is quite different to that
of a railway company or anyone else.
Mr. LESLIE: Accepting the sugges
tion that the Transport Board cannot rely
on a communication sent by post either
arriving or receiving attention, the question arises, what steps do they take to
ensure that the person they seek to visit
by car shall be at home when the car
I do not
arrives, complete with bill.
know whether the right hon. Gentleman
has any information on that point.
Mr. ROBB: None.
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me to be
rather important.
ROCK FALLS ON COAST ROAD.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Maguire:
:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government
are taking
steps to make the Coast Road safe from
Larne to Carnlough; whether he is
aware that an accident occurred on
Tuesday last, 21st instant, caused by
a fall of rock hitting a Transport bus
and injuring three persons; whether
he can state if responsibility for the
safety of this road lies with the Government or with the local authority; and,
if with the latter, will the Government
take the matter up strongly with the
local authority.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I put this question
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
28 OCTOBER 1941
down in order to draw the attention of
the Government to the very dangerous
state of the Coast Road, especially at
one point three-quarters of a mile from
Glenariff. This day week a serious
accident took place, a rock weighing
about seven tons falling on the back end
of a bus and injuring three persons.
There has been a good deal of trouble on
this Coast Road for many years owing
to overhanging rocks on the hillsides
giving way after heavy frost or rain, and
apparently no effort has been made to
prevent that taking place. I do not know
whether the Government or the Antrim
County Council is responsible.
During
the winter the road is very often blocked
to through traffic, and it is also very
dangerous for pedestrians. I travel
along that road four or five times a week,
and this day week I passed the point
about fifteen minutes before this great
mass of rock fell. I had a very narrow
escape. There might have been a vacancy
in this House. I think the Government
should get in touch with an expert who
might be able to suggest some means to
prevent these rocks falling into the road.
The projecting rocks especially might be
blasted or a system of drainage carried
out at the back of the rocks to prevent
them falling on to the roadway. Something at any rate should be done very
quickly, because at the present time great
inconvenience is being caused to traffic
and pedestrians. I am sure other Members of this House have suffered the inconvenience I have experienced, and I
do not want to take any unnecessary
risks during the winter.
Mr. ROBB: The duty of keeping the
roads safe for the travelling public is
primarily a matter for the Antrim County
Council. The hon. Senator will appreciate
that owing to the nature of the cliffs it is
impossible to guard against falls of rock,
etc., taking place, but a patrol is kept
daily, including Sundays, on this section
of road with the object of ascertaining, if
possible, whether there is any indication
of likely falls of rock or limestone, and,
if so, to arrange for warning the public.
In the event of a fall taking place, steps
are taken immediately to clear off the
obstruction. Fortunately so far, the
number of accidents due to fallen rock has
been few, and the hon. Senator can rest
assured that the Antrim County Council
is doing all it can to protect the public.
Mr. MCALLISTER: This question has
been approached so far from the point of
view of the inconvenience caused to the
public. I would put it on a higher plane.
This is a question of life and death. A
rock of seven tons falling on a bus might
have killed all the occupants of the vehicle
instead of only injuring several. If this
House or the Government has any responsibility at all it is a responsibility to save
the lives of the people. I am not satisfied with the answer given by the Leader
of the House. He has told us that the
Antrim County Council is primarily
responsible. I would like to know from
him what is the responsibility of the
Government. Surely the Government has
control over the Antrim County Council,
and where not only the inconvenience of
the public but the lives of the public are at
stake the Government should see that the
county council do their duty. I do not
think the Government has taken a
sufficiently serious view of this matter.
There must be some means of preventing
a recurrence of these falls of rock. I would
suggest that the Minister of Home Affairs
should send a competent engineering
expert to survey this area, see where the
defective rocks are, and report to the
Government some means of preventing
them falling into the roadway.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I can remember
the big storm of last year when there was
a fall of mountain which blocked this
particular road for some days. The
Government say there is a daily patrol of
this road, thus admitting the danger, but
they refuse to accept any responsibility
or to do anything. Surely the public are
entitled to some consideration. How can
the public have any confidence in the
Government when such a state of affairs
as this exists. Here is a road admitted
by the Government to be dangerous to
traffic and pedestrians, and yet the
Government will do nothing to render the
road safe. It is a question that concerns
the public of not only Antrim, but the Six
Counties and the whole of Ireland, because this is a tourist road. It is the
duty of the Government to see that this
danger is removed and that the road is
made safe so that people can travel over
it with confidence.
Mr. ROBB: The Government is satisfied
that the Antrim County Council is carrying on its duty. With regard to this
road, there is an inland road.

National Health Insurance and Contributory Pensions Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: The National Health
Insurance Contributory Pensions and
Workmen's Compensation Act, 1941,
passed recently by the Imperial Parliament has been circulated along with this
Bill and an explanatory memorandum,
to Members of this House, and I do not
propose, therefore, to do more than
merely refer to some of the main provisions of the Measure now before us.
The Workmen's Compensation provisions
are being dealt with in a separate Bill.
It will be observed that Section 11 of
the British Act provides for the extension
of its provisions to Northern Ireland on
the passing of the necessary Act by this
Parliament. This is the usual procedure
in the case of measures affecting the
national health and pensions schemes
since health insurance is established on a
United Kingdom basis and the pensions
scheme is closely interlocked with it.
A Committee has been appointed in
Great Britain under the chairmanship of
Sir William Beveridge to examine the
national insurance schemes in detail, so
as to enable the whole position to be
reviewed after the war. In the meantime amendments in the provisions of the
schemes are being restricted to the aspects
which are most pressing.
What is proposed at present is,
admittedly, far short of the demands of
those who feel that greater provision
should be made to assist those laid aside
by sickness, and it is hoped that after
the war a more comprehensive scheme
may be introduced with a view to placing
Health and Pensions Bill—
Second Reading
28 OCTOBER 1941
health insurance in a position comparable
with that of unemployment insurance.
The proposals in the Bill provide, in
the first place, for an increase of 3s a
week in the rates of sickness and disable-
To meet the cost of this
ment benefit.
it is necessary to increase the contribution by 2d a week-ld being payable by
the employer and 1d by the worker-and
to make certain other financial adjustments in the scheme which, when account
is taken of the increased State grant,
will enable the cost to be met.
a
In the second place, the scheme is
widened to admit non-manual workers
whose salary does not exceed £420
year, thus bringing the health and pensions schemes into line with the unemployment insurance scheme. Manual
workers are insured no matter what their
earnings may be.
In the third place it is proposed to take
power to amend the scheme by regulations so
as to meet war conditions as
they arise. It is expressly provided that
this power will not enable any alteration
to be made in the rates of benefits or
contributions. It will, however, enable
provision to be made for the case of the
ships' crews who have been captured by
the enemy and who would go out of insurance if the present law requiring them
to show that they were available for
work remained in force, and there might
be many similar provisions necessary as
time proceeds.
There are a number of other points,
of which the most important is probably
that which provides for the setting aside
of an additional 1s 6d a year to meet the
increased cost of medical benefit.
It is proposed that the new provisions
will come into operation on the 5th
January next, with the exception of those
enabling the scheme to be modified by
regulations.
These regulations may be
made as soon as the Bill becomes law, and
as they will be dealing with a number of
hard cases, I should like to be in a position to have them made as soon as possible, and I will ask the House to
agree
to
take the remaining stages on Tuesday
next.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
would like to say that I am very glad to
an
see this Bill introduced, because for one
thing it increases the amount of disablement benefit. The war has now been on
for two years, and in recognition of the
increased cost of living most classes of
workers have received increase in
wages, but those who have fallen into
ill-health and have to depend upon disablement benefit have up to now been
overlooked, possibly because there was no
organisation to look after their interests.
It was very hard that a man should have
disablement benefit of only 9s a week to
keep him, and the increase of 3s a week
which he will now receive under this Bill
is a very poor allowance. Indeed I am
sorry the Bill does not make some better
provision for this type of person who in
future will be compelled to live on only
12s a week. However, I am glad that
the Bill will effect some improvement in
the allowance granted to these people. I
have come in contact with several very
hard cases. One man, in particular, I
have had to recommend for poor-law relief
because it was impossible for him to live
on the small pittance allowed him in
respect of disablement benefit, and he was
in a state of starvation. Therefore I am
glad something is being done for these
people, even at this late hour.
Mr. LESLIE: After all, there are two
sides to this question. There are the
people to whom the hon. Senator has
just alluded, who are, no doubt, very
anxious to receive more money, but there
is the other side; the people who have to
pay it. The money is apparently being
provided by increased contributions of a
penny a week from the employees and
employers, with the assistance of the
State, and the assistance of the State is
likely to amount, in years to come, to a
not inconsiderable sum. I imagine that
all that can be said about that was said
before the Sir William Beveridge Committee, but it cannot but be remarked
that in the middle of the war, when everybody is urged to economise to the utmost
and to lend to the State any savings that
can be scraped up, the occasion should be
taken to embark upon a new scale of expenditure, and a scale which, when the
war is over, can scarcely fail to have some
effect on the power of the producer to
compete in the markets of the world.
However, our House of Commons has
passed the Bill, and I do not think we
shall object to it.

cheaper than paying for stamps. What
is to happen to the man drawing £400 a

then? Does he go out of insurance?

State has acknowledged that a man in
these circumstances is entitled by law to
assistance. The question that arises now
is whether 9s a week is sufficient. It is
not a question of who is to pay. That
is already settled. The workman is
called upon
to pay a share of his
insurance, and under this Bill he is called
upon to pay an equal share of the additional cost of this increase in benefit, so
that the speech of Senator Leslie was not
a fair interpretation of the remarks of
Senator Cunningham. The man who
draws unemployment assistance has not
to pay any insurance contributions at all
and he gets benefit as a matter of course,

against the Government, but as this is a

which the assistance is to reach him.

but if he has been a contributor for two

contributor and remain in the scheme.
As regards the doctors, they are to receive
1s 6d a head more, not only for these particular persons but for all panel patients.

Workmen's Compensation (Amendment)
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: In this very short Bill
there are two proposals. In the first
place, to raise the income limit for
"black-coated" workers, under the workmen's compensation scheme, to the same
figure as it is already for unemployment
insurance and as it is proposed to make
it (in the Bill which has just been considered) for national health and contributory pensions. This change, from £350
to £420 per year, has already been made
in Great Britain.
The second proposal-which, so far as
Northern Ireland is concerned, is of
minor practical importance-is to amend
the application of the Workmen's Compensation Acts to a person, such as a taxi
driver, who is not an employee in the full
sense but who plies for hire with a
vehicle, the use of which is obtained
under a contract of bailment from some
other
person or firm owning the vehicle.
The law says that in such circumstances the owner is to be regarded as
the employer and the taxi driver as a
worker entitled to compensation in the
event of an accident. But,
of the wording of the existing section,
the driver's title to compensation deon account
Chartered Bodies Bill
Adjournment
28 OCTOBER 1941
pends on ownership of the vehicle by the
person or firm concerned. If the firm
happened to be buying the taxi under
the hire-purchase system and had not yet.
actually become the legal owner the
driver would not be entitled to compensation. The Amendment is solely for the
purpose of remedying this technical
difficulty, and it follows an Amendment.
already carried out in Great Britain by
a separate Act passed there in 1938.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Chartered and Other Bodies (Temporary Provisions) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: On the outbreak of war a
Chartered and Other Bodies (Temporary
Provisions) Act was passed, but experience
has shown that it did not cover all the
bodies needing assistance during a time
of war. In order that they may transact
their business with greater convenience
this Bill empowers alterations to be made
in the constitution by the making of an
Order in Council on the request of a
particular body for a particular purpose.
Clause 2 provides for the removal of a
restriction which limited the principal
Act to corporations or bodies having jurisdiction in Northern Ireland only. The
effect of this removal will be that cases of
corporations or bodies whose activities are
not wholly confined to Northern Ireland
can be dealt with under the Act.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next."

adjourn until Tuesday next." (Sir
Joseph Davison.)

Question to Minister.
GOVERNMENT AND THE
RAILWAYS.
Mr.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of
McLaughlin :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether it is a fact that all the railways
in Great Britain are being guaranteed
or subsidised to the amount of
£40,000,000 per year; and whether, in
view of the fact that the Army and
Government have first claim on railways
in Northern Ireland just as in Great
Britain, and that the railways here give
the same war service, the Government
will make representations to the
Imperial Government to the end that
railways in Northern Ireland
treated on a parity with the railways in
Great Britain.
are
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I put this question merely to see that the railways in
the Six Counties get similar treatment
from the British Government to that
extended to the railways in Great
Britain. It is a well known fact that in
the Six Counties we have the Road and
Railway Transport Act, and that the
British Government give to the Government of Northern Ireland a sum of
£200.000 per year towards the cost of
their transport undertakings. Strange to
say, the railways get none of that money.
Under the Road and Railway Transport
Act there is no consideration whatever
for the railways. I would like to say
that to my mind the railways in the Six
Counties are giving as much service to
the British Government in its war effort
as
are any of the railways in Great
Britain. We all know that the Government have first call on the railways in
Northern Ireland, and rightly so, and
that the military authorities also have
first claim on the services of those railways, which are at the disposal of the
military at all times.
I appeal to the Government to see that
the stockholders of the railways in
Northern Ireland are treated in a manner
similar to the stockholders of railways in
Great Britain. We are all taxpayers
paying the same taxes as those in Great
Britain, doing the same kind of work,
and sharing the advantages and disadvantages, and all I ask is that the Northern
Government should make representations
to the Imperial Government so that the
railways in the Six Counties shall be
treated at least in a similar manner to
those in Great Britain.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): It
is not correct to say that the Government
of the United Kingdom is subsidising the
railways in Great Britain. That Government, on the first day of September,
1939, acting under powers granted to it
under the Emergency Powers (Defence)
Act, 1939, assumed control of practically
all the railways of Great Britain and of
the undertaking of the London Passenger
Transport Board. It placed them in the
control of the Minister of War Transport, who is responsible for the provision,
allotment and co-ordination of internal
transport services and who exercises his
authority over the undertakings mentioned through a railway executive committee. The property of the railways,
and of the London Passenger Transport
Board is, therefore, at the disposal of the
Minister of War Transport, and the
undertakings are carried on in accordance
with his directions.
Having taken the undertakings out of
the control of their owners, and not having
confiscated them, the Government entered
into an agreement with those owners by
which it guaranteed to them a minimum
annual revenue which is based on the
revenue of the undertakings during three
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
4 NOVEMBER 1941
particular pre-war years, and, at the same
time, it established the right to share in
any
over the guaranteed
minimum.
revenue
The railways of Northern Ireland are
still in the control and management of
their owners, and, while the Army may
have a first claim on their use, the right
to that claim was established not by Act
of Parliament, but by an arrangement
made on a commercial basis in respect of
traffic which is a lucrative source of
revenue to the railways.
As the Government of the United
Kingdom intends making much greater
use of the railways than during peace time,
the increased net railway revenue arising
out of the war is being shared between
the proprietors and the Government,
everything in excess of £43,000,000 going
to the Government. As the railways in
Northern Ireland are not under Government control all increases in their receipts
will accrue to the proprietors, and are not
shared with the Government.
The Government of Northern Ireland
has no power to assume control of the railways of the Province, and nothing has
arisen at any time during the past two
years which would indicate that such control is necessary. In the circumstances,
it is not the intention of the Government
to suggest to the Government of the
United Kingdom that such a step should
be taken, and, consequently, no question
of guaranteeing a minimum revenue can
arise.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: The Leader of

in England are under the control of the
Imperial Government. Surely he cannot
deny-

railways in Great Britain. I suggest
are the

Questions to Minister.
SUBSIDY FOR POTATOES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in
the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether, in view of the fact that the
majority of farms in Northern Ireland
are 10 statute acres and under, the
Government will make representations
to the Imperial authorities to extend
the subsidy or grant of £10 per acre to
growers of potatoes for 1941, to farmers
and plotholders who have grown not
less than one rood of potatoes for 1941.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In putting down
this question I should not have stated.
that the majority of farms in Northern
Ireland are 10 statute acres and under,
but that a large percentage of the farms
in Ulster are of that acreage.
I hope the
answer to this question will be a favourable one, because it must be admitted
that the small farmer who tills half an
acre or three roods, or even one rood for
potatoes is just as hard worked as the
man who ploughs five or six acres.
man who has a rood of potatoes or even
half an acre generally has to dig all that
ground himself, and I sincerely hope
that those who grow less than an acre of
potatoes will get the benefit of the subsidy.
There is a great deal of misunderstanding
around the country at the present time on
tihs point, and I hope the answer to my
question will clear the matter up.
The
While I am on this point, perhaps the
Leader of the House might be able, on
the Motion for the adjournment, to give
us an indication of whether the subsidy
will be continued in 1942. The reason I
ask for that information is that farmers
are now arranging for the 1942 crops.
Some of the land has got to be let, and
it is only right that owners who are going
to let ground for potatoes should know
whether there is going to be a subsidy of
£10 per acre in 1942.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
would need notice of the last part of the
question, as I have no information whatever.
Every person who has grown not less
than one rood of potatoes in Northern
Ireland in 1941 is, in fact, entitled to the
potato acreage payment provided he was
the occupier of one acre of agricultural
land on 1st June, 1941, when the agricul
tural census was taken. If, however, there
has been a change of ownership since 1st
June payment may in certain cases be
made to the new owner.
In the circumstances I do not think it
is necessary to make representations to
the Imperial authorities on the lines
suggested.
MOTOR VEHICLES ON CROSSINGS.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to ask the Leader
of the House whether, with a view to
preventing road accidents, any steps can
be taken to check the practice of motor
vehicles halting on crossings at the side
streets leading to York Street.
They
I think the question explains itself.
I do not know whether other Members
of this House have noticed, as I have,
that people proceeding from Castle
Junction to York Street Station—a thing
that sometimes happens when the proceedings of this House are not unduly
protracted-find the crossings at the side
streets occupied by motor-cars.
come up the side streets, and dash across
the crossing irrespective of whether people
are passing, or, if they cannot get into
the main street, they halt actually on
the crossing. That is something more
than technical obstruction of the pedestrian, and as the evenings get darker
there is a risk that the unfortunate
pedestrian, in moving out to the road,
is caught in the stream of traffic. As
it is this practice appears to me to be
growing, and I suggest it ought to be
checked.
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
11 NOVEMBER 1941
one.
Mr. ROBB: For several reasons the
volume of traffic in York Street and the
other streets in this neighbourhood has
greatly increased in recent years. This
whole area is a built-up one, and the
practice of requiring vehicles emerging
from side streets to stop at the entrances
to York Street is the only practicable
If the vehicle is not stopped it is
not possible for the driver to get a view
of the main street and the traffic thereon
before emerging. This procedure is recommended in the English Highway
Code, and if not followed traffic experts
are satisfied that there would be a great
increase in the number of accidents. No
suggestion has ever been received that
any accidents were caused by vehicles
taking this precaution, and the practice
should not lead to danger. Pedestrians
who may be temporarily obstructed have
two options:
(1) to wait till the obstructing
vehicle moves out on to the main
thoroughfare, or
(2) to pass behind the stationary
vehicle.
The first alternative is much safer, but
even the choice of the second should not
lead to accidents if care is exercised. An
examination of the returns dealing with
accidents in this area shows that during
the period 1st January, 1941, to 31st
October, 1941, the number of accidents
in York Street in which persons were
either killed or injured was 16, 4 persons
being killed and 12 injured. The reports
on these accidents show that in almost
every instance the vehicle involved
was proceeding along York Street
and was not either emerging from
or entering a side street. During
the same period one person was killed
and three persons were injured in the
streets leading to and from York Street,
but none of these accidents has any relation to the practice of requiring
vehicles to halt in the manner they
require to do at present.
Mr. LESLIE: We can only suggest
that perhaps it might be possible in the
streets where there is more of this crosstraffic to follow the practice at Castle
Junction and put studs down so as to
obtain reasonable space for the pedestrian.
At present a pedestrian may start to get
across a crossing, and before he gets across
a vehicle may be on top of him.
Mr. ROBB: I can only say that it has
not happened.

SUBSIDY FOR POTATOES.
The following question stood on the

McLaughlin:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the potato
subsidy of £10 an acre will be extended
to 1942.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I sincerely hope
we shall have a direct answer to this
question from the Minister. I have been
told on very good authority this morning
that it has been suggested that the
Government will not only give a subsidy
for potatoes for 1942, but will increase it
from £10 to £15 an acre. I very much
hope that that is true. It would be the
best day's work the Government ever did
if they granted a subsidy of £15 an acre
to farmers to manure their land, because
that is practically what it amounts to. If
you take into consideration the amount of
land under crop during the past year, a
great deal of it has been under wheat,
and if the farmer gets an inducement to
manure that land he will do so, and it
will mean there will be not only a crop
of potatoes next year, but a root crop in
the succeeding year. Therefore I hope
the Government will go step by step with
England and increase the subsidy to £15
an acre.
It is urgent that the farmers should get
an answer to this question as soon as possible, because some land owners let a good
deal of their land, and they want to know
where they stand as regards letting their
land. If they know this subsidy is being
paid next year it will give them an idea
of what the value of their land will be.
If the right hon. Gentleman cannot give
a definite answer to this question to-day
I hope the Government will make an
announcement on the subject at the
earliest possible date.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):

Ministries Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." The
Ministries Bill--
Second Reading
25 NOVEMBER 1941
way in which this Bill arose was this.
After the retirement of the late Minister
of Public Security and the appointment
of the new Minister, the Minister of
Home Affairs and the Minister of Public
Security, in view of the over-loading of
the Ministry of Public Security, came to
an arrangement whereby the police
functions of the Ministry of Public
Security would be transferred back to
the Ministry of Home Affairs. It is
to give effect to that arrangement that
this Bill has been brought in. The Bill
is not confined to this particular matter,
but it is so framed that if it should be
found necessary or advisable in the future
that functions should be transferred from
one Ministry to another Ministry, it can
be done by Order in Council without
bringing the matter back to Parliament.
That is the effect of this Bill.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time.'
""
Mr. LESLIE: I am rather sorry that
the course of events has necessitated
taking this Bill at such extreme
speed. There is nothing much in
the details of the
the Bill transferring
the police from the Ministry of Public
Security back to the Ministry of Home
Affairs. That does not come into the remarks I have to make. The objection I
have to the Bill-and it is scarcely fair
to say it is an objection to the Bill itself;
it is rather an objection to the necessity
which the Bill seems to recognise. It
seems to me that our method of distribution of functions as between one Department and another has got into rather a
state of confusion. Although we provided
very carefully in the Ministries Act of
1921 that the Minister of each Department might allocate the business of his
Department as he saw fit, we never seem
to have provided what was the appropriate Department for this and that.
The fact is, as you, my Lord, and two
other Members of the House besides myself may recall, the position when we
passed the 1921 Act was, we had got the
1920 Act, we had got a Lord Lieutenant
presiding over Ireland, but we had not
got a Governor of Northern Ireland, and
consequently, I think, we got by degrees
into rather a slip-shod and not the right
way of doing things. Somewhere about
1925 it was necessary to transfer matters
relating to the Sailors' and Soldiers'
Trust from one Department to another,
and that was done by Statute. A more
recent example is to be found in the
Road and Rail Transport Act of 1935.
Section 53 of that Act provided that
certain functions should be transferred
from the Ministry of Commerce to the
Ministry of Home Affairs. Parliament
did that, and I do not think anyone
called attention to it. Those instances
show that the practice has grown up,
rightly or wrongly, of making an
arrangement relating to the allocation
of business between different Ministries
by Act of Parliament, and that is what
I am inclined to think is not quite right.
It seems to me that the proper method
of arranging the distribution of work between Departments is for the Governor, as
representing the Crown, to do it. The
other way is an encroachment by Parliament on the executive authority of the
Crown, and that is a point I should like
to have looked into at a more convenient
time. The separation of the Colonial and
War Departments in 1855 is one instance,
and I think I am right in saying that when
it is necessary to change work it is done
by the Crown and not by Act of Parliament. Of course, Parliament has to be
consulted more or less indirectly later on,
because the money has to be provided,
but that is a different thing.
The present Bill affords an instance of
proceeding the other way. We have been
told that this Bill is urgent, and it is
obvious that time should not be lost; but
the same condition might arise again during
the present emergency, and I do not think
it is desirable that in any minor change
-because it is a minor change-reference
should have to be made to Parliament.
I think it is an encroachment by Parliament on the executive authority of the
Crown. I should rather have liked to have
had more time to have put down something which could have been answered,
after consideration, at a later stage.
As regards the particular matter which
the public have thought over, that the
object of this Bill is to enable some of the
functions of the Ministry of Public
Security to be re-transferred to the
Ministry of Home Affairs, I do not think
that is a matter for Parliament at all.
If the new Minister-whichever you
decide to describe as the new Minister—
does not do his work to the satisfaction of
Parliament, Parliament can interfere. As
Liabilities (War-Time) Bill 280
Ministries Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
regards that matter I do not think we
need trouble at all, and in view of the
urgency-which is the condition of various
branches of administration-it is certainly
obviously not desirable to interpose any
obstacle which would operate to prevent
those changes, which may be material or be
only Departmental trivialities, coming into
force as soon as possible. Therefore I
shall not oppose the Bill, but I think it
is a matter that should receive consideration at a more convenient time.
Mr. ROBB: I might say that that
matter was, perhaps, not considered but
touched upon, but the question dealt with
in the Bill was so urgent that, hampered
as we were by circumstances, the general
administration was not fully considered.
However, the point has not been lost
sight of and will receive consideration.

Liabilities (War-Time Adjustment) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." The
origin of this Bill is this. Under wartime conditions there are certain people
who have found themselves in financial
difficulties, and it was considered desirable
that special arrangements should be made
for such persons. Accordingly this Bill,
which is similar to one passed in England,
provides that such persons, who are
defined as persons who are in serious
financial difficulties owing to war circumstances, may make a scheme of arrangement outside the court in two different
ways. First of all, the measure provides
that the Lord Chief Justice may appoint
liabilities adjustment
officers, and the debtor may arrange for
a scheme to be arranged through the
liabilities adjustment officer for the suspension and perhaps ultimate discharge
of his liabilities without going to the
court, or, if he fails in that, he may go
to the court and get a scheme of arrangement sanctioned by the court, without
one
or
more
281 Liabilities (War-Time) Bill
25 NOVEMBER 1941

Expiring Laws Bill
that being called. an arrangement in
bankruptcy. That is really what it comes
to.
The first clause deals with the arrangement outside the court by the Liabilities
Adjustment Officer. It is not in essence
very different from an arrangement that
would be made in the bankruptcy court,
but it is not called that. It provides that
where the debtor and his creditors assent
to a scheme, after due notice has been
given in accordance with rules made
under the Act, the liabilities adjustment
officer may make a scheme. Any
creditor who has not assented to the
scheme may appeal to the court-which
all through this Bill is the High Courtagainst approval of the scheme, on the
ground that it contravenes the provisions
of this clause or that it is inequitable or
unreasonable, and the court may vary or
revoke the scheme, without prejudice to
the validity of anything done in the
meantime.
Where it appears that any scheme of
arrangement approved under this section
does not bind any creditor by reason of
the fact that the creditor was not given
the prescribed notice, that creditor, or
any creditor who is bound by the scheme,
or the liabilities adjustment officer, may
apply to the court to extend the scheme,
with such variations as may be necessary,
to the creditor not bound thereby or to
revoke the scheme and the court may extend or revoke the scheme accordingly.
The alternative method is that a scheme
similar in essence may be framed by the
court on the application of the debtor or,
if necessary, by a creditor. The effect of
such a scheme is practically the same as if
the man carried an arrangement in the
bankruptcy court, or was made bankrupt,
with one or two modifications. The court
may provide maintenance for the man and
his family, and may provide that any
place where he and his family live shall
continue to be occupied by them. It may
also provide for a reduction in the rent
he has to pay, with the limitation that it
shall not go below the relative value, or
it may provide for a reduction in the rate
of interest on the mortgage. A person
who is in difficulties owing to the war
may thus make an arrangement with his
creditors without becoming a bankrupt.
There are special conditions with regard
to partnerships where only one partner
may be in financial difficulties, and they
are to apply to private but not public
companies. That is the general scheme
of the Bill. It is naturally filled with
details, but those are rather matters for
lawyers.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Expiring Laws Continuance Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." It is
in the usual form. The three Bills that
are being continued until 31st December,
1942, are the Harbours, Docks and Piers
Act, 1920, which enables harbour authorities to vary their charges, the Local
Government (Emergency Powers) Act,
1921, and the Local Authorities and
Jurors Books (Temporary Provisions)
Act, 1940, providing for the suspensions
of Local Government elections during the
war.
In
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am going to
oppose this Bill for the reason that it is
taking out of the hands of the public
rights they are entitled to exercise.
the city of Armagh, where there is a
Commissioner, the people have been deprived of their rights to exercise the
franchise for the past eight years, and I
think it is time that state of affairs
ceased. I do not think it is right that
the Government should have the power
to keep local government electors in the
position where they are not able to elect
their own public representatives. When
the Commissioner for Armagh was
appointed I challenged the Government
to hold a public inquiry to decide
whether or not they were justified in
appointing a Commissioner. They never
did so, and the continuance of the Commissioner in office means an addition of
4d in the £ on the rates, while the public
are deprived of electing their own representatives.
Mr. LESLIE: About two years ago,
when a Bill was before this House in
similar shape, I drew attention to the
fact that this Local Government
(Emergency Powers) Bill was a Bill, or
Expiring Laws Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
rather an Act, which was brought into
force under very special circumstances in,
I think, 1921. Powers were given to the
Ministry of Home Affairs for one purpose
twenty years ago and have been kept on
the Statute Book ever since and used for
a purpose which was certainly not the
purpose for which they were granted. I
think, if I remember rightly, that on
the occasion of which I am speaking, two
or three years ago, I suggested an amendment to the effect that those powers
should cease to operate after 31st
December of the succeeding year, except
in regard to any place or any post or
office which might be filled at the time.
I did not suggest that the authority of
any Commissioner-and I am afraid the
Commissioner for Armagh
I had in mind-should be summarily
terminated.
was one
I proposed to leave that alone, but I
did desire Parliament to take steps to
prevent a similar set of circumstances
arising again, and, if it was necessary for
the Ministry of Home Affairs to be
clothed by Parliament with the summary
power of disregarding the electoral capacities of the ratepayers, special provision.
should be made. I am afraid there is no
doubt that leaving the Act on the Statute
Book as it stands now does afford the
Ministry the power of flouting the wishes
of the ratepayers. I do not say that the
Ministry has used that power wrongly, but
it is not right in a democratic country.
same.
The Parliament of the United Kingdom
decided so long ago as 1898 that the power
of electing representatives should be given
to local government electors. It is just
the same whether it is a rural district
council, the county council, or the Corporation of the City of Belfast-they are
all the
Parliament apparently
gave power to these ratepayers to
choose their own representatives, and
so long as the ratepayers exercise that
power it is not democratic that the
Ministry should deny them that right.
In the special circumstances under which
the Act of 1921 was passed you had a
state of affairs which has long passed
away.
I think that next year at all events
the Government ought to see that this Act
does disappear, and, if necessary, some
shorter Act should be brought in. In the
meantime, for the sake of the other two
Acts in the Schedule, it would not be
desirable to reject the Bill, but I think
enough has been said to impress once more
on the Government that there is a distinct
feeling, not only in this House but in the
country, that this Act should cease to
exist, and that if any special power is
necessary it should be incorporated in a
new Bill. The other two Acts ought
certainly to be continued, and the third
cannot be allowed to drop out on 25th
November before something has been
done to take its place before the ensuing
31st December.
Major BOYLE: I would like to support this Bill. Senator McLaughlin has
told us that Armagh has a Commissioner.
That is quite true, and I would like to
inform the House that when that Commissioner was appointed things in Armagh
were in a very bad state. I rather think

were 11s in the £. Since then they have
fallen to 9s, 7s, 6s 8d, 6s 6d, 6s 4d and
6s 1d in the £, which is a very considerable saving for the ratepayers. This is
in spite of the payment of the Commissioner's salary, which I suppose would be
equivalent to a rate of 3d or 4d in the £.
I think the ratepayers of Armagh are
perfectly satisfied with the present
arrangement, whereas before they were
not satisfied, and neither was the Government.

have never been a local government representative, but I do not believe any city or

Questions to Minister.
. CUSHENDALL ELECTRICITY
SUPPLY.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in
in the name of Mr.
Maguire :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the regulations which have
been made by the Ministry of Commerce under the Electric Supply Acts
(Northern Ireland), 1882-1923, for
(inter alia) the ensuring of a proper
and sufficient supply of electric energy
to consumers, apply to the undertaking known as "The Cushendall
Electric Light & Power Company"
which has its power producing plant
in the County of Antrim; whether he
is aware that on

Saturday, 22nd
November, 1941, the supply of electric
energy was almost completely cut off
thereby causing great inconvenience to
the general public in the district and
in certain cases serious financial loss;
whether, if these regulations do apply,
he will take steps to ensure a proper
and sufficient supply of electric energy
to the consumers in Cushendall; and
whether, in the event of the Cushendall
Electric Light & Power Company being
outside the scope of these regulations,
he will take immediate steps to bring
this undertaking within the scope of
the regulations.
was
no current
Mr. MAGUIRE: I have been asked
by the current consumers of Cushendall
to bring this matter before the House.
The generating plant in Cushendall is
owned privately, and on Saturday and
Sunday week there
available. It was cut off completely, and
consumers were greatly inconvenienced,
a bakery having to close down. There
are a good many military in the town,
and householders were taken completely
by surprise. They got no notice that
the light would be cut off on Saturday,
and on account of the great shortage of
oil lamps, candles, and other means of
lighting they were greatly handicapped
and inconvenienced. The
person who
owns this undertaking was approached,
and the reason given by him for the
failure of the current was that he was
short of oil for the engine that generates
the current. Whether that was due to
carelessness or not I do not know, but,
at any rate, he was entitled to give some.
notice to the consumers and not take
them by surprise on a Saturday afternoon in mid-winter.
The:
On Sunday afternoon he was able to
get the machinery going again, as, after
making inquiries, he was able to secure
oil in the town to start his engine to
generate the current. It seems a great
hardship that a private concern responsible for giving a public service can
take advantage of consumers in such a
sharp manner, and without giving any
notice. If the owner had made inquiries.
on Friday or even on Saturday about a
supply of oil he could have got it.
consumers feel they are being charged a
a very high rate for current. The lighting rate is 1s 1d per unit, which is exceptionally high, and he refuses to put in
any meters for a power supply; in fact,
the cinema in that town has got to pay
ls 1d for its power. There have been
some appeals made to reduce the rate,
and some of the current users feel that
the owner of this electricity concern is
taking a vindictive stand, in order to
curtail or smother criticism, by cutting
off the light for a night or two to let the
consumers see the inconvenience they will
be put to if he closed down the concern.
Some of the consumers have that feeling
about the matter.

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:

been working for forty years, and in all

has paid £8,000 a year towards the rates

to, and I do think the people there are
entitled to consideration.

Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I

Belfast nor Derry is going to help them.
I am advised that the Cushendall
Electric Light and Power Company is a
non-statutory undertaking, and that in
these circumstances the Regulations made
by the Ministry of Commerce for ensuring
a proper and sufficient supply of electrical
energy do not apply.
With regard to the dislocation in the
supply of electricity at Cushendall, which
took place on the 22nd November, 1941,
I am informed that the Ministry of Commerce cannot bring this undertaking
within the scope of the Regulations unless
the company applies for, and the Ministry
makes a Special Order constituting the
company as statutory undertakers within
the meaning of the Electricity (Supply)
Acts (Northern Ireland), 1882 to 1936.
Such an Order to become effective would
require the approval of Parliament.
cannot, of course, at this stage say
whether an application by the company
for statutory powers would be successful.

WAR DAMAGE CONTRIBUTION
FROM SCHOOLS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he is aware that demands are
being made for a war damage contri-.
bution from the Managers of Public
Elementary Schools, and whether he
can give any information on the
subject, particularly as to the fund
from which any supportable claim is
expected to be met.
Mr. LESLIE: Of course, as we are
all aware, there are different kinds of
public elementary schools in Northern
Ireland. There is one to which the question is particularly addressed, the
voluntary school, and there are other
schools under 2 and 4 Committees, and
other schools under regional education
authorities. It may be that the position
of those schools differs, but the point to
Liabilities (War-Time) Bill 296
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie
which I direct attention is the case of the
voluntary school which is not a profitmaking undertaking, which usually has
no income, and no invested funds, and
exists for a public purpose, which is the
supply of education without cost. If it
is really intended that the war damage
contribution should fall upon that school,
on what fund is it to be levied, and in
the event of the contribution not being
paid what is to be the position? The
position is rather difficult, and perhaps
discloses some little lapse in the Imperial
Act which imposes the contribution.
Therefore I put the question on the Paper
in order to give the right hon. Gentleman
an opportunity of clarifying the situation.
The MINISTER OF EDUCATION
(Mr. Robb): Under Part I of War
Damage Act, 1941, all property which
has been assessed for income-tax under
Schedule A or has appeared in a valuation
list for rating purposes is liable for a
war damage contribution. This would include the great majority, if not all, of
the public elementary schools. The assessment notices are issued by the local
inspector of taxes, and I am aware that
these demands are served on the managers
of the schools for the trustees or other
persons in whom the proprietary interest
in the property is vested.
In the case of provided or transferred
schools the payment in respect of war
damage contribution is met out of the
funds of the appropriate education or
regional education committee.
A few voluntary public elementary
schools are vested in the Ministry which
will assume liability for the payment of
the war damage contribution in respect of
these schools.
My Ministry has no knowledge of any
central fund out of which the war damage
contribution in the case of the remaining
voluntary public elementary schools can
be met.
One concession is made to these schools.
Provided or transferred schools or schools
vested in the Ministry are assessed at the
full rate of 2s in the £, while in the case
of other voluntary schools which may be
regarded as for charitable purposes, the
assessment is at the rate of one-third of
the full rate.
Mr. LESLIE : I am still in a little
doubt about the school which is not
assessed, which does not pay rates or
income-tax; in fact, it has not got any
assessment. My recollection is that schools
are not assessed.
Mr. ROBB: That is quite true. They
appear in the valuation list, but are not
assessed. As they appear in the list they
are liable for war damage contribution.
Mr. LESLIE: That follows if they
appear in the valuation list, but the
manager has no knowledge of the figure
at which they are assessed.

CLAUSE 24 (Application of Act).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: I should like to be
assured that this Clause is, so to speak,
competent for this Parliament. It seems
to me to be midway between Section 6 of
the Miscellaneous Provisions Act of 1932,
which has dealt with a rather special purpose and Clause 25 of the Imperial Act,
which is the opposite number of the Bill
now before us. Is the right hon. Gentleman
satisfied that this Clause is within the
competence of this Parliament?
Mr. ROBB: I am advised so. Clause
25 of the Imperial Act says:-
Notwithstanding anything in the
Government of Ireland Act, 1920, the
Parliament of Northern Ireland shall
have power to make laws, in relation to
persons ordinarily resident or carrying
on business in Northern Ireland, for
purposes similar to the purposes of this
Part of this Act.
Clause 24 confines it to persons ordinarily resident or ordinarily carrying on
business in Northern Ireland, and I am
advised it is competent for this Parliament so to legislate.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill,

(Short title, commencement and duration)
ordered to stand part of the Bill.

Business of the House.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Appropriation Bill, the
Excessive Rents (Prevention) Bill, the
Exported Animals (Compensation) Bill,
and the Air Raid Precautions (Postponement of Financial Investigation) Bill,
when they are brought from the House of
Commons."
As regards the Bills mentioned in this
Motion, three are not of a controversial
nature, and in the case of the Excessive
Rents (Prevention) Bill, it is desirable
that it should be completed before the
end of the year. Accordingly, I propose
to ask the House to take the Second
Reading to-day and to complete the Bill
on the next sitting day. With regard to
the next sitting of the House, the Government suggest that it should be on
Thursday, but I am entirely in the
hands of the House as to whether we
should next meet on Thursday or Tuesday.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Appropriation (No. 2) Bill, the Excessive Rents
(Prevention) Bill, the Exported Animals
(Compensation) Bill, and the Air Raid
Precautions (Postponement of Financial
Investigation) Bill, to which they desire.
the concurrence of this House.
Appropriation (No. 2) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First Time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: This Bill is in the usual
form, and I beg to move, "That the Bill
be now read a Second Time."
