Air Raid Precautions (Postponement of
Financial Investigation) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: Clause 9 of the Air Raid
Precautions Act says that:-
The Ministry shall, before the expiration of a period of three years,
from the passing of this Act, in consultation with such associations of
local authorities as appear to it to be
concerned, and with any local
authority with whom consultation
appears to it to be desirable, cause an
investigation to be made into the
working of the financial provisions of
this Act, with particular reference to
the expenses falling to be borne by
local rates, and shall cause a report of
the result of the investigation to be
laid before Parliament.
The brief and non-controversial measure
is designed to relieve the Ministry of
Public Security and the local authorities
which are scheme-making authorities
under the Air Raid Precautions Acts
(Northern Ireland), 1938, of the obligation to carry out at the present time an
investigation into the working of the
financial provisions of that Act.
The present time, with its many pressing problems and urgent need for action,
is hardly opportune for the holding of
an inquiry into the purely financial
aspect of civil defence, and the Ministry
now seek authority not to abandon the
investigation but to postpone it till a
more suitable date.
The financial relations between the
Government and the local authorities in
the matter of civil defence have not been
stabilised. The Act originally made provision for the payment of a grant on
approved air-raid precautions expenditure,
in accordance with the terms of the
Schedule, upon a percentage basis varying from 70 per cent. to 85 per cent.
Excessive Rents Bill-
Second Reading
16 DECEMBER 1941
From time to time, however, the
Minister of Public Security decided, with
the concurrence of the Minister of
Finance, to reimburse in full expenditure
upon certain items. I refer to such
matters as the erection of shelters, the
employment of whole-time paid personnel,
the demolition of buildings and the
clearance of debris, and the provision of
welfare arrangements in the period
immediately after a raid, amongst others.
The local authorities concerned were
consulted by the Ministry of Public
Security before the introduction of this
Bill, and their concurrence was secured
to the step which the Government is now
taking.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Exported Animals (Compensation) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
""
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, That
this Bill be. now read a Second time."
Under the Acts governing this matter the
compensation that is payable in the case
of animals slaughtered owing to disease
was fixed by assessors, but it has been
found that from time to time an animal
arrives in Liverpool or elsewhere, and is
there found to be affected by food-andmouth disease, and, of course, it cannot
there be inspected by people who are in
Northern Ireland. It has, therefore,
been found necessary to appoint agents
to carry out this inspection at the port
where the disease is discovered. This
Bill is designed to give power to appoint
agents to inspect carcases and assess
values which will be reported to trustees
on this side of the water. The Bill is
really necessary to carry out what was
the original intention of the Ministry of
Agriculture..
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Excessive Rents (Prevention) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: The object of this Bill is
to deal with the problem of profiteering
in rents by unscrupulous landlords at the
expense of persons who, on account of
their homes being rendered unfit as the
result of war damage, have been compelled to seek housing accommodation
elsewhere.
It has been found that existing legislation relating to rent restriction is inadequate in dealing with the problem. As
hon. Members are aware under the Rent
Restrictions Acts where a house, the poor
law valuation of which does not exceed
£75, is let for the first time the rent
obtainable becomes the "standard rent"
within the meaning of those Acts. Since
the recent air raids in Belfast a number
of cases have come to the knowledge of
the Ministry of Home Affairs where
houses have been let for the first time at
rents far in excess of what the landlord
would normally receive, and as these rents
are deemed to be the standard rent within
the meaning of the Rent Restrictions Acts
the tenant had no redress. Furthermore,
the type of accommodation which, in
many cases, has been provided, such as
barns, sheds and other buildings of
various kinds, is outside. the scope of the
existing Acts. The Measure now before
the House has been framed so as to apply
to all lettings of any kind whether furnished or unfurnished.
The Bill provides for the setting up of
a Tribunal, consisting of three members,
of whom one, the chairman, shall be a
resident magistrate, or a practising
barrister-at-law or solicitor of not less
than six years' standing.
The Tribunal will deal with cases where
persons, who have had to leave their
homes on account of damage done by air
raids, consider themselves aggrieved by
reason of the amount of rent they are
charged for other housing accommodation.
Where the Tribunal after investigating
the circumstances in any such case finds
that the rent payable by the tenant is not
fair and reasonable, the Tribunal will
fix a rent which is in its opinion fair and
reasonable, and will issue its certificate
accordingly. This rent will, thereupon,
be deemed to be the rent payable under
the tenancy agreement entered into between the landlord and the tenant, and
will for the purposes of the Rent Restrictions Acts be deemed to be the standard
rent. The Tribunal will not deal, however, with cases which come within the
Rent Restrictions Acts unless the letting
occurred after the 1st April, 1941.
Excessive Rents Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
There will be an appeal to the county
court against a decision of a Tribunal.
The Bill casts a duty on the landlord to
repay to the. tenant within one month
any sum paid in excess of the rent which
the Tribunal determines to be fair and
reasonable, but not exceeding the excess
amount
over a period of 12 months.
Should the landlord fail to repay the excess rent within one month from the date
of issue of the certificate of the Tribunal
he will be liable on summary conviction
to a fine not exceeding £100. Notwithstanding this penalty the tenant will be
at liberty to receive from the landlord
the excess rent within 12 months from
the date of payment and without prejudice to any other method of recovery
which the tenant may have, he may
deduct the excess rent from any future
rent payable, to the landlord.
The Measure will remain in force until
the 31st December, 1943. It is hoped
that those persons who feel themselves
aggrieved will not hesitate to bring their
cases before the Tribunal. Only in this
way can the objectionable practice of
profiteering in rents be put an end to. I
beg to move, "That the Bill be now read
a Second time."
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I am quite sure that
the sympathy of the whole House is with
the Bill, because no Member of this
House, I imagine, is going to stand up
in defence of what I might describe as
sharping, and it would appear from what
the right hon. Gentleman said-although
he did not give any instances that there
has been a certain amount of sharping
going on. I am quite sure the House will
desire to stop that and deal with that
which has already occurred to prevent
further cases arising in the future. At
the same time the Bill is, necessarily, I
think, a rather difficult Bill. In trying
to deal with one problem it presents
others, and the first problem that occurs
to me is, is the Government satisfied that
the introduction of this Measure will not
result in what I am calling the bombee,
the man who has been bombed out, being
adversely affected by putting an obstacle
in the way of that man getting another
house?
It has to be considered whether a man
having a house to let and having more
than one applicant for it will let it to a
bombee, who will be subject to this Bill,
or to the other man who will not be
subject to the Bill. Which of those
candidates is likely to be successful in his
endeavour to obtain the house. It has
also to be remembered on behalf of the
landlord that he may be in the position
of a trustee, and in that case he is bound
to get the best rent he can. That is not
going to help the bombee to get a house.
I hope, however, that whoever replies to
the debate will be able to tell us something about that.
That is the first point
I desire to make.
The second point is a smaller matter
which can easily be corrected at another
stage. The Bill does not provide where
the bombing is to take place. It does
not tell us where the enemy action takes
place. It seems to me that we are bound
to limit it to Northern Ireland because it
would be necessary for an applicant to
prove, first of all that he has been
bombed, and secondly that his house has
been rendered uninhabitable, and he cannot very well do that if his premises are
outside Northern Ireland. The problem
that will arise is where the damage is
done, although the general problem will
be, not where the damage is done but in
the place where the man wants to get
another house. It will be a simple matter
to insert "Northern Ireland" there at a
later stage, and I think that will have to
be done. Circumstances have changed
since this Bill made its appearance in
another place, and although it may be
possible to ascertain whether a house has
been rendered uninhabitable in Northern
Ireland it will not be practicable to do
San
so if the house is in Glasgow or
Francisco or anywhere else you can
think of.
Passing to the other question, the reclaiming back of money overpaid. I
think there is no doubt that there is a
certain amount of disquietude as regards
the reopening of contracts which however
ethically unjustifiable they may be, were
at all events within the law. The public
conscience, although it does not like
sharping, still more dislikes the reopening of a contract, and that should
be avoided as far as possible. At the
same time we know that courts of equity
have no particular compunction in up-
Excessive Rents Bill-
Second Reading
16 DECEMBER 1941
setting a contract which they regard as
harsh and unconscionable, and it is very
difficult for someone to presume to set a
bound to the exercise of that power.
We
know it is exercised frequently in cases
where expectant heirs or moneylenders or
frequently both in conjunction are concerned, and we know that a court of
equity requires those who seek equity to
do equity.
They also refuse definitely to allow the
jurisdiction of the courts to be used in
the enforcement of an unconscionable
bargain, because the view taken is that
it is a bargain that a reasonable man
would not consent to, nor would a fairminded man ask him to do so, and the
existence of such a bargain is a kind of
constructive fraud and SO militates
against the public interest. Accordingly
a court of equity will in certain cases
give relief, and very few people will
venture to suggest bounds beyond which
a court of equity will not go.
It follows
then, that any attempt to disturb existing contracts is a matter of some sort
of equitable jurisdiction, and that being
so it becomes a question of what court
you will commit the task of revising contracts already made, and providing for
the prevention of an unjustifiable contract in the future.
I said just now that the words "harsh
and unconscionable" are used in the
Moneylenders Acts, and I should have
liked to get them into this Bill. I shall
be disposed to try, although I do not
think that I shall succeed. But the point
does remain that it is a harsh and unconscionable bargain that this Bill is intended
to get at, and that will have all our good
wishes provided we can do it in a satisfactory way. If you are to start something in the nature of equitable jurisdiction, it becomes a matter of the first
importance to what court you are to entrust that jurisdiction. Your court must
obviously, in the nature of things, be one
that will act quickly and be readily
accessible and will command, to some
extent, public confidence.
in
The first proposal in the Bill was that
there should be only one tribunal. That
was given up, and indeed the Government, by accepting amendments
another place, have gone half the way I
wanted them to go, but I would like them
to go a little further. There was also no
proposal for an appeal. Subsequently
the Government came to the conclusion-
I presume on grounds of accessibilitythat they would have more than one
tribunal, and left the number indefinite.
I think they were right in that, because
it seems that you will require a tribunal
in every seaside town in Northern Ireland. The problem will not be confined
to those two particular towns where
bombing has occurred, because the
bombees are not seeking houses in those
particular areas. Therefore, you will
want a set of tribunals operating
everywhere.
It is obvious that you cannot set up
fifty tribunals. It will be difficult to
start two or three. The first member of
a tribunal is to be a resident magistrate,
or barrister, or solicitor, who is to be the
chairman, and various proposals were
made as regards the other members of the
tribunal. I am not sure that the debate
did not descend to actual personalities,
but if you multiply your tribunals you
will have great difficulty in finding
satisfactory men to serve on them.
will be almost impracticable to set up the
number of tribunals that will be required,
and to staff them. Moreover, if you proceed on those lines the cost will be very
great. You have already agreed to have
more than one tribunal, and if you have
more than one you will have trouble in
providing the necessary jurisdiction.
It
The answer is not to set up any new
tribunals at all, but simply to use the
tribunal you have got already to hand,
operating everywhere, the summary
jurisdiction court, consisting of one resident magistrate sitting alone or with other
resident magistrates, with an appeal to the
county court judge. I shall suggest that
at a later stage. It will give you a better
tribunal-you will have to call it a court
-than you get under the present Bill.
You can avoid certain difficulties. There
is no direct power given in the Bill for
these tribunals to inspect premises, and
no power to pay the expenses of witnesses.
The tribunal will not have the inherent
powers of a court, and I do not think
that will be satisfactory.
But the other method will be satisfactory, and I cannot see that it will do any
injustice to anybody. I think it will be
a thoroughly fair tribunal. Your resident magistrate, sitting alone, will have
some knowledge of his district, and he
will know whom to have as witnesses,
Excessive Rents Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
whereas if you have two
or three
tribunals scattered about the country,
however you arrange them, will not be
satisfactory. On the other hand, under
a resident magistrate you will have a
ready-made court in every petty sessions
district in Northern Ireland. I think
you will find that very satisfactory.
With those two Amendments, the insertion of Northern Ireland, which I am
afraid will be necessary at a later stage,
and the alteration of your court, I think
the Bill will meet with such success as it
is possible for such a Bill to meet with.
But, above all, we must be satisfied, if
we can be and the Government can help
us-that passing this Bill will not prevent
the unfortunate bombee getting a house.
I am prepared to support the. Second
Reading in the hope that the Bill will
be brought into more reasonable line as a
result of future debate.
Mr. McALLISTER: I would like to
say that I quite appreciate that, in
principle, this is a Bill which should
commend itself to the House as a necessary Measure.
I was interested in the
very constructive criticism of Senator
Leslie, but at the moment I cannot accept
all the arguments he has put before the
House. I appreciate very much the
research and labour he seems to have
undertaken in order to enlighten the
House on many points which had not
occurred to some of us. As regards those
points I would like to suspend my judgment until the Committee stage.
With regard to the last criticism of the
hon. and learned Member, he will
perhaps remember that when he and I
were opposing the Summary Jurisdiction
Bill, one of the strongest arguments that
appealed to me was the fact that you
would have a fairly constituted tribunal
of three individuals. We all know that
one man may hold strong views on one
particular thing, and I think the collective views of three individuals will be
more likely to arrive at a satisfactory
finding than if your finding is based on
the opinion of one individual.
The hon. and learned Senator has said
that it will be difficult to find satisfactory
members of these tribunals outside the
resident magistrates. I think the Bar
and the solicitors' professions in Northern Ireland can supply many men of the
With
highest probity, character, and ability to
fill those positions, and they would contribute very much to the satisfactory
administration of this Measure.
regard to the two other points raised by
the hon. and learned Senator, I intend
to suspend judgment on them and to consider them when they arise in Committee.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
also would like to support the Second
Reading of this Bill, because I believe, it
will have a very good effect, and will put
an end to the greedy operations of owners
of barns and outbuildings. I understand that in some places as many
as seventeen members of several families
have been herded into one barn for which
an exorbitant rent has been charged,
although there was no water supply or
other conveniences. With regard to the
preference expressed by Senator Leslie
for a court of summary jurisdiction
instead of a tribunal, I feel that the
working-class people, who are the people
likely to be affected more than any
others, would be less nervous about going
before a tribunal of laymen than they
would be going before a court of resident
magistrates. They would be more content
and would feel more comfortable, and
would be better prepared to put their
case before a tribunal such as is proposed
in the Bill than they would before a
court.
The ordinary workman would rather
suffer anything than go to the court to
have a grievance remedied. He prefers
to keep clear of the court and the police.
I believe the workers would have much
more confidence in a tribunal consisting
of a barrister or solicitor as chairman
and two members representing the tenants
and landlords or estate agents, and therefore, I think, it would be better to confine it to such a tribunal rather than to
the ordinary court.
Major BOYLE: I wish to support the
Bill in every way possible. It seems to
me an outrageous thing that people
should take advantage of the plight of
others who have suffered at the hands of
the enemy.
At the same time I think
there are difficulties in the Bill, and one
of the greatest will be fixing the rent of
furnished houses or furnished apartments
or furnished rooms. In the part of the
country I come from no one can possibly
get an empty house. There is not such
Excessive Rents Bill
Expiring Laws Bill
16 DECEMBER 1941
a house to be had for love or money, but
there are people who have been bombed
out who have taken furnished rooms. I
understand that in many cases the
furniture is very meagre and the rent is
very high. I have heard of cases where
people who were paying rents of from 2s
to 10s a week for a house have charged
£1 for one room. There will be great
difficulty in fixing the rents of such
places, and I hope that in framing the
regulations under this Bill people who
have been charged such rents will be
adequately protected. I have great
pleasure in supporting the Bill in every
way.
I
Mr. ROBB: I am very glad that this
Bill has had such a favourable reception,
although it is true that certain criticisms
have been made. The first criticism made
by Senator Leslie was that by the passing
of this measure a bombee might be
obstructed in getting another house.
am afraid that is a risk we will have to
take. I do not see how we can avoid
that. If there are two competitors for a
house, and one has rights under this Bill
that the other has not got, the sharper
will take the one who has no rights. I
do not see how we can avoid that.
A regards the suggestion that the Bill
should be restricted to persons bombed
out of their houses in Northern Ireland,
we do not want to do that, because there
may be people who have come to live with
relatives over here after being bombed out
of their homes in Glasgow or Liverpool,
and we do not want them to be charged
excessive rents any more than anyone else.
The practical difficulty is, how are these
people going to prove that the place they
have left is unfit for habitation. They
are going to be the plaintiffs in an action,
and it is part of their obligation to prove
their case. They will have to do that if
they are to have the protection of this
Bill.
With regard to the constitution of the
court and as to whether an unsworn body
should be in charge of equitable jurisdiction, if that were pressed it would be
quite easy to swear the members of the
tribunal. On the whole, I think it is the
general opinion of all those persons with
whom I have talked on this matter-and
it is certainly the opinion of the Government that a tribunal more or less in the
nature of an arbitration tribunal, rather
than a single resident magistrate or other
official, would carry greater confidence
amongst the people who have got to
appear before the tribunal. Whether
that is so or not experience will tell, but
prima facie, in a case like this, where
what is really an arbitration tribunal is
going to decide what in the circumstances
is a fair and reasonable rent, the opinion
of a majority of three persons is better
than the opinion of one individual, and
those concerned will have greater confidence in such a tribunal.
With regard to the difficulty of staffing
tribunals, I do not think that will arise.
In the first place, it is not probable that
there will be a great many cases, as far
as one can see. I am quite satisfied that
the announcement of the introduction of
this Bill will act more, as a deterrent than
as a remedy. It will deter people from
charging excessive rents rather than
encourage them to reopen a bargain they
have already made.. The hon. and
learned Member made one point that the
tribunal has been given no power to pay
the expenses of witnesses. Whether that
is a correct view I have not had time to
satisfy myself, and I am not quite sure
whether it is desirable that the tribunal
should have this power, but I will consider the point. The Government hopes
the Bill will carry out what is intended,
namely, to prevent these harsh bargains
being imposed on people who have been
bombed out of their homes.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

fix the sittings of the House without first

PROROGATION OF PARLIAMENT.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command
from His Grace the Governor to read
forthwith in this Chamber to the Senators
and Commons of Northern Ireland therein
assembled His Grace's Proclamation proroguing this present Session of Parliament.
Resolved: "That the Gentleman
Usher of the Black Rod be
manded to inform the Commons that the
Proclamation proroguing the present
Session of Parliament is to be read
com-
Prorogation

Prorogation
27 JANUARY 1942
forthwith, and inviting the Commons to
attend in order to hear the same."-(Mr.
Robb.)
The Commons, being at the Bar, with
their Speaker, the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
His Grace's command), as followeth :
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR OF
NORTHERN IRELAND.
A PROCLAMATION.
--
For Proroguing the present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
certain enactments amending the said Act,
that the Governor of Northern Ireland
shall, in His Majesty's name, summon,
prorogue and dissolve the Parliament of
Northern Ireland :
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the
third day of February, one thousand
nine hundred and forty-two:
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
DUKE OF ABERCORN, KNIGHT OF
THE MOST NOBLE ORDER OF THE
GARTER, KNIGHT OF THE MOST
ILLUSTRIOUS ORDER OF SAINT
PATRICK, Governor of Northern Ireland, by this Proclamation do publish
and declare His Majesty's Pleasure that
the said Parliament be, as from the
reading of this Proclamation in the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday, the third day of
February, one thousand nine hunderd and
forty-two.
And I do hereby further declare His
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to be
that the said Parliament shall on the said
third day of February, one thousand nine
hundred and forty-two, assemble and be
holden at Stormont for the despatch of
divers urgent and important affairs, and
the Senators and Members of the House
of Commons of Northern Ireland are
hereby required to give their attendance
accordingly.
Given this twenty-seventh day of
January, one thousand nine hundred and
forty-two.
(Signed by his own hand),
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
End of the Fourth Session (opened

Tuesday, 25th February, 1941) of the
Fifth Parliament of Northern Ireland in
the Sixth year of the Reign of His Majesty
King George the Sixth.
F
INDEX.
FIFTH PARLIAMENT.
VOLUME OF FOURTH
SESSION, 1941-42
25th FEBRUARY, 1941 to 27th JANUARY, 1942.
